<t0>Colonel Aureliano Buendia, when he stood before the firing squad years later, would remember that distant afternoon when his father took him to discover ice.</t0> <t1>At that time, Macondo was a village of twenty mud houses, built on the bank of a river of clear water that ran along a bed of polished stones, which were white and enormous, like prehistoric eggs.</t1> <t2>The world was so recent that many things lacked names, and in order to indicate them it was necessary to point with one's finger.</t2> <t3>Every year in March, a ragged band of gypsies would set up their tents outside the village, and with a great uproar of pipes and drums, they would display new inventions.</t3> <t4>First, they brought the magnet.</t4> <t5>A hulking gypsy with an untamed beard and sparrow hands, who introduced himself as Melquiades, put on a captivating demonstration with what he proclaimed to be the eighth wonder of the learned alchemists of Macedonia.</t5>
<t6>As he dragged two ingots of metal from door to door, people saw pots and pans, tongs and braziers clattering and rolling from their places, nails and screws desperately trying to jump out of their sockets, causing the beams to creak, and especially the long-lost objects, which had been searched for extensively, tumbling out from their hiding places and rushing pell-mell after Melquiades' magical irons, leaving everyone dumbfounded.</t6> <t7>The gypsy, in his coarse accent, proclaimed, "Objects have a life of their own; all it takes is to awaken their souls."</t7> <t8>Jose Arcadio Buendia, whose unbridled imagination extended beyond the wonders of nature, the most improbable miracles, and even magic, thought that this useless invention could be used to extract gold from the earth's core.</t8> <t9>Melquiades, being an honest man, warned him, "It is useless for that."</t9>
<t10>Since Jose Arcadio Buendia did not trust the honesty of the gypsies at that time, he traded his mule and a pair of goats for two magnetic ingots.</t10> <t11>His wife, Ursula Iguaran, who relied on these animals to increase the few pieces of broken-down furniture and goods in the house, tried to dissuade him to no avail.</t11> <t12>Her husband said nothing but, "Very soon we will have enough gold to cover the floor of the house and then some," and to prove the correctness of his idea, he toiled for months.</t12> <t13>He scoured the entire region, dragging the two iron ingots behind him and shouting Melquiades' magical words, until he reached the riverbed.</t13> <t14>In the end, all he found was a fifteenth-century suit of armor, fused together by rust and making a hollow sound like a giant pumpkin filled with stones.</t14> <t15>When Jose Arcadio Buendia and his four-man exploration team managed to dismantle the armor, a calcified skeleton with a copper medallion around its neck containing a lock of woman's hair emerged from inside.</t15>
<t16>The gypsies came again.</t16> <t17>This time, they brought a magnifying glass the size of a drum, which they claimed was the latest invention of the Jews from Amsterdam.</t17> <t18>They placed a gypsy woman at the other end of the village and set up the telescope at the entrance of their tent.</t18> <t19>Anyone who paid five reals could look through the telescope and see the gypsy woman as if she were just a yard away.</t19> <t20>Melquiades declared, Science has eliminated distance.</t20> <t21>Very soon, mankind will be able to see what is happening anywhere in the world without stepping out of their homes.</t21> <t22>The scorching midday heat showcased the astonishing demonstration of the giant magnifying glass: They piled up dry grass in the middle of the street and focused the sun's rays through the magnifying glass to set it on fire.</t22> <t23>Still unable to accept the failure of his magnets, Jose Arcadio Buendia decided to use this invention as a weapon of war.</t23> <t24>Although Melquiades tried to dissuade him again, he eventually accepted two magnetized ingots and three gold coins minted for the colonies in exchange for the magnifying glass.</t24>
<t25>Ursula cried and lamented.</t25> <t26>The money had been taken from the chest full of gold that her father had saved all his life without eating or drinking, and that Ursula had buried under her bed, saying "save for a rainy day."</t26> <t27>Jose Arcadio Buendia, a scientist so engrossed in his 'tactical' experiments that he would even risk his life, did not attempt to console his wife.</t27> <t28>While trying to demonstrate the effect of the magnifying glass on enemy troops, he got so severely burned by the sun focused through the magnifying glass that his entire body was covered with unhealing sores.</t28> <t29>Despite all the objections of his wife, who was terrified of such a dangerous invention, he even tried to set his own house on fire to see if it would catch.</t29>
<t30>Locking himself in his room for hours, calculating the possibilities of his new weapon, he finally produced a handbook whose instructive clarity was beyond question and whose persuasiveness was irresistible.</t30> <t31>He added a series of descriptions of the experiments he had conducted and a few pages of explanatory illustrations to the book, and sent it to the government with a courier.</t31> <t32>The courier, who crossed mountains, lost his way in endless swamps, battled raging rivers, and narrowly escaped death from despair, troubles, and wild animals, finally found the path that intersected with the road taken by the mules carrying the mail.</t32> <t33>Despite the impossibility of traveling to the capital at that time, Jose Arcadio Buendia promised to go to the capital as soon as the government ordered, to present a practical demonstration of his invention to the military authorities, and to teach those intricate war methods that operated with the sun.</t33> <t34>He waited for years for a response.</t34> <t35>Finally, tired of waiting, when he complained to Melquiades about the failure of his plan, the gypsy proved his honesty by giving him some Portuguese maps and tools for drawing expedition routes, in addition to the coins he had given in exchange for the magnifying glass.</t35>
<t36>After summarizing and writing by hand Keşiş Hermann's studies on how to use the astrolabe, compass, and sextant, he left them for Jose Arcadio.</t36> <t37>Jose Arcadio Buendia locked himself in a small room he built at the back of the house so that no one would disturb his experiments during the long rainy season that lasted for months.</t37> <t38>He completely abandoned his household responsibilities and spent nights observing the orbits of the stars in the garden, almost getting sunstroke in his quest to find an infallible method to determine noon.</t38> <t39>Once he mastered using and operating his instruments, he developed a new concept of space that allowed him to explore unknown seas and uncharted lands and interact with marvelous beings without leaving his desk.</t39> <t40>It was during this period that, while Ursula and the children exhausted themselves in the garden growing bananas, caladium, cassava, sweet potatoes, pumpkin roots, and eggplants, Jose Arcadio developed the habit of pacing around the house and talking to himself without looking at anyone.</t40>
<t41>Then this feverish activity suddenly ceased, giving way to a sort of enchantment.</t41> <t42>For a few days, as if bewitched, he kept repeating a series of assumptions one after another, not even sure if he understood them himself.</t42> <t43>Finally, on a Tuesday afternoon in December, he revealed the worm that had been eating him up inside.</t43> <t44>The dignified solemnity of their father, who was worn out from the wrath of his imagination and weeks of sleeplessness, as he explained his discovery, never left the children's minds:</t44>
<t45>-The world is round, just like an orange.</t45>
<t46>Ursula's patience ran out.</t46> <t47>If you intend to go mad, do it on your own!</t47> <t48>But don't try to fill the children's minds with those gypsy ideas! she shouted.</t48> <t49>Jose Arcadio Buendia, who did not bat an eye, was not frightened by his wife's unbalanced excessiveness, who, in her rage, smashed the astrolabe to the ground.</t49> <t50>He made a new astrolabe and gathered all the men of the village in his room, proving with theories that none of them could grasp that a boat constantly steering could return to the point where it first set out to sea.</t50> <t51>Just when the whole village was convinced that Jose Arcadio Buendia had lost his mind, Melquiades arrived and saved the situation.</t51> <t52>Although it was not known in Macondo at the time, Melquiades praised Jose Arcadio's superior intellect, who had independently developed a theory that had already been proven in practice by merely contemplating astronomy, in front of everyone and, as a token of his admiration, gave him a gift that would have a profound impact on the village's future: an alchemist's laboratory.</t52>
<t53>Meanwhile, Meiquiades had aged at an incredible speed.</t53> <t54>During his first visits, he appeared to be the same age as Jose Arcadio Buendia.</t54> <t55>However, while Jose Arcadio maintained his strength to bring down a horse by grabbing its ears, the gypsy seemed to have been ravaged by a relentless ailment.</t55> <t56>In fact, this decline was the result of a rare assortment of diseases he had contracted during his countless journeys around the world.</t56> <t57>As he explained to Jose Arcadio Buendia while helping him set up the laboratory, death followed him everywhere, sniffing at the cuffs of his pants, but never quite deciding to strike the final blow.</t57>
<t58>The Gypsy had escaped from all the ailments and all the troubles that had devastated people.</t58> <t59>He had survived pellagra in Iran, scurvy in the Malay Archipelago, leprosy in Alexandria, beriberi in Japan, plague in Madagascar, an earthquake in Sicily, and a terrible shipwreck in the Strait of Magellan.</t59> <t60>This hulking creature, said to have deciphered Nostradamus's codes, was a gloomy man with an Asian appearance that suggested he knew the inner workings of everything, and a melancholic air.</t60> <t61>He wore a huge black hat like a raven with its wings spread, and a velvet vest where centuries-old mold ran rampant.</t61> <t62>But despite his boundless wisdom and mysterious depth, he had human worries and the troubles of the world that kept him busy with the trivialities of daily life.</t62> <t63>He complained of the ailments of old age, struggled with the slightest economic difficulties, and had long since stopped smiling because his teeth had fallen out due to scurvy.</t63> <t64>In that suffocating midday heat, when the Gypsy revealed his secrets, Jose Arcadio Buendia was absolutely convinced that this was the beginning of a deep friendship.</t64>
<t65>The children were left in awe by the incredible tales told by the Gypsy.</t65> <t66>Aureliano, who was only about five years old at the time, would always remember him as he first saw him, standing with his back to the gray, flickering light coming through the window, speaking in a sonorous voice that illuminated the darkest corners of the imagination, while sweat from the heat trickled down his temples.</t66> <t67>His brother Jose Arcadio would also pass down that unique image to all his descendants.</t67> <t68>However, for Ursula, who entered the room just as Melquiades dropped and broke the bottle of mercuric chloride, the memory of that first day was not at all pleasant.</t68>
<t69>Ursula said, "This is the smell of the Devil."</t69>
<t70>Melquiades, Not at all, he retorted.</t70> <t71>It has long been proven that sulfur characteristics exist in the devil, but this is just a bit of sublimation, that's all.</t71>
<t72>Then, with the demeanor of a teacher, he began to explain the diabolical characteristics of the cinnabar one by one.</t72> <t73>Ursula, however, without paying any attention, took the children to pray.</t73> <t74>From that day on, whenever Melquiades was mentioned, that sharp smell always came to Ursula's mind.</t74>
<t75>The makeshift laboratory consisted of a primitive water pipe with an array of bowls, funnels, alembics, filters, and sieves added to it, a long-necked glass carafe, a portable copy of the alchemist's stone, and a model of a three-armed Jewish Mary alembic made by the gypsies according to contemporary definitions.</t75> <t76>In addition to these, Melquiades left a series of notes and drawings explaining the processes of the Great Teaching, which would show the seven pieces of metal symbolizing the seven planets, the formulas of Moses and Zosimos for doubling gold, and the ways to make the alchemist's stone.</t76> <t77>Convinced by the simplicity of the formulas for doubling gold, Jose Arcadio Buendia did not leave Ursula alone for weeks, so that she would extract the colonial gold and he could multiply the gold as if he were dividing and multiplying mercury.</t77> <t78>As always, Ursula gave in to her husband's stubbornness.</t78>
<t79>Jose Arcadio Buendia put three colonial gold pieces into a pan, mixed them with copper shavings, yellow arsenic, sulfur, and lead, then threw them into a pot full of castor oil and boiled them until they turned into a thick and sticky paste that resembled wax more than gold.</t79> <t80>After being melted with metals symbolizing the seven planets through dangerous and desperate distillation processes, Ursula's precious paternal inheritance was mixed with alchemical mercury and Cyprus vitriol, and when cooked with lard in the absence of turnip oil, a large piece of burnt lard stuck to the bottom of the pot remained.</t80>
<t81>When the Gypsies returned, Ursula had worked on the entire village and turned them against them.</t81> <t82>But this time, when the Gypsies announced with a cacophony of every imaginable instrument and a town crier that they would be showcasing Naciancenes' legendary invention, curiosity overcame fear.</t82> <t83>Everyone crowded into the tent, paid a coin, and began to watch Melquiades, who had pulled himself together, with a wrinkle-free face, teeth shining like pearls, fresh as ever.</t83> <t84>Those who remembered his scurvy-ridden gums, sagging cheeks, and shriveled lips shuddered with fear at this latest proof of the Gypsy's supernatural power.</t84>
<t85>When Melquiades, after briefly removing his complete set of teeth along with his gums and showing them to those present, and then in that brief moment becoming the familiar trembling old man again, put his teeth back in place and smiled with the full vigor of his revived youth, fear turned into panic.</t85><t86>Even Jose Arcadio Buendia believed that Melquiades' wisdom had reached unimaginable levels, but when the gypsy explained the true nature of the dentures to him in private, he became overexcited.</t86><t87>This matter seemed both so simple and so extraordinary that Jose Arcadio Buendia instantly lost interest in his alchemy experiments.</t87><t88>He was again overwhelmed by a sense of suffocation.</t88><t89>He wasn't eating properly and was wandering around the house incessantly.</t89><t90>There are unimaginable things happening in this world, he said to Ursula.</t90><t91>While all sorts of magical tools and equipment are just across the river, here we are, wallowing in our ignorance.</t91><t92>Those who had known him since the days Macondo was founded were astonished at how much he had changed under Melquiades' influence.</t92>
<t93>In the past, Jose Arcadio Buendia was like a late tribal chief who taught how to plant crops, how to plant trees, how to raise children and animals, and who helped everyone with every task for the order and harmony of the community.</t93> <t94>From the very beginning, because his house was the best in the village, others took it as an example.</t94> <t95>It had a small but bright living room, a dining room decorated with colorful flowers like a terrace, two bedrooms, a courtyard where a grand chestnut tree spread its branches, a well-kept garden, and a pen where goats, pigs, and chickens lived together in peace.</t95> <t96>The only animal forbidden to be kept not only in his house but throughout the village was the fighting rooster.</t96>
<t97>Ursula was no less skillful than her husband.</t97> <t98>This petite, hardworking, serious woman with steady nerves, who had never been heard singing even once in her life, would rush around from dawn till midnight, dragging the rustle of her starched petticoats behind her.</t98> <t99>The compacted earth floor, the unplastered adobe walls, the rough wooden furniture they made with their own hands, were always spotless thanks to her, and the old chest where they stored their clothes smelled wonderfully of basil.</t99>
<t100>Jose Arcadio Buendia, the most enterprising person the village had ever seen, had planned the placement of the houses so meticulously that no one had to exert more effort than anyone else to fetch water from the river.</t100> <t101>He had aligned the streets with such prudence that when the midday heat struck, no house was more exposed to the sun than the others.</t101> <t102>Within a few years, Macondo had become a village of three hundred people, more orderly and industrious than anything they had ever seen or heard of before.</t102> <t103>This was a truly happy village where no one lived past thirty and no one ever died.</t103>
<t104>Since the day the village was founded, Jose Arcadio Buendia made traps and cages.</t104> <t105>In a short time, he filled not only his own house but the entire village with canaries, hummingbirds, and nightingales.</t105> <t106>The chirping of so many different birds together became so irritating that Ursula started plugging her ears with wax to avoid going mad.</t106> <t107>When Melquiades' tribe first arrived, selling crystal balls for headaches, everyone was surprised at how they found this village lost in the swamp's lethargy, and the gypsies explained that they found the village by following the bird sounds.</t107>
<t108>This spirit of social enterprise soon faded in the face of magnets, astronomical calculations, dreams of transforming objects, and the urge to discover the wonders of the world.</t108> <t109>Jose Arcadio Buendia ceased to be that clean, elegant, hardworking man and became a sluggish, shabby figure with a beard that Ursula could barely trim with her vegetable knife, mingling with his hair.</t109> <t110>According to many, he had fallen victim to an unknown spell.</t110> <t111>But when he took out his tools to dig and called upon those gathered around him to open a path that would connect Macondo to great discoveries, even those who most believed in his madness left their homes and work to follow him.</t111>
<t112>Jose Arcadio Buendia was completely ignorant about the geography of the region.</t112> <t113>All he knew was that there were impassable mountain ranges to the east, and beyond the mountains - according to what his grandfather, the first Aureliano Buendia, had told him - lay the old city of Riohacha, where Sir Francis Drake had hunted crocodiles with a cannon and stuffed the ones he shot to send to Queen Elizabeth.</t113> <t114>In his youth, Jose Arcadio Buendia and his men, along with their children, animals, and all sorts of household items, had crossed these mountains to find a way out to the sea, and after twenty-six months, they had founded Macondo to avoid turning back from the expedition.</t114> <t115>This path did not interest Jose Arcadio Buendia at all, as it would only lead to the past.</t115>
<t116>In the south stretched swamps covered with endless vegetation; according to the gypsies, this vast swamp world had no end.</t116> <t117>The great swamp to the west merged into the boundless waters where soft-skinned, mammalian sea creatures, whose heads and bodies resembled women, and whose extraordinarily beautiful breasts lured sailors to their doom, roamed freely.</t117> <t118>The gypsies would sail on these waters for six months until they reached the path where the postal mules passed.</t118> <t119>According to Jose Arcadio's calculations, the only possibility of establishing a connection with civilization was the road to the north.</t119> <t120>Once he was convinced of this, he distributed the tools for clearing the way and hunting weapons to the people who had worked shoulder to shoulder with him while founding Macondo.</t120> <t121>He threw the navigation instruments and maps into his backpack and embarked on that dangerous adventure.</t121>
<t122>In the early days, they did not encounter any significant obstacles.</t122> <t123>They advanced along the rocky bed of the river to the place where they had found the soldier's armor years ago, then veered into the forest and took a path winding through wild orange trees.</t123> <t124>At the end of the first week, they shot and roasted a deer, but after eating only half of it, they agreed to salt the rest and save it for the coming days.</t124> <t125>By taking this precaution, they tried to delay, even if just a little, the necessity of eating the large parrots with bluish flesh that made one's mouth pucker.</t125> <t126>Then, for more than ten days, they did not see the sun.</t126> <t127>The ground became soft and mushy like volcanic ash, the plants grew denser and denser, the cries of birds and the clamor of monkeys gradually faded away, and the world was enveloped in endless sorrow.</t127> <t128>As the members of the exploration party stepped into the steaming pools of oil with their boots, and as their machetes slashed through blood-red lilies and golden salamanders, they were overwhelmed by memories that went back even further than Adam's sin in this damp and silent paradise.</t128>
<t129>For a week, they walked almost without speaking, like sleepwalkers in this gloomy universe illuminated only by the faint glow of fireflies, and their lungs filled with the suffocating smell of blood.</t129> <t130>As they walked and cleared a path, it would be covered again by new plants that sprang up in the blink of an eye, so they couldn't turn back.</t130> <t131>Jose Arcadio Buendia said, "No matter, the important thing is not to lose our direction."</t131> <t132>By following the direction of his compass, he was leading his men towards the elusive north to get them out of this enchanted region as soon as possible.</t132> <t133>It was a starless, pitch-dark night, but the darkness was pregnant with clean, clear air.</t133> <t134>The men, exhausted from the long march, set up their hammocks and for the first time in two weeks, fell into a deep sleep.</t134> <t135>When they woke up after the sun had risen high, they were struck dumb by the enchanting sight that met their eyes.</t135>
<t136>In the distance, among the ferns and palm trees, stood a large Spanish galleon that looked as if it could turn completely white and disintegrate into dust in the silent morning light.</t136> <t137>From the perfectly intact masts of the galleon, which was slightly tilted to the starboard side, hung tattered sails adorned with crests featuring orchids in the center.</t137> <t138>The hull, covered with a shield of petrified barnacles and soft algae, was firmly attached to the rocks.</t138> <t139>The large vessel seemed to exist within a unique space created by loneliness and neglect, protected from the ravages of time and bird droppings.</t139> <t140>In the interior section, carefully explored and examined by the scouting party, there was nothing but a dense forest of flowers.</t140>
<t141>The discovery of the galleon, which was proof of the proximity of the sea, shattered all of Jose Arcadio Buendia's enthusiasm.</t141> <t142>He interpreted the sudden appearance of the sea, which he had sought in vain at great cost and sacrifice, as an insurmountable obstacle in his path, a twist of cruel fate.</t142> <t143>Years later, when Colonel Aureliano Buendia passed through this area again, now a regular postal route, he found the charred skeleton of the boat in the middle of a poppy field.</t143>
<t144>Only when he was convinced that this story was not his father's fabrication did he wonder how the galleon had come so far inland.</t144> <t145>However, Jose Arcadio Buendia did not bother himself with this question when he found the sea four days' journey from the galleon.</t145> <t146>When that ashen, foamy, filthy sea, which was not worth the dangers and sacrifices of the adventure, lay before him, his dreams were shattered.</t146>
<t147>"Damn it!" he shouted, "Macondo is surrounded by the sea."</t147>
<t148>The view that Macondo was a peninsula, based on the haphazard map drawn by Jose Arcadio Buendia upon his return from the expedition, remained valid for a long time.</t148> <t149>In a fit of anger, as if punishing himself for the lack of foresight he showed when choosing the village's location, Jose Arcadio Buendia exaggerated the transportation difficulties on the map.</t149> <t150>We can't go anywhere from here anymore, he lamented to Ursula.</t150> <t151>We will waste our lives here without benefiting from the blessings of science.</t151> <t152>After months of pondering in the small room he used as a laboratory, he came to this decision and became fixated on moving Macondo to a better place.</t152>
<t153>But this time, Ursula had anticipated his crazy intention in advance.</t153> <t154>With the invisible but unbeatable effort of a tiny ant, she twisted the ears of the village women and stood them all in front of their husbands, who were already ready to pack up and leave.</t154> <t155>Jose Arcadio Buendia never realized when and by which opposing forces the plan he had devised was woven into a web of excuses, disappointments, and dismissals until the plan eventually turned into a mere delusion.</t155> <t156>Ursula watched him with innocent attention, and one morning, while he was packing the laboratory equipment into boxes in the back room, she even pitied her husband when she heard him muttering about his moving plans to himself.</t156> <t157>She waited for Jose Arcadio Buendia to finish his work.</t157> <t158>She didn't say a word as he nailed the crates shut and wrote his initials on them with a brush dipped in ink.</t158> <t159>She had learned (from what he muttered to himself) that her husband knew the village men would not support him in this endeavor, but when Jose Arcadio Buendia attempted to remove the door of the room, Ursula found the courage to ask him what he was doing.</t159> <t160>Her husband replied in a hurt tone, Since no one wants to go, we will go on our own.</t160> <t161>Ursula remained completely unfazed.</t161>
<t162>"We are not going anywhere," he said.</t162> <t163>"We had children here, so we will stay here."</t163>
<t164>Jose Arcadio Buendia,</t164>
<t165>"But no one has died yet," he replied.</t165> <t166>If a person doesn't have their dead beneath the land they live on, that person is not of that land.</t166>
<t167>Ursula resisted with an unwavering determination:</t167>
<t168>If my death is necessary for you to stay here, then I will die.</t168>
<t169>Jose Arcadio Buendia never thought his wife had such willpower.</t169> <t170>With all his imagination, he tried to deceive his wife by talking about an astonishing world where, by sprinkling magical water on the ground, fruit trees would grow wherever you wanted, and every remedy for aches and pains was sold for next to nothing.</t170> <t171>But all these stories were of no use to Ursula.</t171>
<t172>"Instead of wasting time with your crazy inventions, think about your sons a little," he said.</t172> <t173>"Look at them, they are wandering around aimlessly like wild donkeys."</t173>
<t174>As soon as his wife said "look," Jose Arcadio Buendia looked out the window and saw the children walking barefoot in the sunny garden.</t174> <t175>It seemed to him as if Ursula had cast a spell and the children had suddenly appeared at that moment.</t175> <t176>That was when something inside him, something mysterious that tore him away from the present and took him to the unknown recesses of his memories, happened.</t176> <t177>While Ursula swept the house, now free from the danger of being abandoned for the rest of her life, Jose Arcadio Buendia stood by the window, his eyes fixed on the children.</t177> <t178>After a while, as he wiped his tearful eyes with the back of his hand, he sighed with a resigned attitude towards his fate.</t178>
<t179>-Okay, he said.</t179> <t180>Tell them to come and help me empty the chests.</t180>
<t181>Their eldest son, Jose Arcadio, was fourteen.</t181> <t182>He had a thick head, dense hair, and his temperament was like his father's.</t182> <t183>Like his father, he was large and strong, but it was evident from his infancy that he did not inherit his father's imagination.</t183> <t184>He was conceived and born during that arduous journey to cross the mountains before Macondo was founded, and his parents celebrated because he did not resemble the wild animals of the mountains.</t184> <t185>Aureliano, who held the distinction of being the first person born in Macondo, would turn six in March.</t185> <t186>He was a quiet, introverted child.</t186> <t187>He had cried while still in his mother's womb and was born with his eyes wide open.</t187> <t188>While they were cutting his umbilical cord, he examined the room with fearless curiosity, turning his head from side to side, scrutinizing the faces around him.</t188> <t189>Then, without paying attention to those who came to see him, he fixed his eyes on the ceiling covered with palm leaves that seemed about to collapse from the rain.</t189>
<t190>When Aureliano was three years old, Ursula did not recall the sharpness of that gaze until the moment he entered the kitchen just as his mother was about to take the boiling soup pot off the stove and place it on the table.</t190> <t191>The child stood at the door in astonishment and said, The soup will spill.</t191> <t192>The pot was standing firmly in the middle of the table, but before the child had even finished his sentence, it began to move toward the edge of the table as if driven by an impulse from within and fell to the ground, breaking.</t192> <t193>Terrified, Ursula told her husband what had happened, but Jose Arcadio Buendia took it in stride.</t193> <t194>He always did that; to him, their sons might as well not have existed.</t194> <t195>He did not care about the children, partly because he equated childhood with mental deficiency, and partly because his mind was always preoccupied with his own absurd, fanciful constructions.</t195>
<t196>From the day she called the children to help her unpack the laboratory tools from the crates, she dedicated her life to them.</t196> <t197>In that small back room, which was increasingly filled with strange maps and exaggerated drawings, she taught the children to read and write, to add and subtract, not only to the extent of her knowledge but also by pushing the boundaries of her imagination, she told them about the wonders of the world.</t197> <t198>This is how the children learned that at the southern tip of Africa, there lived people so wise and serene that they did nothing but sit and think in their spare time, and that it was possible to cross the Aegean Sea on foot by hopping from island to island all the way to the port of Thessaloniki.</t198> <t199>These mind-bending lessons were so deeply ingrained in the children's memories that, years later, just a second before giving the order to fire to the execution squad, Colonel Aureliano Buendia recalled that hot March day, when his father interrupted his physics lesson, raised his hands in the air, stood as if mesmerized without blinking, and listened to the sounds of trumpets, drums, tambourines, and cymbals as the gypsies arrived in the village to announce the latest and most astonishing discovery of the sages of Memphis.</t199>
<t200>The newcomers were a new tribe made up of handsome young men and beautiful women who knew no language other than their own, had oily skin, and were quick with their hands. They had colorful painted parrots that sang Italian arias, chickens that laid a hundred golden eggs while keeping pace with the tambourine's rhythm, trained monkeys that could read people's minds, versatile machines that could sew buttons while also putting out fires, devices that made people forget bad memories, poultices that healed the wounds of the past, and thousands of other extraordinary and unheard-of inventions, dances, and music that turned the streets into a carnival chaos, inspiring Jose Arcadio Buendia to invent a memory machine to keep track of it all.</t200> <t201>In the blink of an eye, they transformed the village into a completely different place.</t201> <t202>The Macondians, bewildered by the fair's crowd, began to lose their way in their own streets.</t202>
<t203>Jose Arcadio Buendia, in order not to lose them in this chaos, held one child's hand with one hand and the other child's hand with the other, bumping into gold-toothed acrobats and six-armed jugglers, feeling suffocated by the smell of manure and sandals rising from the crowd, he frantically ran from place to place to explain the endless mysteries of that incredible nightmare, searching for Melquiades.</t203> <t204>He asked a few gypsies who did not understand his language.</t204> <t205>Finally, when he reached the place where Melquiades always set up his tent, he encountered a tight-lipped Armenian who was hawking an elixir that made people invisible in Spanish.</t205> <t206>Jose Arcadio Buendia, elbowing his way through the crowd that was watching the man as if mesmerized, just as he was about to ask his question, the man gulped down the amber-colored object in one go.</t206> <t207>After the gypsy scrutinized Jose Arcadio Buendia from head to toe with his terrifying gaze, he suddenly transformed into a steaming, deadly pool of tar.</t207>
<t208>The answer Jose Arcadio Buendia received to his question echoed over the pitch deposit: Melquiades is dead.</t208> <t209>Jose Arcadio Buendia was stunned by the news, unable to collect himself until the crowd dispersed to see other wonders and the tight-lipped Armenian's deposit evaporated.</t209> <t210>Later, the other gypsies confirmed that Melquiades had died of fever in the port of Singapore and that his body was thrown into the deepest part of the Java Sea.</t210> <t211>The children paid no attention.</t211> <t212>They insisted on going to see the new discovery of the Memphis Sages, announced at the door of the tent said to belong to Sultan Suleiman.</t212> <t213>They insisted so much that finally Jose Arcadio Buendia paid thirty reals and let them into the tent.</t213> <t214>Inside the tent, a giant man with a body covered in hair, a shaved head, a copper ring in his nose, and a heavy iron chain on his foot was standing guard over a pirate chest.</t214>
<t215>When Dev opened the chest, a coolness spread through the air.</t215> <t216>Inside the chest, there was a large, transparent mass that, with its tiny needles, split the sunlight and turned it into colorful stars.</t216> <t217>Knowing that the children immediately expected an explanation from him, Jose Arcadio, flustered, mumbled something under his breath:</t217>
<t218>-This is the largest diamond in the world.</t218>
<t219>The Gypsy replied, No, they call this ice.</t219>
<t220>When Jose Arcadio Buendia reached out his hand towards the mold without understanding the explanation, the giant pushed him away.</t220> <t221>You'll have to pay five more reals to touch it, he said.</t221> <t222>Jose Arcadio Buendia gave the money and placed his hand on the ice, standing still for a few minutes, his heart filled with the fear and excitement of touching this mystery; Unable to decide what to say, he gave another ten reals so that his sons could also partake in this astonishing experience.</t222> <t223>Little Jose Arcadio did not want to touch the ice.</t223> <t224>As for Aureliano, he took a step forward and touched the ice with his hand, only to pull it back immediately.</t224> <t225>In astonishment, he exclaimed; It's burning hot like fire!</t225> <t226>His father forgot the disappointments of his mad experiments and the corpse of Melquiades, who had been fed to the squid.</t226> <t227>He paid another five reals and, pressing his hand on the block of ice as if swearing on a holy book, he shouted:</t227>
<t228>-Here it is, the greatest invention of our age.</t228>
<t229>When the pirate Sir Francis Drake attacked Riohacha in the sixteenth century, Ursula Iguaran's great-great-great-great-grandmother was so terrified by the alarm bells and cannon blasts that she lost control of her limbs and sat right on a burning stove.</t229> <t230>Because of those burns, she became a useless woman to her man for the rest of her life.</t230> <t231>She could only sit on one side with the support of pillows, and her walk must have been affected as well, for she never went out in public to walk again.</t231> <t232>She became so obsessed with the smell of burns emanating from her body that she stopped seeing anyone.</t232> <t233>She couldn't bear to sleep, fearing that the English and their ferocious dogs would burst through the bedroom window and brand her private parts with hot irons, so she would spend the nights in the courtyard.</t233> <t234>Her Aragonese merchant husband, the father of her two children, spent half of his fortune on medicine and entertainment to free her from this fear.</t234> <t235>In the end, he sold everything he had, took his family, and settled in a village at the foot of a mountain, far from the sea, where peaceful Indians lived, and he built his wife a windowless bedroom so that the pirates in her dreams couldn't find a way in.</t235>
<t236>In that remote village, the great-great-great-grandfather of Ursula, who partnered with a local tobacco grower named Don Jose Arcadio Buendia, made a great fortune from this profitable business in just a few years.</t236> <t237>A few centuries later, the great-great-great-grandson of the local tobacco grower married the great-great-great-granddaughter of the Aragonese merchant.</t237> <t238>That is why, whenever Ursula got fed up with her husband's crazy ideas, she would instantly transcend three centuries of fate and curse the day Sir Francis Drake attacked Riohacha.</t238> <t239>She did this somewhat to vent her feelings, because until their death, they were bound to each other by a bond stronger than love, a shared sense of guilt.</t239> <t240>They were cousins.</t240> <t241>They grew up together in that old village, which had become one of the most beautiful towns in the province thanks to the hard work and customs of their ancestors.</t241> <t242>Although it was clear they would marry from the moment they were born, their own relatives initially opposed this desire when they expressed their wish to marry.</t242> <t243>They feared that iguana-like lizards would be born from the healthy children of these two races that had intermarried and mingled for centuries.</t243>
<t244>Ursula's aunt had married an uncle of Jose Arcadio Buendia and gave birth to a boy who, because he was born with a tail that was furry like a tassel, curly like a corkscrew, and cartilaginous, could not loosen his belt for any woman until he was forty-two years old and had to wear loose pants for his entire life.</t244> <t245>This pig's tail, which no woman was allowed to see, became the man's doom when a butcher friend, as a favor, chopped it off with his cleaver.</t245> <t246>Jose Arcadio Buendia, with the stubbornness of being nineteen years old, solved the problem in one fell swoop: I don't care if my children are piglets, as long as they know how to speak.</t246> <t247>Thus, they got married with a wedding that lasted three days and three nights, with fireworks and brass band music.</t247> <t248>They would have been quite happy if Ursula's mother hadn't scared her daughter by saying she would give birth to children with pig tails, and even advised her not to share her husband's bed.</t248> <t249>Fearing that her large, uncomprehending husband might violate her in his sleep, Ursula began to wear a primitive chastity belt made of sailcloth, tightened with crisscrossed leather straps, and fastened with a large iron buckle in the front.</t249> <t250>They lived like this for a few months.</t250>
<t251>During the day, Jose Arcadio Buendia was busy with fighting cocks, while Ursula was working on embroidery beside her mother.</t251> <t252>At night, instead of making love, they would wrestle fiercely for hours.</t252> <t253>Over time, the people around them sensed something was amiss, and despite being married for a year, rumors spread that Ursula was still a virgin because her husband was impotent.</t253> <t254>This rumor eventually reached the ears of Jose Arcadio Buendia.</t254>
<t255>With an extremely calm demeanor, he said to his wife, "Look, what will people say."</t255>
<t256>Ursula replied, "Let them say whatever they want, we know it's not like that."</t256>
<t257>This situation continued like this for another six months until that tremendous Sunday when Jose Arcadio Buendia defeated Prudencio Aguilar in a cockfight.</t257> <t258>Seeing his rooster covered in blood, Prudencio Aguilar, driven mad with rage, moved quite a distance away from Jose Arcadio Buendia so that everyone in the fighting area could hear:</t258>
<t259>"Good news!" he shouted, "Maybe the Rooster will do your wife a favor too."</t259>
<t260>Jose Arcadio Buendia, without batting an eye, took his rooster.</t260> <t261>To those present, he said, "I'll be right back," and then turned to Prudencio Aguilar:</t261>
<t262>-Go home and get a gun, I will kill you.</t262>
<t263>He returned ten minutes later with the spear inherited from his grandfather, its tip notched.</t263> <t264>Prudencio Aguilar was waiting for him at the entrance of the cockfighting ring, where more than half the village had gathered.</t264> <t265>He didn't have a chance to defend himself.</t265> <t266>The spear, hurled by Jose Arcadio Buendia with the strength of a bull and the sharpshooting precision of the first Aureliano Buendia, who had wiped out the local tigers, pierced his throat.</t266> <t267>That night, while everyone kept vigil over the dead man at the fighting ring, Jose Arcadio Buendia burst into the bedroom just as his wife was about to put on her chastity belt.</t267> <t268>Pointing his spear, he commanded, Take it off.</t268> <t269>Ursula understood her husband's intention.</t269> <t270>You'll be responsible for what happens to us, she murmured.</t270> <t271>Jose Arcadio Buendia thrust his spear into the earthen floor.</t271>
<t272>"If you give birth to a monstrous freak, we will also raise monstrous freaks," he said.</t272> <t273>"But from now on, no one will be killed in this village because of you."</t273>
<t274>It was a warm, moonlit, beautiful June night.</t274> <t275>Prudencio Aguilar's relatives, indifferent to the breeze filled with tears, stayed awake and frolicked in bed until morning.</t275>
<t276>The incident was covered up as an honor duel, but both of them were left with a guilty conscience.</t276> <t277>One night, Ursula, who couldn't sleep, went out to the courtyard to get some water and saw Prudencio Aguilar next to the water jug.</t277> <t278>The man was blue-black, with a sorrowful expression on his face, trying to plug the hole in his throat with alpha grass.</t278> <t279>Ursula wasn't scared; she felt pity.</t279>
<t280>She went to the room and told her husband what she had seen, but her husband didn't dwell on it much.</t280> <t281>He dismissed it, saying, "I guess we can't bear the pangs of conscience."</t281> <t282>Two nights later, Ursula saw Prudencio Aguilar again, this time in the bathroom, cleaning the dried blood from his throat with alpha grass.</t282> <t283>Another night, she saw him wandering in the rain.</t283> <t284>Jose Arcadio Buendia, upset by these visions his wife was seeing, grabbed the spear and rushed into the garden.</t284> <t285>The dead man stood before him with that sorrowful look.</t285>
<t286>Jose Arcadio Buendia shouted, "Go to hell!"</t286> <t287>"If you come back again, I'll kill you once more."</t287>
<t288>Prudencio Aguilar did not leave, and Jose Arcadio Buendia did not dare to swing the spear.</t288> <t289>After that night, he never had a good night's sleep again.</t289> <t290>The great loneliness of the dead man standing in the rain, his deep longing for the living, his haste to find water in the house to wet the plug of alfa grass and clean his wound, weighed heavily on Jose Arcadio Buendia.</t290> <t291>He must be suffering a lot, he said to Ursula.</t291> <t292>It's clear that he's heartbroken from loneliness.</t292> <t293>This touched Ursula so much that the next time she found the dead man checking the pots over the stove, she understood what he was looking for and started leaving jugs of water in every corner of the house.</t293> <t294>One night, when Jose Arcadio Buendia found him washing his wound in his own room, he lost his patience.</t294>
<t295>"Alright Prudencio," he said.</t295> <t296>"We will take our leave and go as far away from this village as possible."</t296> <t297>"You can rest easy now."</t297>
<t298>This is how they embarked on the journey to cross the mountains.</t298> <t299>A few friends of Jose Arcadio Buendia, who were as young as he was, dismantled their homes and gathered their belongings in the excitement of the adventure. They set out towards lands that no one had promised them, taking their wives and children along.</t299>
<t300>Jose Arcadio Buendia, before setting out, buried his spear in the yard, thinking that perhaps this way he could give Prudencio Aguilar some peace in his grave, and he slaughtered his unique fighting cocks one by one.</t300> <t301>Ursula took nothing with her except a few pieces of kitchenware from her dowry chest and the small box containing the gold inherited from her father.</t301> <t302>They had not charted a definite course for themselves.</t302> <t303>They would walk in the exact opposite direction of the road to Riohacha, simply to leave no trace behind and avoid encountering acquaintances.</t303>
<t304>It was a foolish journey.</t304> <t305>Fourteen months later, Ursula, whose stomach was ruined by monkey meat and snake stew, gave birth to a boy who looked entirely human.</t305> <t306>Because her legs were swollen like logs and her varicose veins bulged like fingers due to the pregnancy, she spent half the journey in a hammock carried by two men.</t306> <t307>The children, who looked pitiful with their sunken bellies and lifeless eyes, still endured the journey better than their parents, seeing it as a kind of game.</t307> <t308>After nearly two years of trekking through mountains and valleys, one morning they earned the distinction of being the first mortals to see the western slopes of the mountain range.</t308> <t309>From the cloud-shrouded peak, they saw the vast swamp spreading out like a giant puddle towards the end of the world.</t309> <t310>But they never found the sea.</t310> <t311>After losing their way in the swamp and wandering for several months, one night they camped by a pebbly river that flowed like a frozen stream of glass, far from the last Indians they had encountered on the road.</t311>
<t312>Years later, during the second civil war, Colonel Aureliano Buendia tried the same route to raid Riohacha, but after six days of travel, he realized it was madness.</t312> <t313>Even so, that night when they camped by the river, his father's caravan looked like shipwreck survivors, but instead of dwindling during the journey, they had multiplied and were all determined to stake their claim in the world (and indeed they did).</t313> <t314>That very night, Jose Arcadio Buendia dreamed of a bustling, noisy city with walls made of mirrors rising up from where they camped.</t314> <t315>When he asked what this place was called, they told him a name he had never heard before, which had no meaning, but created a supernatural echo in his dream: Macondo.</t315> <t316>The next day, Jose Arcadio Buendia convinced his men that they would never find the sea.</t316> <t317>He told them to cut down the trees and clear a spot in the coolest part of the shore, right by the river, and that is where they founded the village.</t317>
<t318>Jose Arcadio Buendia could never interpret the dream of houses with mirror walls until the day he discovered ice.</t318><t319>When he saw the ice, he thought he had grasped the deep meaning of the dream.</t319><t320>He thought that in the near future, they could produce large blocks of ice from an ordinary substance like water and build the new houses of the village with them.</t320><t321>Macondo would no longer be a place like an oven where hinges and door knockers bent from the heat, but would turn into a city as cold as winter.</t321><t322>If he did not insist on establishing an ice factory, the only reason was that he had devoted himself to the education of his sons, especially Aureliano, who showed a peculiar talent in alchemy at that time.</t322><t323>The laboratory was cleaned and became spotless.</t323><t324>Father and son, without getting carried away by a flood of new discoveries, calmly reviewed Melquiades' notes and, with long and patient effort, tried to separate Ursula's gold from the bottom of the pot where it had burned and stuck.</t324><t325>Young Jose Arcadio did not participate much in this work.</t325><t326>While his father was engrossed in the stills, the stubborn eldest son, who had always looked older than his age, grew into a big, burly young man.</t326><t327>His voice changed.</t327><t328>He started to grow a mustache.</t328>
<t329>One night, when Ursula entered the room where the boy was undressing, she was overcome with a mixture of shame and pity: He was the first naked man she had seen since her husband, and his equipment was so developed that it almost had an abnormal appearance.</t329> <t330>Pregnant with her third child, Ursula felt the same terror she had felt on her wedding night.</t330>
<t331>At that time, a cheerful, coquettish, foul-mouthed woman who also knew how to read fortunes used to come and go as a daily worker.</t331><t332>Ursula confided her son's condition to her.</t332><t333>She thought that such a disproportionately large tool was as abnormal as her nephew's pig's tail.</t333><t334>When the woman heard this, she burst into laughter that scattered in all directions like broken glass.</t334><t335>On the contrary, she said, The boy will be lucky.</t335><t336>To confirm her prophecy, a few days later she brought the cards and locked herself in the pantry behind the kitchen with Jose Arcadio.</t336><t337>She calmly spread the cards on the carpenter's bench, and while the boy stood beside her more out of boredom than curiosity, she said whatever came to her mind.</t337><t338>Then suddenly she reached out and touched the boy.</t338><t339>She must have been truly astonished, as nothing but "Oh my God" came out of her mouth.</t339><t340>Jose Arcadio felt his marrow froth and swell.</t340><t341>He felt a vague sense of dread and was overcome with the urge to cry his heart out.</t341>
<t342>The woman did not make any insinuations.</t342> <t343>But Jose Arcadio spent the whole night searching for the woman, for the scent that rose from her armpits and seeped into his own skin.</t343> <t344>He wanted the woman to always be by his side, to mother him, to never leave the pantry, and for her to always say "Oh my God."</t344> <t345>One day, unable to bear it any longer, he went to the woman's house.</t345> <t346>He sat in the living room without saying a word, staring blankly, making a formal visit.</t346> <t347>At that moment, he did not desire the woman at all.</t347> <t348>He found the woman completely different, as if she were someone else entirely, with no relation to the fantasy created by her scent.</t348> <t349>Jose Arcadio drank his coffee and left the house in distress.</t349> <t350>That night, during the terrible hours when he couldn't sleep, he desired the woman again with a wild longing, but this time he wanted her not as she was in the pantry, but as she was that day.</t350>
<t351>Days later, the woman unexpectedly called him to the house where she lived with her mother and took the boy to the bedroom under the pretext of showing him the playing cards.</t351> <t352>Then she touched the boy so recklessly that after the initial shiver passed, the young man felt disappointed; what he felt was more fear than pleasure.</t352> <t353>The woman told him to come and see her that night.</t353> <t354>He, knowing that he had no strength left in his knees, said okay, thinking he should get away as soon as possible.</t354> <t355>But that night, as he tossed and turned in his bed burning with fever, he realized that even if he couldn't manage it, he still couldn't help but go to the woman.</t355> <t356>In the dark, listening to his brother's calm breathing, his father's dry cough in the adjacent room, the chickens' clucking in the courtyard, the mosquitoes' buzzing, the pounding of his heart, and the rush of a world he had never noticed before, he dressed by feel and threw himself into the sleeping street.</t356> <t357>Inwardly, he wished that, as the woman had said, the door would not only be unlocked but also barred with an iron rod.</t357>
<t358>But the door was open.</t358> <t359>He pushed the door with the tip of his finger, the sad and distinct creak released by the hinges sent icy echoes through the young man's insides.</t359> <t360>As he tried not to make a sound and slipped sideways to step inside, that smell hit his nose.</t360> <t361>He was still standing in the hall where the woman's three brothers had set up hammocks and were lying down.</t361> <t362>In the dark, he couldn't make out the positions of the hammocks, and to avoid mistaking one bed for another, he was feeling his way to the bedroom door.</t362> <t363>He found the door.</t363> <t364>But he stumbled over the hammock ropes, which were lower than he had thought, and the man who had been snoring until that moment turned over in his sleep and mumbled, It's Wednesday.</t364>
<t365>Jose Arcadio, while pushing the bedroom door, couldn't prevent it from scraping against the uneven floor.</t365> <t366>In the pitch darkness, he suddenly realized that he had completely lost his way.</t366> <t367>In the tiny room lay the woman's mother, sister, sister's husband with their two children, and the woman herself, but perhaps she wasn't in that room.</t367> <t368>If the scent hadn't permeated every corner of the house, he could have found his way by it, but the woman's scent had, just as it had seeped into his own skin, penetrated every nook and cranny of the house with its full intensity.</t368> <t369>As Jose Arcadio stood still, pondering how he had found himself in such an empty state and let himself go, a hand feeling the darkness with fingers spread wide touched his face.</t369> <t370>Jose Arcadio wasn't surprised, because deep down he had been expecting it.</t370>
<t371>He let himself go into that hand and did not object to being dragged to a place whose whereabouts were unknown.</t371> <t372>Then his clothes were stripped off, and in that bottomless darkness where his arms couldn't reach anything to hold onto, he was tossed from side to side like a sack of potatoes.</t372> <t373>It didn't smell like a woman here, it smelled like ammonia, and the more he tried to picture the woman's face, Ursula's face loomed before him.</t373> <t374>He vaguely realized that he was doing something he had wanted to do for a long time but never really thought could be done, yet he didn't know what he was doing, because he couldn't tell where his feet were, where his head was, whose feet and whose head they were, and he could no longer endure the rumbling rising from his kidneys as if glaciers were collapsing, the gas in his intestines, the fear, the maddening silence, and the terrifying loneliness, wanting both to escape and to stay there forever.</t374>
<t375>The woman's name was Pilar Ternera.</t375> <t376>Her family had dragged her along on the expedition that resulted in the founding of Macondo, to separate her from the man who had deflowered her at fourteen and used her until she was twenty-two, but who, being a scoundrel, refused to marry her.</t376> <t377>The man promised to follow her to the ends of the earth, but later, after he had settled his affairs.</t377> <t378>The girl had grown tired of waiting, interpreting every man who appeared in the cards—whether he would come in three days, three months, or three years, whether by land or by sea—as the one who would come for her.</t378> <t379>Waiting had taken away the firmness of her hips, the perkiness of her breasts, and her affection, but her wild heart remained the same.</t379> <t380>Jose Arcadio, madly in love with that enormous toy, would dive into the labyrinth of the room after her every night.</t380> <t381>One time, he found the door bolted and, knowing that once he started he would see it through to the end, he knocked on the door several times.</t381> <t382>After what seemed like an endless wait, the woman opened the door.</t382>
<t383>During the day, he would lie down and drift into dreams, secretly savoring the memories of the previous night.</t383> <t384>But when the woman, with her cheerful, carefree, and talkative demeanor, came home, Jose Arcadio no longer needed to hide his excitement, because this woman, whose tinkling laughter scared the pigeons, had no connection whatsoever with that invisible force that taught him to hold his breath, control his heartbeat, and understand why men fear death.</t384> <t385>Jose Arcadio had become so withdrawn that when his father and brother caused a commotion with the news that they had separated Ursula's gold from the mining debris, he couldn't even understand why the household was so happy.</t385>
<t386>The father and son had really succeeded in this task after days of hard work and effort.</t386> <t387>Ursula was overjoyed and even thanked God for the invention of alchemy.</t387> <t388>Meanwhile, the villagers were flocking to the laboratory.</t388> <t389>They were offering biscuits spread with guava jam to those who came to celebrate this miracle.</t389> <t390>Jose Arcadio Buendia was proudly showing the villagers the recovered gold in the crucible as if he had invented it himself.</t390> <t391>After showing it to everyone, he stood in front of his eldest son, who hadn't been near the laboratory for a few days.</t391> <t392>Pressing the dry and yellowish lump to the boy's nose, he asked, "What do you think this looks like?"</t392> <t393>Jose Arcadio responded as he felt:</t393>
<t394>To the dog poop.</t394>
<t395>His father slapped him so hard with the back of his hand that blood and tears mingled on the boy's face.</t395> <t396>That night, Pilar Ternera found the bottle and cotton in the dark by feel and treated the boy's swollen face with mountain tobacco, then she fulfilled her own desires by trying to love him without causing him pain.</t396> <t397>They became so intimate that after a while they began to whisper to each other without realizing it.</t397>
<t398>"I want to be alone with you," said the young man, "I will tell everyone openly, so we can stop meeting in secret."</t398>
<t399>The woman did not attempt to soothe him.</t399>
<t400>"That would be great," he said.</t400> <t401>"If we are alone, we can light the lamp, gaze at each other to our heart's content, shout out whatever comes to mind without fear of being heard, and you can whisper naughty things in my ear."</t401>
<t402>This conversation, the gnawing grudge he harbored towards his father, and the possibility of wild lovemaking, gave the young man such courage that words cannot describe.</t402> <t403>Without feeling the need for a preliminary explanation, he told his brother everything that had happened.</t403>
<t404>At first, Aureliano could only grasp the great danger that could arise from his brother's adventure, he never understood the appealing side of it.</t404> <t405>As time went by, a worry started to consume the child.</t405> <t406>He was contemplating the ins and outs of the danger in his mind, experiencing his brother's pain and pleasure himself, sharing his brother's fear and happiness.</t406> <t407>Like lying on a bed of embers, he stayed awake until dawn, waiting for his brother.</t407>
<t408>After he arrived, they would talk until it was time to get up.</t408> <t409>So much so that, before long, both of them, in the drowsiness brought on by sleeplessness, neglected both alchemy and their father's wisdom, and started hiding in every nook and cranny.</t409> <t410>These boys have gone mad, said Ursula, there's definitely a worm in them.</t410> <t411>She crushed some goosefoot herb and made a nauseating medicine.</t411> <t412>The boys, with an unexpected indifference, drank the medicine without a sound and, after making eleven trips to the toilet in one day and expelling rose-colored parasites, they convinced Ursula that their drowsiness and distraction were due to these worms, and celebrated by showing them to everyone they met.</t412> <t413>Meanwhile, Aureliano not only understood his brother's experience but lived it as if it had happened to him.</t413> <t414>Once, while his brother was describing the intricacies of love games in great detail, Aureliano interrupted him and asked, What is it like?</t414> <t415>Jose Arcadio responded without hesitation:</t415>
<t416>-Just like an earthquake.</t416>
<t417>Amaranta was born at two o'clock in the morning on a Thursday in January.</t417> <t418>Before anyone entered the room, Ursula examined the girl all over.</t418> <t419>Although the child was pale-skinned and slippery like a salamander, she looked human in every way.</t419> <t420>Until the house was filled to the brim with neighbors, Aureliano didn't even notice the new baby.</t420> <t421>Then, taking advantage of the commotion, he went out; he set out to find his brother, who had been missing from his bed since eleven o'clock at night.</t421> <t422>Without even stopping to think about how he would pull him out of Pilar Ternera's bedroom, he went to the woman's house, determined.</t422> <t423>He wandered around the house for hours, whistling coded signals, and when dawn broke, he had no choice but to return home.</t423> <t424>When he entered his mother's room, what did he see but Jose Arcadio, with innocence written all over his face, playing with his newborn sister.</t424>
<t425>The Gypsies had just returned when Ursula's mourning period had just ended.</t425> <t426>These were the same acrobats and jugglers who had brought the ice last time.</t426> <t427>It soon became clear that, unlike Melquiades' tribe, they were not harbingers of developments and innovations but rather purveyors of entertainment.</t427>
<t428>Even when they brought ice, instead of talking about the benefits it would bring to human life, they advertised it as if it were a circus trick.</t428> <t429>This time, along with a thousand and one tricks, they brought a flying carpet.</t429> <t430>But they presented it not as a fundamental contribution to the development of transportation, but as an entertainment device.</t430> <t431>The people dug up their last gold coins from the ground just to take a quick flight over the village.</t431> <t432>Taking advantage of the chaos that everyone was participating in, Jose Arcadio and Pilar mingled with the crowd and had fun for hours.</t432> <t433>Among all those people, they were two newly in love, flying with happiness, and they almost believed that love was a much deeper and more comforting feeling than the ecstatic but fleeting happiness of the nights they secretly met.</t433> <t434>However, Pilar broke the spell.</t434> <t435>Jose Arcadio, caught up in the joy of being with her, forgot the time and place and brought the world crashing down on the boy's head.</t435> <t436>She said, "Now you have truly become a man."</t436> <t437>When the young man couldn't understand what she meant, she said it plainly:</t437>
<t438>-You're going to be a father.</t438>
<t439>Jose Arcadio couldn't step out of the house for a few days.</t439> <t440>As soon as he heard Pilar's cheerful laughter from the kitchen, he would take refuge in the laboratory where, with Ursula's permission, the alchemy experiments had regained momentum.</t440> <t441>Jose Arcadio Buendia welcomed his wayward son with joy and included him in the alchemical stone research he had finally decided to undertake.</t441> <t442>One evening, when a gypsy, who had seated the village children waving happily behind him, flew the carpet past the laboratory window, the boys' minds were captivated by the flying carpet, but Jose Arcadio Buendia didn't even turn his head to look.</t442> <t443>Let them think they are flying, he said.</t443> <t444>We will fly better than them, and not with a tattered blanket, but with scientific tools.</t444> <t445>Although Jose Arcadio seemed interested, he couldn't fathom the mystery of the alchemist's egg, which resembled a clumsily blown, crooked bottle.</t445> <t446>He couldn't rid himself of his worries.</t446> <t447>He stopped eating and drinking, and couldn't sleep.</t447>
<t448>Just as his father would become sullen when his experiments failed, Jose Arcadio's face would also fall into a thousand pieces, so much so that Jose Arcadio</t448> <t449>Buendia thought the boy was too obsessed with this alchemy business and removed him from his duties in the laboratory.</t449> <t450>Aureliano knew that his brother's problem did not stem from searching for the philosopher's stone, but he couldn't get a word out of him.</t450> <t451>Jose Arcadio's former sincerity was gone; he had ceased to be someone who shared his secrets and talked, becoming instead a withdrawn, rude person.</t451> <t452>He harbored a bitter resentment against the whole world and avoided everyone.</t452>
<t453>One night, as usual, he slipped out of bed, but instead of going to Pilar Ternera's house, he mingled with the noisy crowd of the fair.</t453> <t454>Among the myriad of shows, after wandering without turning his head to any of them, something unrelated to the exhibited skills caught his eye: It was a very young gypsy girl, almost a child, whose neck was weighed down by the beads she wore, and she was the most beautiful woman Jose Arcadio had ever seen in his life.</t454> <t455>The girl was standing among the crowd watching the tragic adventure of the man who turned into a snake for not obeying his parents.</t455>
<t456>Jose Arcadio showed no interest in the performance.</t456> <t457>While the pitiful interrogation of the snake man continued, he pushed through the crowd to the front row where the girl was standing, walked up, and stood right behind her.</t457> <t458>He pressed closely against the girl's back.</t458> <t459>When the girl tried to pull away, Jose Arcadio leaned in even more.</t459> <t460>It was then that the girl felt him completely.</t460> <t461>Unable to believe this was real, she leaned against the young man in shock and fear without moving, and finally, unable to resist, she turned her head and gave a timid smile.</t461>
<t462>At that moment, two gypsies locked the snake man in his cage and took him to the tent.</t462> <t463>The gypsy running the show addressed the audience: Ladies and gentlemen, now we will show you the chilling ordeal of the woman who, as punishment for seeing something she shouldn't have, is beheaded every night at this exact hour for the past one hundred and fifty years.</t463> <t464>Jose Arcadio and the gypsy girl did not watch the beheading scene.</t464> <t465>They went to the girl's tent, and while undressing, they began to kiss each other with frantic passion.</t465> <t466>When the gypsy girl took off her starched lace corset, she was left completely naked.</t466> <t467>Her newly budding breasts and her spindly legs, which were not even as thick as Jose Arcadio's arms, made her resemble a frail frog, but she had a boldness and warmth that made one forget her frailty.</t467>
<t468>However, Jose Arcadio could not respond to the girl's advances, because the tent they entered was a busy place where the gypsies constantly came and went to take their circus equipment and do their work, and sometimes even stood by the bed to play dice.</t468> <t469>The lantern hanging from the central pole illuminated everything.</t469> <t470>When they paused their foreplay, as the girl, not knowing what to do, tried to arouse the completely naked Jose Arcadio lying on the bed, a gypsy woman with marvelous skin, followed by a man who was neither from the camp nor the villagers, entered the tent and started undressing by the bed.</t470>
<t471>At one point, the woman's eyes fell on Jose Arcadio, and she examined his fallen, beastly sign from head to toe with fiery excitement.</t471>
<t472>"Oh my boy," he exclaimed, "I hope you stay just the way you are."</t472>
<t473>The girl next to Jose Arcadio snapped, Leave us alone, and the newcomers knelt down on the floor beside the bed.</t473> <t474>Their passionate caressing also fueled Jose Arcadio's desire.</t474> <t475>At the first thrust, the girl's bones cracked as if a box of dominoes had scattered around, a light sweat broke out on her skin, a mournful moan and a faint smell of mud rose from her entire body as her eyes filled with tears.</t475>
<t476>But he endured this blow with an admirable will and bravery.</t476> <t477>Arcadio felt as if he were ascending to the realm of angels, and the love-filled, explicit words that overflowed from his heart entered the girl's ears and began to spill from her mouth in the gypsy language.</t477> <t478>It was a Thursday.</t478> <t479>On Saturday night, Jose Arcadio wrapped a red scarf around his head and left with the gypsies.</t479>
<t480>Ursula, upon realizing that he had disappeared, turned the village upside down.</t480> <t481>At the place where the tribe had camped, there was nothing but a trash can among the extinguished fires that were still smoldering.</t481> <t482>Someone searching for trinkets among the debris told Ursula that they had seen her son the night before, being taken away in a wheelbarrow with the snake man's cage in the middle of the gypsy caravan.</t482>
<t483>To her husband, who didn't care in the slightest about the boy's disappearance;</t483>
<t484>"The boy has turned into a gypsy!" Ursula exclaimed.</t484>
<t485>After being beaten a thousand times and reheated a thousand times, Jose Arcadio Buendia, who was pounding the mortar in the mortar, said, "I wish it were so. He would learn to be a man that way."</t485>
<t486>Ursula asked around to find out where the gypsies had gone.</t486><t487>With the hope of catching up to them, she set off on the indicated path and began walking, asking as she went.</t487><t488>She got farther and farther from the village, and eventually, thinking she had completely lost touch with the village, she dismissed the idea of returning.</t488><t489>At eight in the evening, after burying the prepared materials in warm manure to prevent them from freezing, Jose Arcadio Buendia entered the house, curious about what had happened to Amaranta, who had turned purple from crying, and noticed that Ursula was nowhere to be seen.</t489><t490>Within a few hours, he gathered a fully armed group, entrusted Amaranta to a woman who volunteered to nurse her, and set off on the invisible paths in search of Ursula.</t490><t491>Aureliano went with them.</t491><t492>Indian fishermen, whose language they did not understand, tried to explain through gestures that they had not seen anyone pass by.</t492><t493>After searching in vain for three days, they returned to the village.</t493>
<t494>Jose Arcadio Buendia agonized for weeks out of curiosity.</t494> <t495>He took care of Amaranta, washed and cleaned her, swaddled her, carried her to the wet nurse four times a day, and even sang lullabies at night that Ursula could never manage.</t495> <t496>At one point, Pilar Ternera volunteered to take care of the household chores until Ursula returned.</t496> <t497>Aureliano, whose instincts had become even sharper due to the disaster that had befallen them, immediately sensed upon seeing the woman that she was responsible for his brother's escape and his mother's subsequent disappearance. He greeted the suggestion so silently and sullenly that the woman never set foot in the house again.</t497>
<t498>Time set everything right.</t498> <t499>Jose Arcadio Buendia and his son found themselves at work without even realizing when they had returned to the laboratory, dusted off the place, set the still on the fire, and patiently started processing the materials that had been lying in manure for months.</t499> <t500>Even Amaranta, lying in her wicker basket, watched with curiosity as her father and brother worked diligently in the small room, which had become heavy with mercury vapors.</t500> <t501>Months after Ursula's departure, strange things began to happen.</t501> <t502>An empty bottle that had been forgotten in a corner of the cupboard for a long time suddenly became too heavy to lift.</t502> <t503>A pot of water on the workbench started boiling vigorously without any fire underneath it and continued to boil for half an hour until all the water had evaporated.</t503> <t504>Unable to explain these occurrences but attributing them to the maturation of the philosopher's stone, Jose Arcadio Buendia and his son watched what was happening with astonishment and excitement.</t504> <t505>One day, Amaranta's basket suddenly started moving, spinning around the room until Aureliano rushed in and caught it.</t505> <t506>But his father did not panic at all.</t506> <t507>Firmly believing that the miracle they had been waiting for was imminent, he put the basket back in its place and tied it securely to the leg of the table.</t507> <t508>It was after this incident that Aureliano heard these words from his father's mouth:</t508>
<t509>-If you do not fear God, at least let the miracle of the mines instill the fear of God in you.</t509>
<t510>Ursula suddenly reappeared about five months after her mysterious disappearance.</t510> <t511>She had undergone a transformation, becoming elevated, rejuvenated, and dressed in a manner unfamiliar to the villagers.</t511> <t512>When Jose Arcadio Buendia saw her like this in front of him, his knees gave way.</t512> <t513>That's it! he shouted, I knew this would happen.</t513> <t514>Indeed, while he had been secluded all that time preparing the materials for the alchemist's stone, he had prayed from the depths of his heart, yearning for the awaited miracle, not the discovery of the philosopher's stone or the release of the breath that would animate metals, or the ability to turn the hinges and locks in the house into gold, but for the event of that very moment to happen, for Ursula to return.</t514> <t515>But Ursula paid no attention to his excitement.</t515> <t516>After giving her husband a perfunctory kiss as if she had been gone for just an hour, she said, Look outside.</t516>
<t517>Jose Arcadio Buendia, when he went out into the street and saw the crowd, was unable to recover from his astonishment for a long time and couldn't figure out what was happening.</t517> <t518>These people were not gypsies.</t518> <t519>They were people with straight hair like theirs, dark-skinned, speaking the same language, complaining about the same things.</t519> <t520>They had mules loaded with food, carts filled with household and kitchen items, in short, the pure and simple goods sold without embellishment by traveling vendors within the daily reality.</t520> <t521>They came from a place just two days' journey beyond the swamp, where the mail arrived every month of the year and where people knew the blessings of life.</t521> <t522>Ursula had not been able to find the gypsies, but she had found the road that her husband, due to his obsession with great inventions, had failed to discover.</t522>
<t523>Pilar Ternera's son was brought to his grandfather's house two weeks after he was born.</t523><t524>Ursula reluctantly took the child into the house, yielding to her husband's insistence that a child of their own blood should not be left out in the cold, but on the condition that the child would not learn his true identity.</t524><t525>They named the boy Jose Arcadio, but to avoid confusion later, they started calling him simply Arcadio.</t525><t526>At that time, there was such activity in the village and such hustle and bustle in the house that the care of the children was relegated to the background.</t526><t527>The children were entrusted to a woman named Visitacion, one of the Guajiro Indians, who had come to the village with her brother, fleeing the insomnia plague that had ravaged their tribe for years.</t527><t528>The Indian siblings were so meek and so willing to be subservient that Ursula had taken them in to help with the household chores.</t528><t529>Thus, Arcadio and Amaranta began speaking the Guajiro language fluently before they even mastered Spanish, learning to eat lizard soup and spider eggs without Ursula's knowledge, who was absorbed in making and selling animal-shaped candies.</t529>
<t530>Macondo had changed.</t530> <t531>Those who came with Ursula spread the word so enthusiastically about how much better this place was compared to the swamp, praising its fertile soil, that the once isolated village transformed into a bustling town with shops, workshops, and a constantly busy caravan route.</t531> <t532>The first to arrive via this route were Arabs with baggy trousers and earrings, who bartered large parrots for glass beads.</t532> <t533>Jose Arcadio Buendia had no time to scratch his head.</t533> <t534>Enchanted by the tangible reality that seemed even more fantastical than the boundless universe created by his imagination, Jose Arcadio Buendia stopped paying attention to his alchemy laboratory, set aside the materials he had painstakingly prepared over months, and became as proactive as he was in the days when he decided where the streets would lead and where the houses would be built to ensure no one was wronged.</t534> <t535>He established such dominance over the newcomers that no foundation was laid, nor any wall built without consulting him, and eventually, they decided to put him in charge of land distribution.</t535>
<t536>When the acrobat gypsies, who had turned the old travelers' fair into a huge casino of fortune and gambling games, returned, they were greeted with great fanfare, thinking that Jose Arcadio had returned with them.</t536> <t537>However, Jose Arcadio did not return, and according to Ursula, the snake man, the only person who could tell where her son was, was also not there.</t537> <t538>As a result, the gypsies were not allowed to stay in the town because they were said to spread lust and perversion wherever they went, and they were warned never to set foot there again.</t538> <t539>However, Jose Arcadio Buendia did not forget to say that the doors of the town were always open to the tribe of Melquiades, who had greatly contributed to the development of the town with his infinite wisdom and unique inventions.</t539> <t540>Nevertheless, according to what the nomads said, Melquiades' tribe had been wiped off the face of the earth because it had surpassed the limits of human comprehension.</t540>
