<t541>At least for now, freed from the torment of his imagination, Jose Arcadio Buendia established such an order and work system in a short time that only one thing was free: the permission to release the birds, which had been bringing joy to life with their chirping since the founding days of the village, and replace them with a clock in every house.</t541> <t542>These were wooden clocks traded by the Arabs for parrots.</t542> <t543>Jose Arcadio Buendia set the clocks so perfectly that the town was enlivened every half hour by the successive chorus of the same tune, and when noon arrived, the sounds of a waltz rising uninterruptedly and in unison filled everywhere.</t543>
<t544>It was also Jose Arcadio Buendia who decided to plant almond trees instead of acacias in the streets during those years and found a way to make the almonds immortal without ever revealing his secret.</t544> <t545>Years later, when Macondo was filled with wooden houses with zinc roofs, the almond trees, with their broken and dusty branches, stood in the oldest streets, long forgotten who had planted them.</t545> <t546>While his father was busy organizing the town and his mother was making money from the business of distributing rooster and fish-shaped candies attached to sandalwood sticks twice a day, Aureliano would lock himself in the abandoned laboratory for hours, teaching himself the art of silverwork.</t546> <t547>He had grown so quickly that, soon after, when his older brother's clothes no longer fit him, he started wearing his father's, but since he was not as stocky as the others, Visitacion would pleat the shirts and put darts in the pants.</t547>
<t548>Puberty had taken away the softness of his voice, making the young man silent and introverted, but at the same time, it had restored the intense expression in his eyes that he had at birth.</t548><t549>He had devoted himself to silverwork so much that he rarely left the laboratory, even to eat.</t549><t550>Thinking that he might need a woman, Jose Arcadio Buendia gave him the keys to the house and some money.</t550><t551>But Aureliano used the money to buy hydrochloric acid to make a solution for melting gold and decorated the keys with gold plating.</t551><t552>Aureliano's excesses paled in comparison to what Amaranta and Arcadio did, who, despite starting to grow their seven-year molars, still clung to the Indian woman's skirt and stubbornly spoke in the Guajiro language instead of Spanish.</t552><t553>Ursula would say to her husband, "You have no right to complain; they take after their lineage, what can you do."</t553><t554>While Ursula cursed her misfortune, believing that her children's wildness was as terrible as a pig's tail, Aureliano gave her such a look that she froze, not knowing what to do.</t554>
<t555>"Someone is coming," said the young man.</t555>
<t556>Ursula, as she did with every prophecy of her son, tried to interpret this one with the logic of a housewife.</t556> <t557>It could happen, someone might come.</t557> <t558>Every day, a bunch of strangers came to Macondo, none of whom aroused suspicion or became the subject of prophecy.</t558> <t559>However, Aureliano was too convinced of the accuracy of his prophecy to heed this logic.</t559>
<t560>"I don't know who it is," he said, "but whoever it is, they have already set off."</t560>
<t561>Indeed, that Sunday arrived, Rebeca.</t561> <t562>She was only eleven years old.</t562> <t563>She had made a strenuous journey from Manaure to Macondo with leather merchants who had taken on the task of bringing the girl along with a letter addressed to Jose Arcadio Buendia, but who could not for the life of them remember who had entrusted the child to them.</t563> <t564>All her belongings consisted of a small trunk, a rocking chair decorated with tiny hand-painted flowers, and a sack that made a rattling noise with every swing, containing the bones of her mother and father.</t564> <t565>The letter addressed to Jose Arcadio was written with extremely heartfelt words by someone who still loved and respected him despite the long time and distance that had come between their friendship.</t565> <t566>The letter writer, being the daughter of Ursula's cousin's cousin and that unforgettable friend Nicanor Ulloa and his dear wife Rebeca Montiel, and thus distantly related to Jose Arcadio Buendia, wrote that by sending this homeless orphan girl to him, they believed they were fulfilling a humanitarian duty, and noted that they had placed the bones of the girl's parents in the sack so they could be buried according to Christian rites.</t566>
<t567>The names mentioned in the letter, as well as the signature at the bottom, were clearly legible, but neither Jose Arcadio Buendia nor Ursula could figure out if they had a relative with that name.</t567> <t568>They also did not recognize the name of the sender.</t568> <t569>The village called Manaure had never reached their ears.</t569> <t570>It was not possible to learn anything from the girl either.</t570> <t571>As soon as the girl arrived, she sat in her rocking chair, sucking her finger, and began to watch everyone with her big, bewildered eyes, showing no sign that she understood the questions being asked.</t571> <t572>She wore an old, tattered dress that had once been striped but was later dyed black, and shoes with peeling patent leather.</t572> <t573>Her hair was gathered behind her ears with two black ribbons.</t573> <t574>She had on a sweat-faded cotton shirt and a copper-framed amulet made from a carnivorous animal's tooth on her right arm.</t574> <t575>Her skin, tinged with green, and her drum-tight, swollen belly were signs of an illness and poverty that had begun before she was born, but when food was placed in front of her, she simply held the plate on her knees without taking a single bite.</t575>
<t576>They almost believed that the girl was deaf and mute, but finally, the Indians asked her in their language if she wanted water, and the girl blinked and nodded as if she recognized them, saying yes.</t576> <t577>Since they had no other option, they kept the girl at home.</t577> <t578>Aureliano, trying to guess her name, patiently listed all the saints' names, but when he got no reaction from the girl, he finally called her by the name mentioned in the letter, and they called her Rebeca.</t578> <t579>Since no one had died yet and there was no cemetery in Macondo, they left the sack of bones in the open to find a suitable place to bury it.</t579>
<t580>The sack kept getting caught on people's feet and, with a clattering sound similar to a hen clucking on its nest, would appear in the most unexpected places.</t580> <t581>It took Rebeca a very long time to adapt to family life.</t581> <t582>She would go to the farthest corners of the house, sucking her thumb while sitting in her rocking chair.</t582> <t583>She was interested in nothing but the chiming of the clock, and every half hour, she would nervously dart her eyes around as if trying to catch the sound in the air.</t583> <t584>For several days, they couldn't get her to eat a single bite.</t584> <t585>While they wondered how she hadn't died of starvation yet, the Indians, who silently roamed the house and knew everything that was going on, discovered that Rebeca was eating the damp earth from the yard and pieces of lime she scraped off the walls with her nails.</t585> <t586>Judging by the fact that she did this secretly and with a sense of guilt, hiding her stash to eat when no one was around, her parents or whoever raised her must have scolded her a lot for this habit.</t586> <t587>To break her of this bad habit, they spread cow manure in the yard and rubbed red pepper on the walls, but the girl showed such cunning and skill in finding earth that Ursula had to take even stricter measures.</t587>
<t588>After placing the orange juice and rhubarb root in a pot and leaving it out in the frost all night, she made the girl drink it on an empty stomach the next morning.</t588> <t589>Although no one had said that this remedy would cure the habit of eating dirt, Ursula figured that if something bitter entered an empty stomach, the liver would react to it.</t589> <t590>Rebeca was so rebellious and, despite her frailty, so strong that even though they tied her hands and feet like a calf to make her swallow the medicine, she kept kicking, cursing in her native language with the most unmentionable words according to the Indians, and biting and spitting on anyone who came near her.</t590> <t591>When Ursula learned of this, she added the whip to her treatment method.</t591> <t592>Whether it was the rhubarb laxative, the beating, or both, it is unknown, but within a few weeks, Rebeca began to show signs of improvement.</t592> <t593>She joined the games of Arcadio and Amaranta, who saw her as a sister, and ate heartily, using the knife and fork properly.</t593> <t594>It was also soon understood that she spoke Spanish as well as the Indians' language, was very skilled in handicrafts, and accompanied the clock's chime with her own nonsensical words.</t594>
<t595>The household soon began to see her as one of the family.</t595> <t596>She would cuddle up to Ursula more than her own children, call Arcadio and Amaranta her siblings, Aureliano her uncle, and Jose Arcadio Buendia her grandfather.</t596> <t597>Thus, in the end, she earned the right to take the name Rebeca Buendia like the other children, and she carried this name with honor until the end of her life.</t597>
<t598>At the time when Rebeca overcame her habit of eating dirt and started sleeping in the same room with the other children, one night, the Indian woman who slept in the same room woke up and heard a strange clattering noise coming from the corner.</t598> <t599>Thinking that an animal had entered the room, she jumped up in a panic and saw Rebeca sitting in a rocking chair with her finger in her mouth, her eyes shining like a cat's in the dark.</t599> <t600>Visitacion, who had fallen victim to blind fate, saw with horror the signs of the disease that had once forced them to flee from the century-old kingdom where they were prince and princess, in Rebeca's eyes.</t600> <t601>It was the disease of insomnia.</t601>
<t602>The next morning, they saw that the Indian Cataure had left the house.</t602><t603>His sister did not go with him because a voice inside her said that even if he went to the other end of the world, this deadly disease would not leave him alone.</t603><t604>No one could understand Visitacion's panic.</t604><t605>Jose Arcadio Buendia, making a joke of it, said, The less we sleep, the better, that way we get more out of life.</t605><t606>But the Indian woman explained at length that the most frightening aspect of the disease was not the sleeplessness, as it did not cause fatigue, but that it gradually became worse and led to memory loss.</t606><t607>According to her, once someone got used to the sleeplessness, they first forgot memories from their childhood, then they gradually forgot the names and uses of objects, eventually they did not recognize people, forgot themselves, and fell into a void of memory without a past.</t607><t608>Jose Arcadio Buendia, thinking it was one of the diseases stemming from Indian superstitions, laughed heartily.</t608><t609>However, Ursula, just in case, separated Rebeca from the other children.</t609>
<t610>A few weeks later, just as Visitacion was beginning to settle down one night, Jose Arcadio Buendia, unable to sleep, started tossing and turning in bed.</t610> <t611>Ursula was also awake, and when she asked her husband, "What's wrong?" Jose Arcadio Buendia replied, "Prudencio Aguilar is on my mind again."</t611> <t612>They didn't close their eyes for a minute that night, but the next day, since they didn't feel any discomfort, they forgot what had happened during the night.</t612> <t613>At lunch, Aureliano, who had stayed up all night in the laboratory to dip the needle he was going to give Ursula as a birthday present in gold, explained with surprise that he didn't feel tired at all.</t613> <t614>They didn't panic until the third day, when it was time to go to bed, and they realized that no one was sleepy and that they hadn't slept for more than fifty hours.</t614>
<t615>The Native American woman, with her resigned attitude towards fate, said, "The children didn't sleep either."</t615> <t616>Once illness enters a house, no one can escape it.</t616> <t617>Indeed, they had fallen victim to the affliction of insomnia.</t617> <t618>Ursula, who had learned the healing properties of plants from her mother, immediately brewed some aconite syrup and gave it to everyone to drink, but none of them could sleep, and they spent the evening daydreaming while standing.</t618> <t619>In this hallucinatory wakefulness, they not only saw the people in their own dreams but also those who appeared in others' dreams.</t619> <t620>It was as if a crowd of guests had filled the house.</t620> <t621>Rebeca, sitting in the rocking chair in the corner of the kitchen, saw a man who looked very much like her, dressed in white linens, bringing a bouquet of roses from under the top button of his shirt.</t621>
<t622>The woman next to the man pulled a rose from the bouquet with her elegant hands and placed it in the child's hair.</t622><t623>Ursula deduced that the man and woman were Rebeca's parents, but despite her efforts to recall where she knew them from, she ultimately concluded that she had never seen them in her life.</t623><t624>While all this was happening, Jose Arcadio Buendia, having been careless and not taken precautions, allowed the animal-shaped sweets made at home to continue being sold in the town.</t624><t625>Everyone from seven to seventy was sucking on those irresistible green roosters infected with insomnia, those beautiful pink fish soaked in insomnia, and those sweet little yellow foals dipped in insomnia.</t625><t626>Thus, the sun that rose on Monday morning found the entire town awake.</t626><t627>At first, no one panicked.</t627><t628>On the contrary, they were happy not to sleep, because at that time there was so much work to be done in Macondo that no one had time to keep up.</t628><t629>They worked so much that soon they were crossing their arms and counting the clock's chimes at three in the morning.</t629><t630>Those who wanted to sleep not out of fatigue but simply because they missed dreaming tried every method they could think of to exhaust themselves and fall asleep.</t630><t631>They gathered together and engaged in endless conversations, repeated the same jokes for hours on end, and stretched out the barren rooster story as much as they could.</t631>
<t632>It was a tale that stretched on and on, the storyteller would ask, "Shall I tell you the tale of the barren rooster?" If they said yes, he would say, "I didn't ask you to say yes, I asked if you wanted me to tell the tale of the barren rooster."</t632> <t633>If the listeners said no, the storyteller would say, "I didn't ask you to say no, I asked if you wanted the tale of the barren rooster," and he would get out of it that way.</t633> <t634>If no one made a sound, the storyteller would say, "I didn't ask you to be silent, I asked if you wanted me to tell the tale of the barren rooster," and he would start all over again.</t634> <t635>If someone tried to leave, the storyteller would say, "Did I tell you to leave? I asked if you wanted the tale of the barren rooster," and he would make them sit down, and thus the tale would go on and on, night after night, without end.</t635>
<t636>When Jose Arcadio Buendia realized that the illness had spread throughout the entire town, he gathered the heads of the families and told them what he knew about the insomnia plague. In the end, they decided to take measures to prevent the disease from spreading to other towns in the swamp.</t636> <t637>They took the bells that the Arabs had given in exchange for parrots off the goats' necks and placed them at the town's entrance gate for those who tried to enter the town without heeding the guards' advice and warnings to wear.</t637> <t638>In those days, strangers walking the streets of Macondo rang these bells so that those who were infected with the disease would understand that they were healthy and keep their distance.</t638> <t639>During their stay in Macondo, they were not allowed to eat or drink anything, because there was no doubt that the disease was transmitted through the mouth; insomnia had contaminated all food and drinks.</t639> <t640>Thus, they ensured that the disease remained within the town's borders.</t640> <t641>This quarantine was so effective that, over time, the extraordinary situation became ordinary, life took on a new order, work regained its old rhythm, and no one bothered about that unnecessary habit of sleep anymore.</t641>
<t642>Aureliano accidentally discovered the formula that would prevent memory loss for at least a few months.</t642> <t643>Since he was one of the first to contract the disease, he had become an expert in this field and had also achieved perfect mastery in the art of silver processing.</t643> <t644>One day, while looking for the small anvil he used to hammer metal foils, he couldn't remember its name.</t644> <t645>He asked his father, and his father said "Iskaça."</t645> <t646>Aureliano wrote this down on a piece of paper and stuck it to the handle of the anvil: Iskaça.</t646> <t647>This way, he wouldn't forget it again.</t647> <t648>Since the name of the thing was already peculiar, it didn't occur to him that this might be the first sign of memory loss.</t648> <t649>But a few days later, he realized that he could hardly remember the names of any of the objects in the laboratory.</t649> <t650>So, he wrote down the names of all of them and stuck the labels on; now, when he looked at them, he knew what they were.</t650>
<t651>When his father complained that he had come to forget even the most important memories of his childhood, Aureliano shared the method he had found with his father, and Jose Arcadio Buendia first applied it at home, then throughout the entire town.</t651> <t652>He dipped the brush in ink and wrote the name of everything on it: table, chair, clock, pot, wall, bed, pan.</t652>
<t653>He went to the pen and labeled every animal and plant there with tags like cow, goat, pig, chicken, cassava, caladium, and banana.</t653> <t654>As he studied how far memory loss could go over time, he realized that they could deduce the names of objects from the tags, but would forget what they were used for.</t654> <t655>Then he took it a step further.</t655> <t656>The note he hung around the cow's neck is concrete evidence of how prepared the people of Macondo were against memory loss: This is called a cow.</t656> <t657>It needs to be milked every morning so that it gives milk, and the milk must be boiled to mix with coffee to make café con leche.</t657> <t658>Thus, they began to live a reality that was momentarily grasped by names, but slipped away from their hands the moment they forgot what the written letters meant.</t658>
<t659>They placed a sign that read Macondo at the beginning of the road leading to the swamp.</t659> <t660>They hung a sign that read God Exists on the main street.</t660> <t661>In all the houses, writings were put up to remind people of objects and emotions.</t661> <t662>But this system required such great attention and strong nerves that most people succumbed to the enchantment of a self-invented, useless but comforting imaginary reality.</t662> <t663>Pilar Ternera, who once read the future by laying out cards, now played the biggest role in spreading this deception by claiming to read the past.</t663> <t664>In this way, those afflicted by the insomnia plague began to live in a world built on the uncertain possibilities of the cards.</t664> <t665>According to the cards, someone's father was the dark-skinned man who arrived at the beginning of April, someone's mother was the dark-skinned woman who wore a gold ring on her left hand, and someone's birthday was the last Tuesday when the skylark perched on the laurel branch and sang.</t665>
<t666>In the face of these means of distraction, Jose Arcadio Buendio, who had fallen into despondency, decided to create the memory machine he had once planned to make in order to remember the unique inventions of the gypsies.</t666> <t667>This marvel, created by human hands, was based on the idea of refreshing all the knowledge acquired throughout a lifetime every morning from start to finish.</t667> <t668>He was designing it in the form of a rotating dictionary.</t668> <t669>By turning the handle mounted on the axis of the dictionary, the wheel would spin, and the most essential information in a person's life would pass before their eyes within a few hours.</t669>
<t670>Just as he was finishing writing almost fourteen thousand articles, a strange-looking person appeared, pulling a black cloth-covered handcart with a tightly packed suitcase tied with a rope in his hand, and ringing the mournful bell of the sleepers, coming from the road out of the swamp.</t670> <t671>He went straight to Jose Arcadio Buendia's house.</t671> <t672>When Visitacion opened the door, she did not recognize him; at first, she thought he was a vendor unaware that nothing could be sold in a town irreversibly mired in forgetfulness.</t672> <t673>He was so old that he could barely move his hands and feet.</t673> <t674>Despite the tremor in his voice and the shakiness of his hands, it was still clear that he came from a world where people could still sleep and remember.</t674> <t675>Jose Arcadio Buendia found him sitting in the living room, fanning himself with his patched black hat while carefully reading the writings pasted on the walls.</t675> <t676>Thinking that perhaps he had once known him very well but could not remember now, he greeted the man with a great show of affection to avoid making a faux pas.</t676>
<t677>The visitor noticed this affectation.</t677> <t678>He realized that it was forgotten not with the irreparable forgetfulness of the heart, but with a much more relentless and irrevocable kind of forgetfulness.</t678> <t679>He knew this forgetfulness well, because it was the forgetfulness of death.</t679> <t680>It was then that he woke up.</t680> <t681>He opened the suitcase filled with nondescript objects and took out a box containing a bunch of bottles.</t681> <t682>As soon as he made Jose Arcadio Buendia drink a pale-colored liquid, his mind cleared up.</t682> <t683>Jose Arcadio Buendia's eyes filled with tears before he could realize he was in a strange living room where everything was labeled, feel ashamed of the nonsense written on the walls, recognize the visitor, and be overjoyed.</t683> <t684>The visitor was Melquiades.</t684>
<t685>While the people of Macondo were celebrating their regained memories, Jose Arcadio Buendia and Melquiades rekindled their dormant friendship.</t685> <t686>The gypsy intended to stay in the town.</t686> <t687>He had indeed gone to the other world, but had returned because he couldn't bear the loneliness.</t687> <t688>Rejected by his tribe, having lost his supernatural powers due to his attachment to life, he decided to retreat to this remote corner where death had not yet been discovered and dedicate himself to taking photographs on zinc plates.</t688> <t689>Jose Arcadio Buendia was unaware of this invention.</t689> <t690>But he was struck dumb with amazement when he saw himself and all his family members depicted forever on an iridescent metal plate.</t690> <t691>The photograph, which showed Jose Arcadio Buendia with his grizzled and spiky hair, his shirt fastened with a cardboard collar held by a copper button, and that bewildered seriousness that Ursula mocked by saying he looked like a frightened general, dated back to that time.</t691> <t692>On that clear December morning when the photograph was taken, Jose Arcadio Buendia, thinking that while everyone else would slowly fade away, he would remain forever on a metal plate, was genuinely scared.</t692> <t693>This time, contrary to the usual, it was Ursula who calmed him down and erased this thought from his mind, deciding to let Melquiades stay in the house, having forgotten her old grudge, but she never agreed to have her photograph taken, as she (in her own words) did not want to become a source of amusement for her grandchildren.</t693>
<t694>That morning, she dressed the children in their holiday clothes, powdered their faces, and gave each of them a spoonful of marrow broth so they could stand still for about two minutes in front of Melquiades' incredible machine without moving a muscle.</t694> <t695>In that unique family photograph, Aureliano, dressed in black velvet, was standing between Amaranta and Rebeca.</t695> <t696>On his face was that reluctant expression, reflecting his clairvoyant sensitivity, which he would have years later when he faced the firing squad.</t696> <t697>But he had not yet foreseen what would happen to him.</t697> <t698>He was a master silversmith, renowned throughout the swampy region for the delicacy of his work.</t698> <t699>He worked silently in the workshop he shared with Melquiades.</t699> <t700>While his father and the gypsy, amidst the clinking of bottles and trays, the spilling acids, and the constant shimmer of silver bromide appearing and disappearing on the photographic plate, loudly tried to decipher Nostradamus' prophecies, Aureliano seemed to escape and take refuge in another time.</t700> <t701>Due to his dedication and meticulousness in his work, Aureliano soon earned more money than Ursula did from her sweets, but everyone found it strange that, despite being a grown man, he had no relationships with any women.</t701>
<t702>Indeed, he had not yet touched a woman's hand.</t702> <t703>A few months later, Francisco the Devil-Breaker showed up.</t703> <t704>This two-hundred-year-old vagabond would come and go to Macondo every now and then, spreading the folk songs he wrote and composed himself.</t704> <t705>In these songs, Francisco the Devil-Breaker would recount the events that happened in the towns along the road from Manaure to the edge of the swamp.</t705> <t706>Therefore, anyone who wanted to convey a message or announce something from one town to another would slip two coins into Francisco's palm, and he would include the news in his song.</t706> <t707>Listening to these songs in the hope of hearing news from her son Jose Arcadio, Ursula learned of her mother's death in this way.</t707> <t708>Francisco, who became famous as Francisco the Devil-Breaker for defeating the devil in an impromptu folk song contest and whose real name no one knew, had slipped away from Macondo during the insomnia plague.</t708> <t709>One night, just as suddenly as he had left, he appeared in Catarino's shop.</t709>
<t710>When he arrived this time, there was a woman with him who was so fat that she couldn't lift herself up and walk.</t710> <t711>Four Native Americans were carrying the woman from place to place with a rocking chair that they could barely move, and a melancholy-faced young mestizo girl was staying by the woman's side with an umbrella to keep the sun off her head.</t711> <t712>That night, Aureliano went to Cacarino's shop.</t712> <t713>Francisco was sitting in the middle of a circle of listeners, like a chameleon carved from a single piece of stone, playing that same old accordion given to him by Sir Walter Raleigh in Guiana, tapping the rhythm with his feet, which were cracked from saltpeter and accustomed to stomping, and singing news with his familiar, raspy voice.</t713>
<t714>In front of the large door at the back, where people were constantly coming and going, the owner of the rocking chair sat silently, fanning herself.</t714> <t715>Catarino, with an artificial rose tucked behind his ear, was selling pitcher after pitcher of sugarcane juice to the gathered crowd, while also taking the opportunity to grope the men here and there.</t715> <t716>By midnight, the heat became unbearable.</t716> <t717>Aureliano listened to the news until the end, but heard nothing that concerned his family.</t717> <t718>Just as he was about to head home, a fat woman gestured and called him over.</t718>
<t719>"Why don't you come inside too," he said.</t719> <t720>"It's only twenty cents."</t720>
<t721>Aureliano, without understanding why, threw the money into the bowl on the woman's lap and entered the room.</t721> <t722>The young mestizo girl was lying completely naked with tiny dog-like breasts.</t722> <t723>That night, sixty-three men had been with her before Aureliano.</t723> <t724>The air in the room had started to thicken with sweat and breath, becoming almost muddy.</t724> <t725>The girl pulled the soaking sheet and handed one end to Aureliano.</t725> <t726>The sheet had become as heavy as a sailcloth.</t726> <t727>They twisted it from both ends, wringing out the water until it returned to its normal weight.</t727> <t728>They flipped the mattress over, but the sweat had soaked through to the other side as well.</t728> <t729>Aureliano was hoping these tasks would never end.</t729>
<t730>Although he knew about love games from hearsay, his legs were so weak that he could barely stand, and despite being in his adolescence, his face was burning like fire and he was almost about to wet himself out of fear.</t730> <t731>When the girl made the bed and told him to undress, Aureliano started to stammer: They forced me in here.</t731> <t732>They said, throw twenty cents into the bowl and be quick about it.</t732> <t733>The girl understood his confusion.</t733> <t734>In a soft voice, she said, if you throw another twenty cents when you leave, you can stay inside longer.</t734> <t735>Aureliano, comparing his own naked state with his brother's, undressed, sinking into the ground with shame.</t735> <t736>No matter how much effort the girl put in, Aureliano became increasingly indifferent, increasingly withdrawn.</t736> <t737>In a miserable voice, he muttered, I'll throw in another twenty cents.</t737> <t738>The girl thanked him quietly.</t738> <t739>Her back was peeling from lying down so much.</t739> <t740>Her skin clung to her ribs, and she was breathless from deadly exhaustion.</t740>
<t741>Two years ago, in a place far away, he had fallen asleep without extinguishing the candle and woke up surrounded by flames.</t741> <t742>The house he lived in with his grandmother had burned down to ashes.</t742> <t743>After that, his grandmother started taking him from town to town, and to make up for the burned house, she had him sleep with anyone who could pay twenty cents.</t743> <t744>According to the girl's calculations, she needed to sleep with seventy people a night for another ten years, because she had to cover the travel and food expenses not only for herself and her grandmother but also for the Indians carrying the rocking chair.</t744> <t745>When Mama knocked on the door for the second time, Aureliano, unable to accomplish anything, left the room on the verge of tears.</t745>
<t746>That night, he spent thinking about the girl, on one hand with desire, on the other with pity, until morning.</t746> <t747>He felt an irresistible urge to love and protect her.</t747> <t748>At dawn, exhausted from sleeplessness and the fire of desire, he made his decision: He would marry her to save her from her tyrannical grandmother and to savor those fulfilling nights shared by seventy men alone.</t748> <t749>However, when he arrived at Catarino's shop at ten in the morning, the girl had already left town.</t749>
<t750>As time extinguished this mad desire to marry, it also increased his sense of emptiness.</t750> <t751>He threw himself into his work with all his might.</t751> <t752>To hide the shame of his uselessness, he vowed to remain without a woman for life.</t752> <t753>Meanwhile, Melquiades had printed everything in Macondo that could be photographed onto zinc plates and had left the photo lab to Jose Arcadio Buendia, who had decided to use it to scientifically prove the existence of God.</t753> <t754>Jose Arcadio Buendia was convinced that by taking overlapping photographs in different parts of the house, he would eventually capture an image of God, if He existed, or put an end to the claims about God's existence.</t754>
<t755>Melquiades devoted himself entirely to interpreting Nostradamus's prophecies.</t755> <t756>He would sit until late hours, gasping for breath in his faded velvet vest, scribbling with his sparrow-like hands adorned with rings that had lost their former brilliance.</t756> <t757>One night, he thought he had found a prophecy foretelling the future of Macondo.</t757>
<t758>Macondo will be a town full of glittering large houses made of glass, and there will be no trace left of the Buendia lineage.</t758> <t759>Jose Arcadio Buendia roared, "That's wrong, the houses will be made of ice as I saw in my dream, not glass, and the Buendia lineage will endure as long as the world exists, per omnia secula seculorum."</t759>
<t760>Ursula was doing her best to maintain common sense in this house full of excesses.</t760> <t761>Meanwhile, she had expanded her candy-making business, establishing a large bakery.</t761> <t762>The bakery worked all night, producing baskets of bread, buns, and cookies, and the goods from the oven were transported and sold out within hours along the roads winding through the swamp.</t762> <t763>Ursula had reached the age where she could sit back and rest, yet she was working harder with each passing day.</t763> <t764>She was so engrossed in her money-making ventures that one day, while adding sugar to the dough with the Indian woman, her eyes drifted to the garden, where she saw two beautiful girls she didn't recognize, sitting under the sun and working on embroidery.</t764>
<t765>These were Rebeca and Amaranta.</t765> <t766>As soon as they discarded the mourning clothes they had stubbornly worn for three years after their grandmother's death, the colorful clothes they wore seemed to have earned them a whole new place in the world.</t766> <t767>Contrary to expectations, the more beautiful of the girls was Rebeca.</t767> <t768>She was a fair-skinned girl with large, calming eyes, and skillful hands that seemed to embroider patterns with invisible threads.</t768>
<t769>The younger Amaranta was a bit rough, but she had inherited her late grandmother's natural charm and resilience.</t769> <t770>Although it was clear she would be as tall and imposing as her father, Arcadio looked like a child next to them.</t770> <t771>Arcadio had learned to read and write from Aureliano and was now trying to master silverwork.</t771> <t772>It suddenly dawned on Úrsula that the house was filled with grown-ups, and she realized that her children were almost old enough to get married and start their own families, and that they would scatter due to the lack of space in the house.</t772> <t773>So, she took out the money she had saved up over the years, collected a bit more from her customers, and set about expanding the house.</t773> <t774>She added a guest room for occasional visitors, a daily sitting room, a dining room with a table that could seat twelve people, nine bedrooms overlooking the garden, and a terrace that would be shaded by the rose garden in the afternoon heat, with ferns and begonia pots lined up along its railings. She also enlarged the kitchen to fit two stoves.</t774> <t775>The pantry where Pilar Ternera had read José Arcadio's fortune was demolished and replaced with a new pantry twice its size to ensure there would never be a shortage of food in the house.</t775>
<t776>Ursula had two outhouses built under the shade of the chestnut tree in the garden, one for women and the other for men, a large barn in the back garden, a chicken coop surrounded by wire mesh, a shed for the milk-giving cows, and an aviary open to the wind from all sides so that the passing birds could perch as they pleased.</t776> <t777>Just like her husband, who seemed to be in a feverish delirium, Ursula would follow with a troop of masons and carpenters, deciding where the light and heat would come from, and without worrying about keeping the job small, she was creating a spacious place.</t777> <t778>The primitive structure built by the village founders was filled with tools, materials, and workers who, tired of the clattering bone sack that followed them wherever they went, would tell anyone who would listen not to be a hindrance.</t778> <t779>In the chaos where the smell of quicklime and tar filled the entire town and everyone's lungs, no one noticed how not only the largest structure in the town but also the coolest house ever built in the swampy region, with its doors always open to guests, suddenly rose as if it had sprouted from the ground.</t779>
<t780>In the chaos, Jose Arcadio Buendia, who tried to catch God off guard and take His picture, was the person who understood the least about how the house was built and completed.</t780> <t781>As the house neared completion, Ursula informed her husband, pulling him out of his dream world, that she had received orders to paint the facade blue instead of white as they wanted.</t781> <t782>She showed the official document.</t782> <t783>Not understanding what his wife was talking about, Jose Arcadio Buendia read the signature at the bottom of the paper.</t783>
<t784>"Who is this man?" he asked.</t784>
<t785>-He was a justice of the peace, Ursula replied with sadness.</t785>
<t786>-He was an official sent by the government.</t786>
<t787>The magistrate Don Apolinar Moscote had arrived in Macondo quietly and unobtrusively.</t787> <t788>He stayed at the Jacob Hotel, built by one of the first Arabs who bartered parrots and knick-knacks, and the next day rented a one-room house with a door opening onto the street, two blocks away from the Buendia's house.</t788> <t789>He placed a table and a chair he got from Jacob in the room, hung the state emblem he had brought with him on the wall, and wrote "Magistrate" on the door.</t789> <t790>The first order he issued was that all houses be painted blue in celebration of the anniversary of national independence.</t790> <t791>Jose Arcadio Buendia, who grabbed the decree and rushed to the magistrate's house, found him lying in a hammock he had set up in the room, taking a nap.</t791> <t792>Did you write this? he asked.</t792> <t793>Don Apolinar Moscote, a middle-aged, red-faced, timid man, replied, Yes.</t793> <t794>On what authority? Jose Arcadio Buendia pressed.</t794> <t795>Don Apolinar Moscote took a paper out of the desk drawer and showed it.</t795> <t796>I was appointed as the magistrate of this town.</t796> <t797>Jose Arcadio Buendia didn't even look at the appointment document.</t797> <t798>Without losing his composure, he said, In this town, we don't issue orders on written paper.</t798> <t799>And get this straight, we don't need a magistrate, because we never have any need for one.</t799>
<t800>Standing in front of Don Apolinar Moscote without raising his voice, he explained one by one how they founded the village, how they distributed the land, how they opened the roads, and how they did everything necessary without bothering the government and without allowing anyone to disturb them.</t800> <t801>"We live in such peace that not even one of us has met their end yet," he said.</t801> <t802>"As you can see, we don't even have a cemetery."</t802> <t803>No one was upset because they didn't receive help from the government.</t803> <t804>On the contrary, everyone was pleased with their situation because the government hadn't disturbed their peace so far, and they wanted it to continue this way; they hadn't founded this town for any upstart to come and give orders.</t804>
<t805>While he was explaining these things, Don Apolinar Moscote, without losing his grace for a moment, had put on his jacket made of the same white cotton as his trousers.</t805> <t806>Jose Arcadio Buendia finished his words by saying, "If you intend to sit here like one of us, you are more than welcome, but if you are going to cause trouble by painting people's houses blue, you better pack up and go back to where you came from."</t806> <t807>"Because my house will be as white as a dove."</t807>
<t808>Don Apolinar Moscote turned pale.</t808> <t809>He took a step back, clenched his teeth, and spoke with a somewhat sorrowful demeanor:</t809>
<t810>-Know that I am armed.</t810>
<t811>Jose Arcadio Buendia, without understanding how his hands, which once knocked down horses with a single blow, regained the same strength, grabbed Don Apolinar Moscote by the collar, lifted him into the air, and held him at eye level.</t811>
<t812>"I am doing this because I prefer to carry you around alive rather than dead for the rest of my life," he said.</t812>
<t813>Then he grabbed the man by his collar, lifted him into the air, and carried him out to the street, taking him all the way to the start of the swamp road before setting him down on his feet.</t813> <t814>A week later, the man returned, accompanied by six barefoot, bald-headed soldiers armed with shotguns, and with his wife and seven daughters crammed into an ox cart.</t814> <t815>Two more carts loaded with household items, suitcases, and kitchen utensils followed.</t815> <t816>Don Apolinar Moscote settled his family into the Jacob Hotel and began searching for a house.</t816>
<t817>In the meantime, he reopened his office under the protection of the soldiers.</t817> <t818>The founders of Macondo, determined to drive out the invaders from the town, took their eldest sons with them and went to Jose Arcadio Buendia, saying they were at his command.</t818> <t819>But Jose Arcadio Buendia objected, saying that humiliating a man in front of his family was not manly.</t819> <t820>He explained that Don Apolinar had brought his wife and daughters with him.</t820> <t821>Therefore, he decided to resolve the matter amicably.</t821>
<t822>Aureliano went with him.</t822> <t823>At that time, he had just started to grow out the black mustache he used to twist with paraffin at the ends, and his voice, which was one of his greatest assets on the battlefield, was approaching a thunderous boom.</t823> <t824>Without even taking a pocketknife with them and paying no heed to the soldiers at the door, they burst into the magistrate's office.</t824> <t825>Don Apolinar Moscote did not lose his composure.</t825> <t826>He introduced them to his two daughters who were in the office at the time: sixteen-year-old Amparo, who was dark like her mother, and nine-year-old Remedios, who had lily-white skin and beautiful green eyes.</t826> <t827>The girls were graceful and well-mannered.</t827> <t828>As soon as the men entered, without waiting to be introduced, they ran to fetch chairs.</t828> <t829>But they themselves remained standing.</t829>
<t830>Jose Arcadio Buendia said, "Alright, my friend, you can stay here, but don't think that we are afraid of the armed bandits you posted at the door. We are keeping quiet out of respect for your wife and daughters."</t830>
<t831>Don Apolinar Moscote was taken aback, but Jose Arcadio Buendia did not give him a chance to speak.</t831> <t832>We have only two conditions, he continued, first, everyone will paint their house in the color they want.</t832> <t833>Second, the soldiers will leave immediately.</t833> <t834>We will ensure public order.</t834>
<t835>The magistrate raised his right hand, spreading all five fingers.</t835>
<t836>-Word of honor?</t836>
<t837>Jose Arcadio Buendia said, "Enemy words."</t837> <t838>And he added in a bitter tone: "Because never forget this: You and I are enemies."</t838>
<t839>The soldiers left before evening.</t839> <t840>A few days later, Jose Arcadio Buendia found a house for the judge's family.</t840> <t841>Everyone except Aureliano was content with their life.</t841> <t842>The thought of Remedios, the judge's young daughter who was almost young enough to be his own daughter, had left an unending ache in his heart.</t842> <t843>This was a tangible pain, like having a stone in your shoe that hurts as you walk.</t843>
<t844>The completion of the new house, as white as a dove's wing, was celebrated with a dance party.</t844> <t845>Ursula had thought of doing this on the day she first noticed that Hebeca and Amaranta had grown up and blossomed.</t845> <t846>The reconstruction of the house was actually intended to properly accommodate the suitors who would come and go for the girls.</t846> <t847>So that nothing would be lacking, she gathered her skirt at her waist and worked hard like a laborer in the house's renovation.</t847> <t848>Even before the craftsmen had finished their work, Ursula spared no expense to furnish the house from top to bottom, ordering a heap of items, bringing in tableware, and acquiring a new invention that left the entire village in awe and thrilled the young people: the Laterna.</t848> <t849>They brought the Laterna, disassembled and packed into several crates, along with the other items.</t849> <t850>The Viennese-style living room set, Bohemian crystals, tableware from the Indies company, Dutch-made tablecloths, and a host of candelabras, chandeliers, curtains, and covers entered the house along with the Laterna: The import company had also sent the Italian expert Pietro Crespi as a courtesy to assemble the Laterna, tune it, demonstrate how to use it, and teach how to dance to the latest fashionable music engraved on six paper strips.</t850>
<t851>Pietro Crespi was young and blonde.</t851> <t852>In Macondo, no man had ever been seen who was so handsome, so charming, and so well-mannered.</t852> <t853>He was also very particular about his attire.</t853> <t854>He never took off his embroidered waistcoat and dark jacket, even if he was melting from the heat and sweat.</t854> <t855>Maintaining a certain distance from the household and sweating profusely, he shut himself in the guest room for weeks, working with the same dedication that Aureliano had for his silver engravings.</t855> <t856>One morning, without opening the door or calling anyone to witness the miracle, he inserted the first strip into the lantern.</t856> <t857>The irritating sounds of hammers and saws suddenly ceased as those who were astonished by the orderly and smooth sound of the music dropped whatever they were holding.</t857> <t858>Everyone rushed to the living room.</t858>
<t859>Jose Arcadio Buendia was thunderstruck.</t859> <t860>His astonishment was not due to the beauty of the tune, but to the fact that the hurdy-gurdy operated on its own.</t860> <t861>Without wasting any time, Jose Arcadio set up Melquiades' camera in the hope of capturing a picture of the invisible hands playing this devilish invention.</t861> <t862>The Italian joined them at the table that day.</t862> <t863>Rebeca and Amaranta, who were serving at the table, watched in admiration as this angelic man used the cutlery with his ringless white hands with great finesse.</t863> <t864>In the sitting room adjacent to the parlor, Pietro Crespi was teaching them how to dance.</t864> <t865>He showed the girls how to move their feet without touching them, keeping time with a metronome under the friendly gaze of Ursula, who never left the room while they were taking lessons.</t865>
<t866>On the days they had lessons, Pietro Crespi wore special trousers made of tight and flexible fabric, and soft-soled shoes.</t866> <t867>Jose Arcadio Buendia would say to his wife, "Don't worry, dear."</t867> <t868>"This man is not a man, but a creature of the spirits."</t868> <t869>"He won't harm the girls."</t869> <t870>Nevertheless, Ursula remained cautious until the dance lessons ended and the Italian left Macondo.</t870> <t871>Then they began preparing for the party.</t871> <t872>Ursula made a list of those to be invited.</t872> <t873>Everyone descended from the founders of the village was invited to this party, except for the family of Pilar Ternera, who had recently given birth to two more children of unknown paternity: If friendship had not been the main criterion in the selection, there would have been no complaints about the invitation list.</t873> <t874>But those invited were not only the old friends of the Jose Arcadio Buendia family from before the great migration and the founding of Macondo; the sons and grandsons of these friends, who had been friends with Aureliano and Arcadio since childhood, and the daughters who embroidered with Rebeca and Amaranta were also included in the list.</t874> <t875>Even the name of kind-hearted Don Apolinar Moscote, who could do no more than support two policemen with wooden batons on his limited budget, was not mentioned.</t875>
<t876>To cover the household expenses, the girls had opened a tailor shop.</t876> <t877>At the same time, they were making and selling velvet flowers, guava pear sweets, and offering writing services for those who wanted love letters written.</t877> <t878>But no matter how much they were the most beautiful, most industrious, and most humble girls in the village, or how skillful they were in the new dances, they still couldn't manage to get invited to the party.</t878>
<t879>While Ursula and the girls were arranging the furniture that brought life back to the rooms crafted by the skillful hands of the builders, polishing the silver, and adorning the empty walls with beautiful girl pictures in boats filled with roses, Jose Arcadio Buendia, having firmly believed in God's absence and giving up on searching for Him, dismantled the lantern to uncover its magical secret.</t879> <t880>Two days before the party, even though he ended up with a bunch of extra screws, keys, and bolts, and the springs he wound in one direction unwound in another, he managed to reassemble the parts of the lantern.</t880> <t881>There had never been as much astonishment, hustle, and chaos as in those days.</t881> <t882>Still, the newly arrived chandeliers with arms were lit and prepared at the specified day and time of the invitation.</t882> <t883>The doors of the house, which still smelled of resin and whose whitewash had not yet dried, were opened, and the children and grandchildren of the village founders toured the terrace adorned with fern and begonia pots, the huge silent rooms, and the garden filled with the scent of roses.</t883> <t884>Then they gathered in the hall to see the unknown invention covered with a white sheet.</t884>
<t885>Those who had seen pianos in other villages of the marsh were disappointed when they saw this instrument that resembled a piano.</t885> <t886>But the disappointment of Ursula, who had set up the machine so that Amaranta and Rebeca could dance, was worse than all of theirs when the machine didn't work.</t886> <t887>Melquiades, who had become feeble and blind with age, tried to operate the machine using the skills of his infinite mind.</t887> <t888>Finally, while Jose Arcadio was fiddling with the organ grinder, he accidentally managed to set the jammed part in motion, and first a noise, then music in the form of mixed notes was heard.</t888> <t889>The keys striking the springs, which were tied backwards and not properly adjusted, eventually gave up.</t889> <t890>But the young people, descendants of the twenty-one brave souls who had dared to cross the mountains to the west in search of the sea, did not give up in front of this cacophony and the dance continued until morning.</t890>
<t891>Pietro Crespi came again to repair the lantern.</t891> <t892>Rebeca and Amaranta helped him with their smiles to arrange the springs and separate the tangled melodies.</t892> <t893>Their demeanor was so pleasant and innocent that eventually Ursula gave up waiting for them to come to their senses.</t893>
<t894>On the evening Pietro Crespi was to leave, a lantern party was organized in his honor, and Crespi demonstrated his mastery of modern dances with Rebeca.</t894> <t895>Arcadio and Amaranta were not falling behind them either.</t895> <t896>However, this beautiful show was interrupted; because Pilar Ternera, who was among those watching from the door, got into a hair-pulling fight with a woman who dared to say that Arcadio had a woman's buttocks.</t896> <t897>Towards midnight, Pietro Crespi bid farewell with a tearful speech, promising to return soon.</t897> <t898>Rebeca escorted him to the door.</t898> <t899>After closing the windows and doors and turning off the lights, she went up to her room and started crying.</t899> <t900>This endless crying, the reason for which even Amaranta did not know, lasted for days.</t900> <t901>Rebeca's introversion was not surprising.</t901> <t902>Despite appearing friendly and sincere, she was an introverted girl who never revealed what was in her heart to anyone.</t902>
<t903>Although she was a tall, sturdy-boned, healthy young girl, she could never give up the rocking chair she brought with her on her first arrival, which had been reinforced with nail upon nail for years and now had its arms broken off.</t903> <t904>Despite being a grown girl, no one knew that she hadn't given up the habit of sucking her finger.</t904> <t905>To hide this from everyone, she had made it a habit to lock herself in the bathroom frequently and to sleep with her face turned to the wall.</t905> <t906>On rainy afternoons, while sitting with her friends on the begonia-filled terrace doing embroidery, she would lose track of the conversation when her eyes fell on the mounds of earth raised by worms, and a tear loaded with homesickness would fall from the corner of her eye.</t906> <t907>The hidden tastes that had been conquered and forgotten years ago with oranges and jams surfaced with an irresistible force as soon as Rebeca began to cry.</t907> <t908>She started eating soil again.</t908> <t909>The first time, she ate the soil out of sheer curiosity because she saw disgust as the best remedy to prevent her mind from being swayed.</t909>
<t910>Indeed, the taste of soil in her mouth was disgusting at first.</t910> <t911>However, as curiosity gnawed at her insides, she too ate soil and gradually discovered the ancestral taste, savoring the pure flavors of basic nutrients.</t911> <t912>She filled her pockets with handfuls of soil; while teaching her friends the most difficult needlework, or talking about other men who weren't worth the sacrifice of eating wall plaster, she secretly ate soil with a passion mixed with pleasure and anger.</t912> <t913>When she ate soil, it felt as if the only person worth such degradation was closer, more tangible.</t913> <t914>The soil on which his delicate leather shoes stepped on the other side of the world, the mineral that left a bitter taste in her mouth after eating it, seemed to carry the warmth and density of his blood to Rebeca, giving her heart peace.</t914> <t915>One day, Amparo Moscote suddenly appeared and asked for permission to tour the house.</t915> <t916>Amaranta and Rebeca, stunned by this unexpected visit, welcomed Amparo with an icy formal demeanor.</t916> <t917>They showed her the repaired and renovated house, played the lantern, and offered orange marmalade with biscuits.</t917>
<t918>In the short time Ursula came to their side, Amparo quickly won her favor with her dignity, charm, and manners.</t918> <t919>After two hours had passed and a shortage of topics to talk about began, Amparo took advantage of Amaranta's distraction to slip a letter into Rebeca's hand.</t919> <t920>When Rebeca glanced at it, she saw the words "Dear Miss Rebeca Buendia" written in that pearl-like handwriting, with that green ink, and those meticulous words, explaining how to operate the lantern.</t920> <t921>She folded the letter with her fingertips, looked at Amparo Moscote with a gaze that promised eternal and unconditional gratitude and friendship until death, and tucked the letter into her chest.</t921>
<t922>The friendship that quickly solidified between Amparo Moscote and Rebeca Buendia rekindled Aureliano's hope.</t922> <t923>The memory of young Remedios had never ceased to haunt his mind, but Aureliano had not had the chance to see her.</t923> <t924>Whenever he went for a walk with his closest friends, Magnifico Visbal and Gerineldo Marquez—these young men shared the same names as their fathers, who were among the founders of the village—he would cast curious and furtive glances at the tailor's shop, only to see the girl's older sisters each time.</t924> <t925>The fact that Amparo Moscote had started coming and going from their house had kindled a spark of hope in his heart.</t925> <t926>She must come with her sister, he murmured to himself.</t926> <t927>She absolutely must come.</t927> <t928>As they say, if you say something forty times, it will come true, and Aureliano repeated this so earnestly and so tirelessly that one day, while he was trying to make a fish out of silver filigree, he suddenly felt as if the girl would come at any moment.</t928>
<t929>Indeed, shortly after, he heard that childish voice, and when he looked up and saw the girl standing at the door in her pink organza dress and white boots, his heart leapt to his throat.</t929>
<t930>Amparo Moscote, -You cannot enter Oraya.</t930> <t931>They are working, she was calling out from the hall.</t931>
<t932>Aureliano, without giving the girl a chance to respond, burst out.</t932> <t933>Holding the tiny fish by the chain coming out of its mouth, he said to the girl, Come inside.</t933>
<t934>Remedios approached and asked a lot of questions about the fish, which Aureliano couldn't answer because he was suddenly seized by an asthma attack.</t934> <t935>Aureliano thought, what more could he want than to spend the rest of his life beside this lily-skinned, emerald-eyed girl, who addressed him as "sir" with every question out of respect for her father.</t935> <t936>Melquiades was sitting in a corner, scribbling away with indecipherable codes.</t936> <t937>Aureliano suddenly hated him.</t937> <t938>All he could do was tell Remedios that he would give her the fish as a gift.</t938> <t939>The girl was so surprised and startled by this gift that she rushed outside the workshop.</t939> <t940>That day, Aureliano lost the patience he had while waiting for the days he eagerly watched for a chance to see Remedios.</t940> <t941>He neglected his work.</t941> <t942>As before, he waited with all his might, hoping the girl would come again, but Remedios did not appear.</t942>
<t943>He kept looking for the girl in his sisters' shop, at the window of their house, but he only found her in his imagination, which filled his unbearable loneliness.</t943> <t944>Aureliano would sit with Rebeca in the living room for hours, listening to the music box.</t944> <t945>Rebeca listened to the music box intently, because the music being played was the same that Pietro Crespi had taught them to dance to.</t945> <t946>Aureliano also listened to the music box intently, because everything, including the music, reminded him of Remedios.</t946>
<t947>The house was filled with love in every corner.</t947> <t948>Aureliano expressed his love with poems that had no beginning or end.</t948> <t949>He poured his love onto the coarse parchment papers given by Melquiades, wrote on his arms, scribbled poems on the bathroom walls, and in everything he wrote, Remedios appeared in various forms and sizes: In the heavy air at two in the afternoon that made everyone sleepy, there was Remedios; in the sweet scent of roses, Remedios; in the mystery of moths flocking to the light, Remedios; in the steam of freshly baked bread, again Remedios, always Remedios, everywhere Remedios.</t949> <t950>Rebeca would take her embroidery and sit by the window, waiting for her love to give a sign at four in the afternoon.</t950> <t951>She was well aware that the postman's mule came swaying every two weeks.</t951> <t952>Yet she always waited for him, believing that one day, unexpectedly, he would come.</t952>
<t953>But the exact opposite happened; one time, the mule did not appear on the usual day.</t953> <t954>Driven mad by despair, Rebeca rushed into the garden in the middle of the night, consumed by a desire to commit suicide, and ate handfuls of soil while crying out of pain and anger.</t954> <t955>She chewed on soft, slippery worms, crushing snail shells with her teeth.</t955> <t956>She vomited until dawn.</t956> <t957>Fever took over her head, she lost consciousness, and began to say whatever came to her mind without any sense of shame or sin.</t957> <t958>When Ursula heard the girl's ravings, she lost her mind, forced open Rebeca's chest, and at the bottom of the chest, she found exactly sixteen beautifully scented letters tied with pink ribbons, leaves and flowers hidden among old books, and dried butterflies that crumbled like dust upon touch.</t958>
<t959>Aureliano was the only person who could understand what such great despair meant.</t959> <t960>That afternoon, while Ursula was trying to bring Rebeca to her senses, Aureliano went to Catarino's shop with Magnifico Visbal and Gerineldo Marquez.</t960>
<t961>Additions were made to the shop over time, wooden rooms that smelled of faded flowers and were occupied by women with untied hair were added.</t961><t962>A tin jazz band consisting of an accordion and a drum was playing the songs of Devil-Breaker Francisco, who hadn't visited Macondo for a few years.</t962><t963>The three buddies got drunk on sugarcane juice.</t963><t964>Magnifico and Gerineldo, who were Aureliano's age but more experienced, had pulled a woman each onto their laps and were drinking continuously.</t964><t965>When the worn-out woman with gold teeth tried to caress Aureliano, he shivered from head to toe.</t965><t966>He pushed the woman away.</t966><t967>He realized that the more he drank, the more he remembered Remedios, but as he drank, he could better endure the pain brought by the memories.</t967><t968>He didn't know when his feet left the ground.</t968><t969>His friends and the women were flying in a bright void, floating in the air as if they had no weight.</t969><t970>They were uttering words that didn't come out of their mouths, making mysterious gestures that contradicted the expressions on their faces.</t970><t971>Catarino put his hand on his shoulder and said, "It's almost eleven o'clock."</t971><t972>Aureliano turned his head.</t972>
<t973>He saw Catarino's huge, misshapen face, the velvet flower tucked behind his ear, and the film broke.</t973> <t974>Just like during the period when they had sleeping sickness and forgot everything, everything in his mind was suddenly erased.</t974> <t975>Then he came to himself at an unknown hour in an unknown room.</t975> <t976>Pilar Ternera, in her undershirt, barefoot, her hair falling over her shoulders, stood there holding the lamp towards him, astonished in disbelief.</t976>
<t977>-Aureliano!</t977>
<t978>Aureliano gathered his feet and raised his head.</t978> <t979>He didn't know how he had come there, but he knew why he had come.</t979> <t980>Because he had nurtured and developed this passion deep in his heart since childhood.</t980>
<t981>-I came to sleep with you, she said.</t981>
<t982>His clothes were covered in mud and vomit.</t982> <t983>At that time, Pilar Ternera, who was living alone with her two small children, didn't ask him anything.</t983> <t984>She took Aureliano and brought him to bed.</t984> <t985>She wiped his face and eyes with a damp cloth, took off his clothes, then she herself stripped naked, and to prevent the children from seeing anything if they woke up, she lowered the mosquito net and got into bed.</t985> <t986>She was tired of waiting for a man who would stay for good, of men who left, and of countless men who lost their way to her house, and the inconsistency of the cards had confused her mind.</t986> <t987>During the years she spent waiting, her skin had wrinkled, her breasts had sagged, and the fire in her heart had extinguished.</t987> <t988>In the dark, she groped for Aureliano.</t988> <t989>She pressed her hand on his stomach and kissed his neck with maternal affection, murmuring, "Poor child."</t989> <t990>Aureliano shivered.</t990> <t991>With a silent, calm skillfulness, without making the slightest wrong move, he released all the pain that had accumulated inside him, and Remedios turned into an endless swamp smelling of wild animals and freshly ironed clothes within him.</t991>
<t992>Aureliano was crying when he surfaced from the mire.</t992> <t993>At first, he was sobbing uncontrollably, in short bursts.</t993> <t994>Then, as if something painful inside him had swollen and suddenly burst, he emptied out.</t994> <t995>The woman, scratching his head with her fingertips, patiently waited until he expelled the darkness consuming his life from his body.</t995> <t996>Then, she asked, "Who?"</t996> <t997>Aureliano told her who he was.</t997> <t998>Pilar Ternera, with her usual laughter that always startled the pigeons, this time laughed without waking even the children.</t998> <t999>"Are you going to be a nanny or a husband?" she mocked.</t999> <t1000>Still, Aureliano sensed a deep understanding beneath her mockery.</t1000> <t1001>As he left the room, freed not only from doubts about his manhood but also from the pain that had settled in his heart for months, Pilar Ternera spontaneously made him a promise.</t1001>
<t1002>"I will talk to the girl," he said.</t1002> <t1003>"Consider this job in the bag from now on."</t1003>
<t1004>He also kept his word.</t1004> <t1005>However, it coincided with a bad time; the old order and harmony at home were no longer there.</t1005> <t1006>When Rebeca's love fever, which could no longer be kept secret due to her shouting and yelling, was revealed, a fire also ignited in Amaranta's heart.</t1006> <t1007>She too was burning with the pain of a secret love.</t1007> <t1008>She would lock herself in the bathroom, pour her hopeless love into passionate letters, and then hide the letters at the bottom of her chest.</t1008> <t1009>Ursula was exhausted from dealing with the illness of the two girls who had fallen into bed.</t1009> <t1010>No matter what she did, she could not learn Amaranta's trouble.</t1010> <t1011>Finally, it came to her mind.</t1011> <t1012>This time, she went and rummaged through Amaranta's chest and found a stack of letters tied with a pink ribbon, written to Pietro Crespi but never sent, swollen by the lilies placed between them that had not yet dried, and wet with tears that had not yet dried.</t1012> <t1013>Crying with rage, she cursed the day she thought of becoming a laterna, banned the girls' embroidery lessons, and declared mourning until the girls lost all hope, even though there was no death in sight.</t1013>
<t1014>Jose Arcadio Buendia, who changed his initial negative impression of Pietro Crespi and admired his skill with musical instruments, also failed to calm his wife.</t1014> <t1015>Just at that moment, when Pilar Ternera informed Aureliano that Remedios was willing to marry, this event added fuel to the fire.</t1015> <t1016>Aureliano realized that bringing up this matter to his parents would only serve to upset them further.</t1016> <t1017>Jose Arcadio Buendia and Ursula, who were called to the living room to discuss a private and important matter, listened to their son's words without making a sound.</t1017>
<t1018>However, when Jose Arcadio Buendia heard the name of the prospective bride, he turned red and purple.</t1018> <t1019>Love is a terrible affliction, he shouted at the top of his lungs.</t1019> <t1020>With so many decent and honorable girls around, you choose to marry the daughter of our sworn enemy.</t1020> <t1021>Ursula, on the other hand, found this choice quite appropriate.</t1021> <t1022>She praised the beauty, manners, industriousness, and politeness of the Moscote sisters, saying she loved all seven of them, and congratulated her son.</t1022> <t1023>Seeing his wife's delight, Jose Arcadio Buendia saw no other way but to concede and put forth a condition: Since the man was also in favor, Pietro Crespi and Rebeca would also marry.</t1023> <t1024>Ursula would take Amaranta to the capital at the first opportunity; the different environment and different people would console the girl.</t1024>
<t1025>As soon as she heard the news, Rebeca's illness vanished.</t1025> <t1026>She sat down and wrote a letter to her fiancé, sharing the good news.</t1026> <t1027>After showing the letter to her parents and getting their approval, she mailed it herself without needing an intermediary.</t1027> <t1028>Amaranta seemed to accept the family's decision.</t1028> <t1029>In time, her health improved, but she swore that Rebeca would only marry over her dead body.</t1029>
<t1030>The following Saturday, Jose Arcadio Buendia dressed in his best clothes.</t1030> <t1031>He put on his navy blue suit, attached his cardboard collar, wore the deerskin boots he had worn on the night of the first party, and went to ask Remedios Moscote's father for her hand in marriage by God's command.</t1031> <t1032>The magistrate and his wife were both pleased and uneasy because they did not know the reason for this unexpected visit.</t1032> <t1033>After the reason for the visit was explained, they came to the conclusion that there was a mistake in the name of the prospective bride.</t1033> <t1034>To clear up this mistake, the woman woke Remedios from her sleep, took the drowsy child in her arms, and brought her.</t1034>
<t1035>They asked the girl whether her intention to marry was genuine, and she, half asleep and half awake, said that at that moment, all she wanted was to sleep.</t1035> <t1036>Understanding the concerns of the Moscotes, Jose Arcadio Buendia went to ask Aureliano about the truth of the matter.</t1036> <t1037>When he returned, the Moscotes were dressed up, had rearranged the chairs and sofas in the guest room, placed flowers in vases, and were waiting with their eldest daughters lined up beside them.</t1037> <t1038>Feeling uncomfortable both from the unpleasantness of the situation and the stiff collar that was choking him, Jose Arcadio Buendia confirmed that the prospective bride was indeed Remedios: Don Apolinar Moscote, too stunned to know what to say, remarked, Is this for real?</t1038> <t1039>We have six more daughters.</t1039> <t1040>And they are all of marriageable age, all single, and all would be delighted to become the wife of a sensible, hardworking young man like your son.</t1040>
<t1041>Aureliano, on the other hand, sets his eyes on someone who still wets their pants.</t1041> <t1042>His wife, a woman with sad eyes and a sad face, who remains steadfast even if the mosque collapses, immediately scolded her husband for his indiscretion.</t1042> <t1043>After drinking the fruit liqueur, they gladly accepted Aureliano's decision.</t1043> <t1044>Only, Senora Moscote said she wanted to speak with Ursula woman to woman.</t1044> <t1045>Grumbling about being involved in men's business with her dough-covered hands, but also feeling deeply moved, Ursula got up the next day and went to see Senora Moscote.</t1045> <t1046>Half an hour later, she returned with the news that Remedios was not yet of age.</t1046> <t1047>Aureliano did not see this as a serious obstacle at all.</t1047> <t1048>He had waited so long for his bride that he could wait until she came of age.</t1048>
<t1049>The newly established order at home was disrupted by Melquiades' death.</t1049> <t1050>Melquiades' death was not an unexpected event, but it happened under unexpected circumstances.</t1050> <t1051>A few months after his arrival, the poor man suddenly deteriorated so much that he quickly turned into a useless old grandfather who wandered like a shadow in the bedrooms, dragged his feet while walking, never stopped talking about the old days, and whose existence everyone forgot until he was found dead in his bed.</t1051> <t1052>In the beginning, Jose Arcadio Buendia, who had taken an interest in photography and Nostradamus' prophecies, was helping him.</t1052> <t1053>But as it became increasingly difficult for them to understand each other, Jose Arcadio also started to leave Melquiades alone.</t1053> <t1054>Melquiades had become blind and deaf.</t1054> <t1055>He confused the people he talked to with those he had known long ago, and answered questions with words formed from a mishmash of various languages.</t1055> <t1056>He wandered around breathing deeply, and although he moved among objects, he walked without touching anything as if he had an instinctive sense of direction.</t1056> <t1057>One day, he forgot to put on his dentures that he had placed in the glass of water by his bedside the night before; and then he never wore them again.</t1057>
<t1058>When Ursula decided to expand the house, she had Melquiades build a room next to Aureliano's workshop, away from the noise and commotion of the house.</t1058> <t1059>She had a window installed that let in plenty of light, had a bookshelf made, and personally cleaned and arranged on the shelves the books that were almost falling apart from dust and moths, the bundles of papers filled with scribbles, the glass holding his dentures and tiny yellow-flowered water plants rooting at the bottom.</t1059> <t1060>Melquiades must have liked his new place very much because he was never seen around again; he even stopped coming to the dining room.</t1060> <t1061>He only went to Aureliano's workshop and spent hours writing on the dry parchment papers that crumbled like starched cloth, which he brought with him.</t1061> <t1062>He also ate the meals that Visitacion brought him twice a day there.</t1062> <t1063>Recently, he had lost his appetite and had started eating nothing but vegetables.</t1063> <t1064>Soon, a sorrowful pallor, like that of vegetarians, settled on his face.</t1064> <t1065>His skin developed a thin layer of moss, like the old vest he never took off.</t1065> <t1066>His breath began to smell like that of sleeping animals.</t1066> <t1067>Aureliano, who was entirely absorbed in his rhyming, gradually forgot about him.</t1067>
<t1068>Only once did something comprehensible come to his ears from Melquiades' endless monologues, and he listened.</t1068> <t1069>In fact, the only thing that could be gleaned from Melquiades' mumblings was the constantly repeated word equinox, equinox, equinox, and the name Alexander von Humboldt.</t1069> <t1070>When Arcadio began to help Aureliano with his silver work, he got a bit closer to Melquiades.</t1070> <t1071>Melquiades responded to his attempts to get closer with nonsensical answers in Spanish.</t1071> <t1072>But one day, on an overly emotional evening, the clouds enveloping his brain seemed to part for a moment.</t1072> <t1073>Years later, as Arcadio stood before the firing squad, he would remember the shiver he felt on the day Melquiades sat him down and read a few pages of writings that he didn't understand but which sounded like sacred commands when read aloud.</t1073>
<t1074>After Melquiades finished reading, he smiled for the first time in a long while and spoke in Spanish: When I die, boil mercury in my room for three days, Arcadio, he hinted these to Jose Arcadio Buendia.</t1074> <t1075>Although he tried to learn something more detailed; he could not get any words from Melquiades other than, I have attained the secret of immortality.</t1075>
<t1076>When Melquiades' breath started to smell bad, Arcadio began taking him to the river on Thursday mornings.</t1076> <t1077>Bathing seemed to do him good.</t1077> <t1078>Melquiades would undress and dive into the water with the young men.</t1078> <t1079>With that mysterious sense of direction, he stayed away from deep and dangerous places.</t1079> <t1080>Once he said, "We are originally water.</t1080> <t1081>We all originated from water," he said.</t1081> <t1082>This went on for a long time.</t1082> <t1083>Other than the night of the party where he made a pitiful effort to operate the lantern, and the days he went to the river with Arcadio, carrying a gourd, a towel, and almond oil soap under his arm, no one saw him.</t1083>
<t1084>One Thursday morning, when Aureliano went to take him to the river, he heard him say, "I died of fever on the beaches of Singapore."</t1084> <t1085>That day, Melquiades dove into a place in the water where he shouldn't have, and he never came back up.</t1085> <t1086>They found his body only the next day, several miles away.</t1086>
<t1087>The current had made a bend there and thrown him ashore.</t1087> <t1088>When they found him, a vulture was perched on his belly.</t1088> <t1089>Despite all of Ursula's resistance, who had not shed so many tears even when her own father died, Jose Arcadio Buendia did not allow Melquiades to be buried.</t1089> <t1090>He kept saying that Melquiades was immortal and that he himself had revealed the secret of resurrection after death.</t1090> <t1091>He brought out an alembic that had been forgotten in a corner and placed the pot of boiling mercury, bubbling with blue bubbles, next to the corpse.</t1091> <t1092>Don Apolinar Moscote dared to remind him that keeping a drowned person unburied was harmful to public health.</t1092> <t1093>Jose Arcadio Buendia, saying, "You are mistaken, sir, because he is alive," boiled the mercury burner for exactly seventy-two hours until the corpse shone brightly and seemed about to explode, and a pestilent odor filled the house.</t1093> <t1094>Only after seventy-two hours had passed did he allow them to bury him, and not haphazardly, but with a grand funeral that could be conceived for the greatest man of Macondo.</t1094> <t1095>It was the first and most crowded funeral in the village.</t1095> <t1096>A more grandiose one would only be seen a century later at the funeral procession of the Great-Grandmother.</t1096> <t1097>They buried him in a pit dug in the middle of the area they had designated as the cemetery.</t1097> <t1098>They placed a stone at his head and wrote the only thing they knew about him: MELQUIADES.</t1098>
<t1099>They kept vigil for nine nights.</t1099> <t1100>Taking advantage of the chaos created by the crowd that gathered in the garden to drink coffee, tell jokes, and play cards, Amaranta found an opportunity to declare her love to Pietro Crespi.</t1100> <t1101>A few weeks earlier, Pietro Crespi had formalized the promise he made to Rebeca, and opened a shop selling musical instruments and mechanical toys in a place where Arabs used to trade trinkets with parrot monstrosities and villagers called Turkish Street.</t1101> <t1102>The handsome Italian, whose head adorned with shiny curls made every woman's heart skip a beat, treated Amaranta as if she were a spoiled child not worth taking seriously.</t1102>
<t1103>"I have a little brother," he said.</t1103> <t1104>"He will come to help me in the shop."</t1104>
<t1105>Amaranta was very offended by these words, felt humiliated, and said with great anger that she was ready to prevent her sister's marriage even at the cost of her own life.</t1105> <t1106>The dramatic aspect of this threat affected the Italian so much that he went and told Rebeca about it.</t1106> <t1107>Thus, Amaranta's trip, which had been continuously postponed due to Ursula's busyness, was carried out within a week.</t1107> <t1108>Amaranta did not say a word, but did not refrain from whispering in Rebeca's ear while kissing her goodbye:</t1108>
