<t1111>On one hand, the absence of Ursula, and on the other, the invisible presence of Melquiades wandering through the rooms dragging his feet, turned the house into a vast, empty ruin.</t1111><t1112>Rebeca managed the house, and the Indian woman ran the oven.</t1112><t1113>At twilight, when Pietro Crespi arrived with the heartwarming scent of lavender and the gift toys he never failed to bring, his fiancée would take him to the guest room, opening all the doors and windows wide so no one would think ill of them.</t1113><t1114>This was an unnecessary precaution, as the Italian had shown himself to be so measured and respectful that he wouldn't even touch the hand of the girl who would become his wife within a year.</t1114><t1115>Crespi's visits filled the house with beautiful toys.</t1115><t1116>Dancing ballerinas when wound up, musical boxes that played by themselves, monkeys that jumped and somersaulted, galloping horses, tambourine-playing clowns, and many more.</t1116><t1117>The various, mind-boggling toy animals brought by Pietro Crespi pulled Jose Arcadio Buendia out of the sorrow he felt from Melquiades' death and returned him to his old days of fascination with alchemy.</t1117><t1118>For a while, he spent his time in a world of gutted animals, trying to perfect them by dismantling their mechanisms and turning them into perpetual motion machines using pendulum principles.</t1118><t1119>As for Aureliano, he stopped doing any work, claiming he was teaching Remedios to read and write.</t1119><t1120>At first, the child didn't want to leave her toys and preferred her dolls over washing and dressing up to wait in the guest room for this man who moved into their house every day.</t1120><t1121>However, Aureliano's patience and passion eventually prevailed, and the little girl began to sit for hours learning the alphabet, drawing pictures of houses with cows in the barn with colored pencils, and round suns with yellow rays stretching beyond the hills.</t1121>
<t1122>The only one who was not happy was Rebeca, and that was because of Amaranta's intimidation.</t1122> <t1123>She knew her sister's temperament, how proud she was, and she was frightened by the intensity of her anger.</t1123> <t1124>She would lock herself in the bathroom for hours, sucking her finger, using her iron willpower to stop herself from eating dirt.</t1124> <t1125>In the anxiety of not knowing what would happen, she had Pilar Ternera read her fortune to ease her heart.</t1125> <t1126>After Pilar Ternera spouted a bunch of cliché phrases, she finally said what she had to say:</t1126>

<t1168>Jose Arcadio Buendia finally achieved his goal.</t1168> <t1169>He connected the clockwork mechanism to a ballerina, and the toy danced to its own rhythm for three days and three nights.</t1169> <t1170>This invention captivated his mind more than all his other absurd pursuits.</t1170> <t1171>He stopped eating and drinking.</t1171> <t1172>He couldn't sleep.</t1172> <t1173>Only Rebeca's care and affection kept him from falling into a madness from which he could never recover.</t1173> <t1174>He paced his room, thinking out loud until dawn, searching for ways to apply the law of the pendulum to anything that could be made useful by movement, such as carts and rakes.</t1174> <t1175>His sleeplessness had weakened and exhausted him so much that he did not recognize the white-haired, shaky man who entered his room at dawn.</t1175> <t1176>It was Prudencio Aguilar.</t1176> <t1177>When Jose Arcadio Buendia finally realized who he was, he shivered with a homesick longing, surprised that even the dead could age.</t1177> <t1178>Prudencio! he cried out.</t1178> <t1179>How far you have come!</t1179> <t1180>After so many years of death, the longing for the living was so intense, the desire to exchange a few words so great, and the proximity of the other death within death so terrifying that Prudencio Aguilar had finally come to love his mortal enemy.</t1180> <t1181>He had searched for him for years.</t1181> <t1182>He had asked the dead coming from Riohacha, he had asked the dead coming from the Upar Valley, he had asked the dead coming from the swamp.</t1182> <t1183>He had received no news, because until Melquiades came and marked it as a tiny dot on the maps of death, Macondo was unknown in the land of the dead.</t1183> <t1184>Jose Arcadio Buendia talked with Prudencio Aguilar until dawn.</t1184> <t1185>A few hours later, exhausted from sleeplessness, he went to Aureliano's workshop and asked, What day is it today?</t1185>

<t1208>On the one hand, he was shouting something in a high-pitched and incomprehensible language.</t1208> <t1209>Aureliano saw that things were getting out of hand and that if he let it go, his father would destroy the house, so he asked for help from the neighbors.</t1209> <t1210>It took ten people to bring Jose Arcadio to the ground, fourteen people to tie his hands and feet, and twenty people to drag him to the chestnut tree in the garden.</t1210> <t1211>When they tied him to the tree and left him, he was shouting something in an unknown language, and green foam was coming out of his mouth.</t1211> <t1212>When Ursula and Amaranta returned, Jose Arcadio Buendia was tied to the chestnut tree by his hands and feet: he was soaking wet from the rain and had completely lost his mind.</t1212> <t1213>Ursula and Amaranta tried to talk to him.</t1213> <t1214>He did not recognize them and said something in a language they did not understand.</t1214> <t1215>Ursula untied his wrists and feet, which had been cut by the ropes.</t1215> <t1216>She left only his waist tied.</t1216> <t1217>Later on, they made a shelter out of palm leaves to protect him from the sun and rain.</t1217>
<t1218>Aureliano Buendia and Remedios Moscote entered into matrimony on a Sunday in March, in front of the altar set up in the guest room by Father Nicanor Reyna.</t1218> <t1219>Thus concluded the stormy four weeks that had turned the Moscote family upside down due to Remedios' transition to adolescence without abandoning her childhood habits.</t1219> <t1220>Her mother had sat her down and explained at length how to transition to young womanhood, but still, one day in February, while Aureliano was sitting and chatting with his sisters-in-law, Remedios had burst into the room, showing her underwear stained with a chocolate-colored liquid.</t1220> <t1221>It was decided that the wedding would take place one month from that date.</t1221> <t1222>During this time, the household was at their wits' end trying to teach Remedios to bathe herself, dress herself, and manage some basic household chores.</t1222>

<t1247>Father Nicanor Reyna, whom Don Apolinar Moscote brought from the swamp to conduct his wedding ceremony, was an old man hardened by the ingratitude of his profession.</t1247> <t1248>His skin was so pale it seemed as if his bones were about to burst out from within.</t1248> <t1249>He had a round belly and an angelic expression on his face that stemmed more from simplicity than goodness.</t1249> <t1250>After the wedding, he intended to return to his homeland.</t1250> <t1251>However, when he saw that the people of Macondo had shelved both virtue and sin, forgotten what baptism and feast days were, his heart could not bear such depravity.</t1251>
<t1252>Believing that there could be no place more in need of God's love and fear, and that no more virgin soil could be found to sow God's seed, he decided to stay another week, to baptize anyone circumcised or uncircumcised, to marry long-time couples before God, and to pray over the dead.</t1252> <t1253>But no one paid any attention.</t1253> <t1254>Whoever he approached, they said that they had managed just fine without a priest for all these years, and that they conducted their affairs with God without any intermediaries for their souls.</t1254> <t1255>When Father Nicanor grew tired of going from street to street preaching, he thought of building a church.</t1255>
<t1256>This was going to be a church like no other in the world, adorned with stained glass on both sides, featuring life-sized depictions of saints, and people would travel all the way from Rome to this land of non-believers to find their God.</t1256> <t1257>He took a copper bowl in his hand and went out to collect donations.</t1257> <t1258>Everyone was giving generously from their hearts, and what was given was not insignificant at all, but it was impossible to satisfy the holy father's greed.</t1258> <t1259>He said it had to be a bell strong enough to bring the drowned to the surface and loud enough to make an impact wherever it struck.</t1259> <t1260>His voice became hoarse from pleading, and he could no longer speak.</t1260> <t1261>When he couldn't express his voice outwardly, it spread inwardly, and his bones and marrow began to resonate.</t1261> <t1262>On a Saturday when he couldn't even gather enough money for the doors, he went mad with despair, not knowing what to do, he set up a pulpit in the middle of the village, and on Sunday, like in the days of his insomnia, he took a rattle in his hand and called everyone, whether they heard or not, to an open-air mass.</t1262> <t1263>Most went out of curiosity.</t1263> <t1264>Some went out of longing for the old days, and some so that God wouldn't consider ignoring His messenger an insult to Himself.</t1264> <t1265>Thus, at eight in the morning, half the village gathered in the square and listened to the sermon given by Father Nicanor, whose voice was hoarse from begging.</t1265> <t1266>Finally, as the gathered crowd began to slowly disperse, Father Nicanor raised his arms as if to indicate he was about to say something important.</t1266>
<t1267>"Wait a minute," he said.</t1267> <t1268>"Now we will witness an indisputable proof of God's infinite power."</t1268>
<t1269>During the ceremony, the child who was helping him brought a cup of dark cocoa with steam rising from it.</t1269> <t1270>Father Nicanor finished the cocoa in one gulp.</t1270> <t1271>He wiped his mouth with the handkerchief he took out from the sleeve of his robe, opened his arms wide, and closed his eyes.</t1271> <t1272>And Father Nicanor suddenly rose about fifteen centimeters off the ground.</t1272> <t1273>There could be no more convincing proof of God's greatness.</t1273> <t1274>After going door to door for a few more days and downing hot cocoa, he repeated this flying trick.</t1274> <t1275>When the bag in the sacristan boy's hand was stuffed full, he started the construction of the church within a month.</t1275> <t1276>No one doubted that God's hand was in the priest's flying, except for Jose Arcadio Buendia, who watched the crowd gathered around the chestnut tree to see the flying demonstration again without moving a muscle.</t1276> <t1277>When Father Nicanor began to lift his feet off the ground along with the chair he was sitting on, Jose Arcadio Buendia did nothing but spread out a little more on the wooden bench and shrug his shoulders.</t1277>
<t1278>Then I said, "Hoc est simplicissimus."</t1278> <t1279>This man discovered the fourth state of matter.</t1279>
<t1280>Peder Nicanor raised his hands in the air and the four legs of the chair touched the ground at the same time.</t1280>
<t1281>"No," he replied.</t1281> <t1282>"This fact undoubtedly proves the existence of God."</t1282>
<t1283>Thus, it was understood that the hocus-pocus spoken by Jose Arcadio Buendia was Latin.</t1283> <t1284>Father Nicanor, taking advantage of being the only person who could communicate with him, tried to inject the belief in God into his deranged mind: Now, he went to the chestnut tree every day, preaching in Latin.</t1284> <t1285>However, Jose Arcadio Buendia paid no attention to the babble, did not care about the cocoa miracle, and insisted that he wanted a photograph of God as the only proof.</t1285>
<t1286>Upon this, Father Nicanor brought medallions, icons, and even a Veronica handkerchief.</t1286> <t1287>But Jose Arcadio Buendia did not accept them, saying they were artistic things without a scientific basis.</t1287> <t1288>He was so adamant that in the end, Father Nicanor gave up trying to call him to the righteous path and continued to visit him purely out of human compassion.</t1288> <t1289>But then Jose Arcadio Buendia rolled up his sleeves and tried to undermine the priest's faith by trapping him in logical dilemmas.</t1289> <t1290>One day, Father Nicanor brought a chess set under the chestnut tree and invited Jose Arcadio to play.</t1290> <t1291>Jose Arcadio Buendia did not respond to this invitation, saying that there was no point in playing a game according to pre-agreed rules.</t1291> <t1292>Having never seen chess played without rules, Father Nicanor never mentioned chess again.</t1292> <t1293>Astonished by Jose Arcadio Buendia's rational conversation, he asked how they had managed to tie him to the tree.</t1293>
<t1294>This is the simplest, replied Jose Arcadio Buendia, I'm crazy, that's why.</t1294>
<t1295>After that day, the priest, who began to doubt his own faith, never went to him again and dedicated himself to the construction of the church.</t1295> <t1296>Rebeca's hopes were rekindled.</t1296> <t1297>The future now depended on the construction of the church.</t1297> <t1298>Because one Sunday, when Father Nicanor came to eat with them and the family sat down at the table, while they were talking about the magnificent masses and ceremonies that would be held after the church was built, Amaranta said, "The luckiest of us will be Rebeca."</t1298> <t1299>When Rebeca didn't understand what she meant, she explained with a naive smile:</t1299>
<t1300>-You are the one who will celebrate the completion of the church with your wedding.</t1300>
<t1301>Rebeca wanted to close the subject immediately.</t1301> <t1302>At this rate, the church wouldn't be finished for another ten years.</t1302> <t1303>However, Father Nicanor didn't think so at all.</t1303> <t1304>The generosity of charitable citizens led him to optimistic calculations.</t1304> <t1305>Despite Rebeca's silent anger, who had a lump in her throat, Ursula found Amaranta's idea quite reasonable and made a considerable donation to speed up the construction.</t1305> <t1306>According to Father Nicanor, if there were another donation like this, the church would be finished in less than three years.</t1306> <t1307>Believing that Amaranta's intentions were not as pure as they seemed, Rebeca never spoke to her again.</t1307> <t1308>That night, while they were arguing, Amaranta said, This was the mildest, most harmless thing I could do.</t1308> <t1309>At least this way, I won't have to kill you for another three years.</t1309> <t1310>Rebeca accepted this challenge of hers.</t1310>
<t1311>Pietro Crespi was devastated when he heard that their wedding had been postponed again.</t1311> <t1312>Rebeca encouraged him by proving her loyalty once more.</t1312> <t1313>"If you want, we can elope," she said.</t1313> <t1314>But Pietro Crespi was not the kind of man to jump into an adventure with his eyes closed.</t1314> <t1315>He didn't have a trace of his fiancée's boldness.</t1315> <t1316>Respecting a given word was his most valuable treasure.</t1316> <t1317>Upon this, Rebeca began to employ bolder methods.</t1317> <t1318>Once, when a gust of wind from an unknown source decided to give Father Nicanor the money needed to complete the church.</t1324> <t1325>Amaranta was not worried at all.</t1325> <t1326>Every day, she sat on the terrace with her friends, embroidering, which was stored in the console in her bedroom.</t1327> <t1328>There were two months left until the church was completed.</t1328>
<t1329>However, the plan didn't go as expected, and with the wedding approaching, an increasingly impatient Rebeca decided to start preparing her wedding dress earlier than Amaranta had anticipated.</t1329> <t1330>When she pulled out the drawer and opened the papers and then the bundle, she saw that moths had riddled the dress, the veil's threads, and even the bridal crown made of orange blossoms with holes.</t1330> <t1331>She had no doubt that she had put handfuls of mothballs into the bundle, but this disaster was so typical that she couldn't bring herself to blame Amaranta.</t1331> <t1332>There was less than a month left until the wedding.</t1332> <t1333>Fortunately, Amparo Moscote promised to have a new wedding dress ready within a week.</t1333> <t1334>On that rainy afternoon when Amparo came to do Rebeca's final fitting, Amaranta almost fainted.</t1334>
<t1335>Her breath was cut off, and cold sweat ran down her back.</t1335> <t1336>For months, she had been waiting in fear for that moment to arrive.</t1336> <t1337>Because if the last resort to prevent Rebeca from getting married failed, she had no doubt that she would dare to poison her at the last moment.</t1337> <t1338>That day, while Rebeca, wrapped in the fabric armor that Amparo had wound around her body with thousands of pins and infinite patience, stood breathless from the heat, Amaranta lost track of her work, embroidered incorrectly, pricked her finger with a needle, but with a terrifying calmness decided that the last day would be the Friday before the wedding.</t1338> <t1339>She would poison Rebeca with the spirit of opium she would add to her coffee.</t1339>
<t1340>An unexpected and unsolvable new obstacle postponed the wedding once more and indefinitely.</t1340> <t1341>A week before the wedding day, little bride Remedios woke up in the middle of the night, submerged in the warmth of water that burst with a pain as if something was being torn from inside her.</t1341> <t1342>Three days later, she passed away from blood poisoning along with the twins lying head to toe in her womb.</t1342> <t1343>Amaranta was writhing in guilt.</t1343> <t1344>She had prayed so fervently for a terrible event to occur so that she wouldn't have to poison Rebeca, that she blamed herself for Remedios' death.</t1344> <t1345>This was not the obstacle she had desperately awaited.</t1345> <t1346>Remedios had brought joy to the house.</t1346> <t1347>She had decorated a room near the workshop with dolls and toys from her recent childhood and settled there with her husband.</t1347> <t1348>Her cheerful and lively spirit transcended the four walls of the bedroom and had become like a breeze of health on the begonia terrace.</t1348> <t1349>She would start singing at dawn.</t1349> <t1350>She was the only one who dared to intervene in the fights between Rebeca and Amaranta.</t1350>
<t1351>He had also taken care of Jose Arcadio Buendia.</t1351> <t1352>He would bring him his food, help him with his daily needs, wash and brush his hands and face, pick out the lice and fleas from his hair and beard, and if the weather turned bad, he would reinforce the shelter made of palm leaves with a canvas.</t1352> <t1353>In recent months, he had picked up Latin and had even started to speak broken Latin with Jose Arcadio.</t1353> <t1354>When Pilar Ternera's son, born of Aureliano, was brought home and baptized with the name Aureliano Jose in a ceremony held among the family, Remedios decided to embrace him as her first child.</t1354> <t1355>Her maternal instinct surprised Ursula.</t1355> <t1356>As for Aureliano, the sole meaning and purpose of his life had become his wife: He worked in his workshop all day, and around noon, Remedios would bring him his coffee.</t1356> <t1357>Every evening, they would go together to the Moscotes' house.</t1357> <t1358>Aureliano would engage in endless domino games with his father-in-law, while Remedios would chat with her sisters or consult her mother on more important matters.</t1358>
<t1359>Becoming related to the Buendia family had solidified Don Apolinar Moscote's respectability in the village.</t1359> <t1360>By frequently traveling to the capital, he managed to get the government to take action and succeeded in having a school built, which would be headed by Arcadio, who had inherited his grandfather's passion for teaching.</t1360> <t1361>With sweet talk, he persuaded the villagers to paint most of the houses blue for the national independence day.</t1361> <t1362>Under the pressure of Father Nicanor, he had Catarino's shop moved to a back street and closed down the dens of iniquity that had opened in the heart of the village.</t1362> <t1363>On his trips to the capital, he brought armed mounted police to ensure security; no one even remembered the initial agreement not to allow armed men into the village.</t1363> <t1364>Aureliano was pleased with his father-in-law's dominance.</t1364> <t1365>His friends teased him, saying, "You'll get as fat as he is."</t1365> <t1366>However, the monotonous life that made his cheekbones more prominent and intensified the sparkle in his eyes did not make him gain weight; instead, it deepened the thoughtful line at the corner of his lips and the expression of determination.</t1366>
<t1367>They had endeared themselves so much to both the boy's and the girl's side that when Remedios announced she was going to have a child, even Rebeca and Amaranta set aside their quarrels and grievances and took up knitting, blue if it was a boy and pink if it was a girl.</t1367>
<t1368>A few years later, while standing before the firing squad, the last person to cross Arcadio's mind was Remedios.</t1368> <t1369>Ursula declared mourning by locking the windows and doors, and she did not allow anyone to enter or leave the house unless it was very important.</t1369> <t1370>She banned speaking loudly for a year and placed a photograph of Remedios, tied with a black ribbon, and an oil lamp that was never extinguished at the head of her bed.</t1370> <t1371>The later generations who kept the lamp burning without ever extinguishing it would look long and hard at the picture of this girl in a pleated skirt, white boots, and an organza ribbon in her hair, unable to reconcile it with the image of a typical great-great-grandmother.</t1371> <t1372>Amaranta took on the care of Aureliano Jose.</t1372> <t1373>She embraced him like a son who would share her loneliness and save her from the torment of having caused Remedios' death with her frantic prayers.</t1373> <t1374>In the afternoons, Pietro Crespi would come, tiptoeing in his black ribboned hat, to check on Rebeca, whose heart was bleeding in her long-sleeved black dress.</t1374>
<t1375>Even thinking about setting a new date for the wedding seemed like such a great disrespect that their engagement turned into an endless relationship.</t1375> <t1376>The days when they played various games to turn off the lamps just to kiss were long gone; their love had now been abandoned to death, a breathless romance that no one cared about anymore.</t1376> <t1377>Having lost all her hope and all her supports, Rebeca started eating soil again.</t1377>
<t1378>One completely unexpected day -the mourning had lasted so long that the lacework sessions had started again- at noon, when the heat descended with a deadly fervor, someone slammed the street door wide open, and the house shook to its very foundations.</t1378> <t1379>Amaranta, who was embroidering with her friends on the terrace, Rebeca, who was sucking her finger in the bedroom, Ursula in the kitchen, Aureliano in his workshop, and even Jose Arcadio Buendia under the chestnut tree, all thought there was an earthquake and the house was collapsing on top of them.</t1379> <t1380>A burly man had arrived.</t1380>
<t1381>His broad shoulders couldn't fit through the doors.</t1381><t1382>On his thick neck, reminiscent of wild bulls, hung a Madonna medallion for good luck.</t1382><t1383>His arms and chest were joy.</t1400><t1401>It was Jose Arcadio.</t1401><t1402>He had returned penniless, just as he had left.</t1402><t1403>Ursula even had to pay for the horse he had rented.</t1403><t1404>He spoke Spanish <t1407>When he woke up, after swallowing sixteen raw eggs one after another, he headed straight to Catarino's shop.</t1407> <t1408>The women who saw his gigantic figure were instantly filled with curiosity.</t1408> <t1409>Jose Arcadio ordered music to be played and treated everyone to cane liquor.</t1409> <t1410>He was also saying that he would wrestle with five people at once.</t1410> <t1411>The people there, realizing that they couldn't even bend his arm, let alone bring him down, dismissed it saying, "No way."</t1411> <t1412>He was wearing a Ninos-en-cruz amulet.</t1412> <t1413>Catarino, who didn't believe in gaining strength through amulets or magic, bet twelve pesos that he couldn't move the counter.</t1413> <t1414>Jose Arcadio lifted the counter, carried it over his head, and placed it in the middle of the street.</t1414> <t1415>It took eleven people, sweating profusely, to put the counter back in its place.</t1415> <t1416>Just as the fun was reaching its peak, Jose Arcadio revealed and displayed his unprecedented mark, covered in red and blue tattoos in various languages.</t1416>
<t1417>He told the women clinging to him that he would stay with whoever gave him the most money.</t1417> <t1418>The wealthiest among them gave twenty pesos.</t1418> <t1419>Upon this, Jose Arcadio suggested that the women each give ten pesos and draw lots for him.</t1419> <t1420>This was an unprecedented fee, because even the most successful woman would earn at most eight pesos a night.</t1420> <t1421>Nevertheless, they accepted the proposal.</t1421> <t1422>They wrote their names on fourteen pieces of paper and put them into a hat.</t1422> <t1423>Each woman drew a lot.</t1423> <t1424>When only two pieces of paper remained, it was already clear to whom they belonged.</t1424>
<t1425>Jose Arcadio, "Both of you cough up another five each, and I'll stay with both of you," he said.</t1425>
<t1426>He was earning his bread in this way.</t1426> <t1427>He had mingled with homeless sailors and had circled the globe exactly sixty-five times.</t1427> <t1428>That night, the women he slept with at Catarino's shop stripped him naked in the middle of the night and took him down to the dance hall, showing that from his neck to his toes, both front and back, he was covered in tattoos with no space left for even a pin to prick.</t1428> <t1429>Jose Arcadio could not mix with the family.</t1429> <t1430>He would sleep soundly all day and go to the red-light district at night, betting on his strength.</t1430> <t1431>On the days when Ursula could coax him to sit at the table, he would cheerfully recount his adventures in distant lands.</t1431> <t1432>Once, their ship had wrecked, and they had stayed on a raft in the Sea of Japan for two weeks, surviving by eating their friends who had died from sunstroke and whose flesh, cooked by the sun, tasted somewhat bitter and somewhat sweet.</t1432>
<t1433>On a hot afternoon in the Bay of Bengal, their boat struck and killed a sea dragon, and when they cut open the dragon's belly, they found a crusader's helmet, metal buckles, and weapons.</t1433> <t1434>In the Caribbean Sea, he had seen Victor Hugues' ghost ship, whose sails had been torn by death, whose masts had been gnawed by worms, still trying to find its way to Guadeloupe.</t1434> <t1435>As Jose Arcadio recounted these tales, Ursula would cry as if reading letters from her son in exile that had never arrived, saying, "Here you had a place above our heads, my son."</t1435> <t1436>She would lament, "I always gave the meals I set aside for you to the pigs!"</t1436> <t1437>Yet she could not wholeheartedly believe that this giant of a man, who could devour half a pig in one sitting and still seem unfazed, was the same person as the young man kidnapped by the gypsies.</t1437> <t1438>The other family members shared more or less the same feelings.</t1438> <t1439>Amaranta did not hide her disgust at his animal-like belching at the table.</t1439> <t1440>Arcadio, who knew nothing of the true nature of their bond, would dismissively answer his questions, which were meant to win his affection.</t1440>
<t1441>Aureliano was trying to relive the days when they slept in the same room, to recall childhood memories.</t1441><t1442>However, Jose Arcadio, whose mind was filled with a multitude of memories <t1462>They got married three days later during the afternoon service.</t1462> <t1463>Jose Arcadio went to Pietro Crespi's shop the day before.</t1463> <t1464>Crespi was teaching music to his students.</t1464> <t1465>Without feeling the need to pull him aside and whisper, Jose Arcadio openly said, I'm marrying Rebeca.</t1465> <t1466>Pietro Crespi's face turned ashen.</t1466> <t1467>He handed the instrument to one of the students and stopped the lesson.</t1467> <t1468>When they were alone in the room filled with musical instruments and mechanical toys, Pietro Crespi said, She's your sister.</t1468>

<t1470>Pietro Crespi wiped his forehead with a handkerchief soaked in coca.</t1470>
<t1471>"It would be against nature," he tried to explain.</t1471> <t1472>"It is also against the laws."</t1472>
