<t1478>-If you truly love family, there's the lovely Amaranta for you, he said.</t1478>
<t1479>Peder Nicanor announced during the Sunday mass that Jose Arcadio and Rebeca were not siblings.</t1479> <t1480>Ursula never forgave this event, which she deemed an incredible disrespect, and banned the newlyweds from ever setting foot in the house again.</t1480> <t1481>She considered them as good as dead.</t1481> <t1482>They rented a house across from the cemetery and settled in by setting up Jose Arcadio's hammock, their only piece of furniture.</t1482> <t1483>On their wedding night, a scorpion that had crawled into her slipper stung Rebeca's foot.</t1483> <t1484>The girl's tongue went numb.</t1484> <t1485>Nevertheless, this incident did not prevent them from having a honeymoon that caused quite a stir.</t1485> <t1486>The neighbors were astonished by the screams that woke the neighborhood eight times a night and three times during the siesta, and they began to pray that this wildness would not disturb the peace of the dead.</t1486> <t1487>Aureliano was the only one who thought about and cared for them.</t1487> <t1488>He brought them a few pieces of furniture and gave them money until Jose Arcadio came to his senses and started working the abandoned land adjacent to the garden.</t1488> <t1489>On the other hand, although life's twist had brought her an unexpected joy, Amaranta's hatred for Rebeca never faded.</t1489>
<t1490>At Ursula's request, who didn't know how to make people forget the disgrace, Pietro Crespi continued to come for dinner on Tuesdays.</t1490> <t1491>Crespi, who bore his great ruin with dignified composure, still wore a black mourning band on his hat as a sign of respect for the family and demonstrated his affection for Ursula with various gifts.</t1491> <t1492>He brought sardines from Portugal and rose jams made by the Turks.</t1492>
<t1493>Once, he had brought a very beautiful Manila shawl.</t1493> <t1494>Amaranta served him kindly, mending the frayed cuffs of his shirt.</t1494> <t1495>As a birthday gift, she embroidered a dozen handkerchiefs with his initials in the corner.</t1495> <t1496>On Tuesdays, after lunch, while Amaranta sat on the terrace doing embroidery, Pietro Crespi, who kept her company, began to see this woman, whom he had always regarded as a child, as a gift from God.</t1496> <t1497>Amaranta was wild and carefree, but there was nothing she didn't understand.</t1497> <t1498>When she was treated well, she would become gentle and affectionate.</t1498> <t1499>Everyone believed that they would eventually get married.</t1499> <t1500>Indeed, one Tuesday, Pietro Crespi proposed marriage to Amaranta.</t1500> <t1501>Amaranta did not put down her work.</t1501> <t1502>After waiting for the heat pressing against her ears to pass, she gave her voice a mature tone and said:</t1502>
<t1503>"Of course, Crespi," he said.</t1503> <t1504>"But let's leave this matter for a time when we know each other better.</t1504> <t1505>It's never good to rush anything."</t1505>
<t1506>Ursula was bewildered.</t1506> <t1507>She respected Pietro Crespi, but she couldn't determine whether his decision to marry Amaranta after his legendary engagement to Rebeca was morally consistent.</t1507> <t1508>However, when she saw that no one else seemed to care, she decided to stop dwelling on it.</t1508> <t1509>Aureliano, the man of the house, further confused his mother with his incomprehensible words:</t1509>
<t1510>"Now is not the time to worry about marriages," he was saying.</t1510>
<t1511>These words, which Ursula could only understand a month later, were the only ones Aureliano could sincerely utter at that moment.</t1511> <t1512>What he said encompassed not only the topic of marriage but everything unrelated to the war.</t1512> <t1513>Even he couldn't fully comprehend the series of insignificant but irreversible events that led him to that point when he stood before the firing squad.</t1513> <t1514>Remedios's death had not caused the devastation in Aureliano that he had feared.</t1514> <t1515>He felt a silent rage that gradually turned into an inward resentment during the period he lived without a woman.</t1515> <t1516>He had returned to his work but had not given up his habit of playing dominoes with his father-in-law.</t1516> <t1517>The conversations held in the mourning house from evening to evening brought the two men closer together and strengthened their friendship.</t1517> <t1518>His father-in-law would say, "Remarry, Aurelito."</t1518> <t1519>"Look, I have six more daughters."</t1519> <t1520>"Choose the one you like and take her."</t1520>
<t1521>The night of the elections, Don Apolinar Moscote, who had just returned from one of his frequent trips, was quite pessimistic about the political situation in the country.</t1521> <t1522>The Liberals were determined to fight.</t1522> <t1523>At that time, since Aureliano did not have sufficient knowledge about the distinction between Conservatives and Liberals, his father-in-law briefly explained to him what was what.</t1523> <t1524>According to Moscote, the Liberals were Freemasons, bad people, who would hang and strangle priests, disregard religious rules, come up with new inventions like civil marriage and divorce, grant the same rights to illegitimate children as to other children, and weaken the power of the state by dividing the country with a federal system.</t1524>
<t1525>However, the Conservatives, who derived their power directly from God, wanted to defend the established order, ensure public order, and protect the sanctity and morality of the family institution.</t1525> <t1526>They were the guardians of our Lord Jesus' religion, the defenders of the principle of sovereignty, and they would not allow the country to be divided into autonomous administrative regions; they would ensure the integrity of the homeland.</t1526> <t1527>Aureliano, influenced by his humane feelings, sided with the Liberals because of their stance on illegitimate children.</t1527> <t1528>But he could never understand how discussions about intangible matters could bring the parties to the brink of war.</t1528> <t1529>He also attributed the arrival of six armed soldiers, led by a sergeant, to a village where no one had any political passion, during election time, to his father-in-law's meddling.</t1529>
<t1530>When the soldiers arrived in the village, they did not remain idle.</t1530> <t1531>They went from house to house and collected all the weapons they could find.</t1531> <t1532>They even took bread knives along with cane knives.</t1532> <t1533>Then they distributed blue ballots with the names of Conservative candidates and red ballots for the Liberals to men over twenty-one years old.</t1533> <t1534>On the evening before the elections, Don Apolinar Moscote read a proclamation banning the sale of alcoholic beverages and the gathering of more than three people from different families.</t1534> <t1535>The elections passed without incident.</t1535> <t1536>At eight on Sunday morning, a ballot box was placed in the square.</t1536> <t1537>Six soldiers stood guard at the ballot box.</t1537>
<t1538>The election was conducted in an atmosphere of great freedom and maturity.</t1538>
<t1539>Aureliano, since he was with his father-in-law who did not leave the ballot box to ensure no one voted more than once, witnessed these free and mature elections with his own eyes.</t1539> <t1540>At four in the afternoon, the end of voting was announced with a drum played in the square.</t1540> <t1541>Don Apolinar Moscote sealed the ballot box, labeled it, and signed the label.</t1541> <t1542>That night, while playing dominoes with Aureliano, Moscote told the sergeant to break the seal of the ballot box and count the votes.</t1542> <t1543>From the box came an equal number of blue and red ballots, but the sergeant left only ten red ballots in the box and filled the difference with blue ones.</t1543> <t1544>Then they sealed the box again and sent it to the capital without delay the next morning.</t1544> <t1545>The Liberals will declare war, said Aureliano.</t1545>
<t1546>Don Apolinar was completely focused on the domino tiles.</t1546> <t1547>If you're saying this because we changed the votes, don't worry, they won't start a war, he said.</t1547> <t1548>We left a few red ballots so that no one would have anything to say.</t1548> <t1549>Aureliano was slowly understanding the disadvantages of being in opposition.</t1549> <t1550>If I were a liberal, I would go to war over these votes; he said.</t1550>
<t1551>His father-in-law scrutinized him over his glasses.</t1551>
<t1552>"What else, Aurelito," she said.</t1552> <t1553>"If you were a Liberal, you wouldn't even know that the ballots were changed, even though you're my son-in-law."</t1553>
<t1554>It wasn't the election results that stirred up the village, but the soldiers' refusal to return the weapons they had collected from the houses.</t1554> <t1555>A group of women gathered and went to Aureliano, asking him to request the bread knives from his father-in-law.</t1555> <t1556>Don Apolinar Moscote, as if revealing a state secret to his son-in-law, said that the soldiers had taken the knives to the capital as evidence of the Liberals' war preparations.</t1556> <t1557>This deceitful, treacherous attitude horrified Aureliano.</t1557> <t1558>He said nothing to his father-in-law.</t1558> <t1559>One evening, while Gerineldo Marquez and Magnifico Visbal were sitting with their friends discussing the matter of the knives, they turned to Aureliano and asked if he was a Liberal or a Conservative. He did not hesitate.</t1559>
<t1560>If I absolutely had to take a side, I would be a Liberal, he said.</t1560> <t1561>Conservatives are deceitful, scheming.</t1561>
<t1562>The next day, upon the insistence of his friends, he went to Dr. Alirio Noguera, supposedly to cure the pain in his liver.</t1562> <t1563>He couldn't understand why he needed to lie about being sick.</t1563> <t1564>Dr. Alirio Noguera had come to Macondo a few years earlier with a box of unpleasant pills and a saying that no one could make sense of: "A nail drives out another nail," he said.</t1564> <t1565>In fact, he was a charlatan.</t1565> <t1566>Behind the guise of a doctor with no respectability, a terrorist was hidden.</t1566> <t1567>The short boots he always wore covered the marks left on his legs by five years of shackles.</t1567> <t1568>He had been arrested during the first actions of the Federalists, had to disguise himself in the form he most opposed in the world, and managed to escape to Curaçao by wearing a priest's robe.</t1568> <t1569>After living in exile for many years, he could no longer stay put upon hearing news from fellow exiles spread across the Caribbean coming to Curaçao, and he took a smuggler's boat to Riohacha.</t1569>
<t1570>He had nothing but pills made of powdered sugar and a fake diploma from Leipzig University.</t1570><t1571>He cried in disappointment at what he saw.</t1571><t1572>The federalist force, which the smugglers had endlessly described as a boiling cauldron ready to explode, had dissipated in an obscure election frenzy.</t1572><t1573>The fake doctor, devastated by the defeat and seeking a place to spend the rest of his life in peace, took refuge in Macondo.</t1573><t1574>In a room filled with rented bottles of pills in a corner of the area, he lived off the desperate patients who, after trying every remedy, consoled themselves with sugar pills for a few years.</t1574><t1575>During the years when Don Apolinar Moscote supposedly governed, there was no sign of incitement from the doctor.</t1575><t1576>He spent his time reminiscing about the old days and battling asthma.</t1576><t1577>With the approach of the elections, things got out of hand, and the doctor found himself once again in the role of an agitator.</t1577><t1578>He established relationships with the village youths who were completely unaware of politics and continuously ran an incitement campaign.</t1578>
<t1579>The red votes that came out of the ballot box, which Don Apolinar Moscote interpreted as a youthful whim, were part of the doctor's plan.</t1579> <t1580>He had made the young people vote to prove that the elections were nothing but a sham.</t1580> <t1581>The only thing that has an effect is acts of violence, he would say.</t1581> <t1582>Most of Aureliano's friends intended to overthrow the Conservative government.</t1582> <t1583>However, no one dared to reveal their plans to him.</t1583> <t1584>They couldn't open up to him not only because he was the son-in-law of the justice of the peace, but also because he was a reserved person.</t1584> <t1585>Moreover, it was known that he voted blue just because his father-in-law wanted him to.</t1585>
<t1586>In short, Aureliano's political stance was revealed purely by chance.</t1586> <t1587>Although he was perfectly healthy, he went to the doctor out of sheer curiosity.</t1587> <t1588>When Aureliano entered the room that smelled of camphor and was covered in cobwebs, he found himself in front of someone who resembled a dusty lizard, wheezing with every breath.</t1588> <t1589>Without asking him anything, the doctor took him to the window and looked inside his lower eyelid.</t1589> <t1590>Aureliano said, "My problem isn't there."</t1590>
<t1591>They had advised him to say it that way.</t1591> <t1592>Pressing the tips of his fingers against his liver, he added, The pain that keeps me awake at night is right here.</t1592> <t1593>Upon this, Dr. Noguera, pretending to be bothered by the sun, closed the curtain and explained to Aureliano in simple terms that he could understand that killing the Conservatives was an act of patriotism.</t1593>
<t1594>Aureliano carried a bottle of medicine in his shirt pocket for several days.</t1594> <t1595>Every two hours, he would take out the bottle, take three pills into his palm, and slowly suck on them.</t1595> <t1596>Don Apolinar Moscote mocked him for believing in old wives' remedies, but those involved in the assassination plot considered him one of their own.</t1596>
<t1597>Although none of them knew exactly what action would be taken, almost all the sons of the village founders had joined the organization.</t1597> <t1598>However, the day the doctor hinted at what was to be done, Aureliano understood the plan.</t1598> <t1599>Although he believed that the Conservative regime needed to be overthrown, he was frightened by what was being planned.</t1599> <t1600>Dr. Noguera was fixated on assassinations.</t1600> <t1601>These actions, which would be triggered at the push of a button, would plan the killing of individuals one by one, ultimately leading to the elimination of those running the regime along with their families, especially their children, thereby eradicating Conservatism throughout the country.</t1601> <t1602>Needless to say, Don Apolinar Moscote, his wife, and his six daughters were also on this list.</t1602>
<t1603>Aureliano, without losing his composure, said to the doctor, "You are not a liberal at all."</t1603> <t1604>"You can be nothing but a butcher."</t1604>
<t1605>The doctor responded with the same calmness.</t1605> <t1606>Then give the bottle back.</t1606> <t1607>You don't need it anymore.</t1607>
<t1608>Aureliano only learned six months later that the doctor had looked at his emotional, introverted, solitary nature and concluded that he could not be a man of action.</t1608> <t1609>His friends did not leave Aureliano's side, fearing he might reveal their plans, so they did not leave him alone.</t1609> <t1610>Aureliano reassured them.</t1610> <t1611>He would not let a single word slip, but on the night they came to kill Moscote and his family, they would find him standing guard at the door.</t1611> <t1612>He was so determined in this matter that the assassination plan was postponed to an indefinite date.</t1612> <t1613>It was precisely during those days that Ursula asked what he thought about Pietro Crespi and Amaranta getting married, and Aureliano said it was not the time to worry about their marriage.</t1613> <t1614>For a week, he had been carrying an old model pistol under his shirt; he did not take his eyes off his friends.</t1614> <t1615>In the evenings, he would go to visit Jose Arcadio and Rebeca, who had started tidying up their house, and after seven o'clock, he would play dominoes with his father-in-law.</t1615> <t1616>At lunch, he would chat with Arcadio, who was now a grown young man, and he saw that Arcadio was very excited about the outbreak of war.</t1616> <t1617>In the school where Arcadio taught, there were students older than him.</t1617> <t1618>Those who had just learned to speak and those with hair and beard intertwined were together.</t1618> <t1619>The fire of liberalism had also engulfed the school.</t1619> <t1620>There was talk of Father Nicanor being shot, the church being turned into a school, and free love being recognized.</t1620>
<t1621>Aureliano tried to calm Arcadio's outbursts.</t1621> <t1622>He advised him to be sensible and moderate.</t1622> <t1623>However, Arcadio ignored his rational views and realism, and accused him of being unprincipled in front of everyone.</t1623> <t1624>Aureliano waited.</t1624> <t1625>Finally, one day in early December, Ursula, trembling all over, burst into the workshop.</t1625>
<t1626>-The war has broken out!</t1626>
<t1627>In fact, it had been three months since the war broke out.</t1627> <t1628>Martial law had been declared throughout the country.</t1628> <t1629>The only one who received news of the events immediately was Don Apolinar Moscote.</t1629> <t1630>But even though the army unit that occupied the village had set out, he hadn't even told his wife about it.</t1630> <t1631>The soldiers entered the village before dawn with two light cannons pulled by mules and set up headquarters in the school.</t1631> <t1632>A curfew was imposed after six in the evening.</t1632> <t1633>A much more meticulous investigation was conducted than the previous one, all the houses were searched thoroughly, and even agricultural tools were confiscated.</t1633> <t1634>They dragged Dr. Noguera to the square, tied him to a tree, and shot him without any questioning.</t1634> <t1635>Father Nicanor tried to impress the military authorities with the miracle of flying, but one of the soldiers brought down the butt of his rifle, splitting his head in two.</t1635> <t1636>The enthusiasm of the Liberals turned into this silent horror.</t1636> <t1637>The sallow-faced, silent Aureliano continued his domino games with his father-in-law.</t1637>
<t1638>Although he held the title of the so-called civilian and military leader of the village, Don Apolinar Moscote had once again lost his respect.</t1638> <t1639>The decisions were made by the captain, and extraordinary meetings were held every morning to maintain order.</t1639> <t1640>One day, four of the soldiers under his command forcibly took a woman who had been bitten by a rabid dog from her family and beat her to death with their rifle butts.</t1640> <t1641>Two weeks after the occupation, on a Sunday, Aureliano went to Gerineldo Marquez's house and, in his usual taciturn manner, asked for a cup of plain coffee.</t1641> <t1642>When the two were alone in the kitchen, Aureliano spoke in a tone of authority that had been unfamiliar until that day.</t1642> <t1643>Prepare the boys, he said.</t1643> <t1644>We are going to war.</t1644>
<t1645>Gerineldo Marquez couldn't believe his ears.</t1645>
<t1646>"With which weapons?" he asked.</t1646>
<t1647>Aureliano replied, With their weapons.</t1647>
<t1648>In a reckless move made at midnight on Tuesday, twenty-one men under the command of Aureliano Buendia, none of whom were over thirty years old, armed with kitchen knives and sharpened tools, stormed the garrison, seized the weapons, and executed the captain and the four soldiers who had killed the woman in the garden.</t1648>
<t1649>That night, before the sound of the firing squad's bullets had even died down, Arcadio was declared the military and civil leader of the village.</t1649> <t1650>Those among the insurgents who were married only had enough time to bid farewell to their wives.</t1650> <t1651>Leaving them behind, they set out at dawn amidst the enthusiastic demonstrations of the people liberated from terror.</t1651> <t1652>According to the latest news, they were heading to join the forces of the revolutionary General Victoria Medina, who had set out towards Manaure.</t1652> <t1653>Before leaving, Aureliano brought Don Apolinar Moscote out from the closet where he had been hiding.</t1653> <t1654>Don't be afraid, father, he said.</t1654> <t1655>The new government promises to protect your and your family's personal safety.</t1655> <t1656>Don Apolinar Moscote couldn't determine whether this rebel, wearing long boots and carrying a rifle over his shoulder, was the same person he used to play dominoes with in the evenings.</t1656>
<t1657>This is madness, Aurelito, he shouted.</t1657>
<t1658>Aureliano said, It's not madness, it's war.</t1658> <t1659>And don't call me Aurelito anymore.</t1659> <t1660>I'm now Colonel Aureliano Buendia.</t1660>
<t1661>Colonel Aureliano Buendia organized thirty-two armed uprisings, and he was defeated in all of them.</t1661> <t1662>He had seventeen sons by seventeen different women, and before the oldest reached thirty-five, all seventeen were killed in one night.</t1662> <t1663>He survived fourteen assassination attempts, seventy-three ambushes, and a firing squad.</t1663>
<t1664>He drank strychnine-laced coffee strong enough to knock out a big horse and still managed to save his skin.</t1664><t1665>He refused the medal of merit given by the President.</t1665><t1666>He rose to the command of the revolutionary forces, his fame spread from one border to the other throughout the country, he became the person the government feared the most, and he never allowed his picture to be taken.</t1666><t1667>After the war, he refused the lifelong pension that was offered to him and even in his old age, he earned his living by making silver fish in his workshop in Macondo.</t1667><t1668>Despite leading his men into battle in every skirmish, the only wound he received was by his own hand.</t1668>
<t1669>After signing the Neerlandia Treaty, which ended the twenty-year-long civil war, he shot himself in the chest, but the bullet missed all vital organs and exited through his back as smoothly as pulling a hair from butter.</t1669> <t1670>The only thing that remained from all this was a street in Macondo that bore his name.</t1670> <t1671>However, a few years before he died of old age, as he himself said, at dawn when he set out with twenty-one companions to join General Victorio Medina's forces, he did not expect any of this.</t1671>
<t1672>All he said to Arcadio before leaving was, "We are leaving Macondo to you."</t1672> <t1673>"We are leaving it in good condition; let us find it in better condition when we return."</t1673>
<t1674>Arcadio interpreted these words as it suited him.</t1674> <t1675>He fashioned a uniform for himself.</t1675> <t1676>He rummaged through Melquiades' books from cover to cover and acquired a jacket with braids and epaulettes, like a marshal's uniform.</t1676> <t1677>He secured the sword at his waist with gold-embroidered buckles he had stripped from the captain who was executed by firing squad.</t1677> <t1678>He positioned the cannons at the entrance of the village, outfitted his former students who had risen up with uniforms, and sent them out into the streets with fiery speeches.</t1678> <t1679>In this way, he wanted to create the impression among outsiders that the village was defended by invincible forces.</t1679> <t1680>This was a double-edged deception.</t1680> <t1681>Because the government did not dare to attack the village for ten months; and when they finally did, they attacked with such overwhelming force that the village's resistance crumbled within half an hour.</t1681>
<t1682>Arcadio revealed his keen interest in proclamations from the very first day of his administration.</t1682> <t1683>He would announce everything that came to his mind through a proclamation, sometimes issuing four decrees in a single day.</t1683> <t1684>He mandated compulsory military service for those over eighteen, declared that animals wandering aimlessly after six in the evening would be considered public property, and required the elderly to wear a red armband.</t1684>
<t1685>He had cornered Peder Nicanor, threatened him with torture, and forbidden him from holding services and ringing the bell unless it was for the victory of the Liberals.</t1685> <t1686>To ensure that no one doubted the decisiveness of his orders, he lined up a firing squad in the village square and had a scarecrow shot.</t1686> <t1687>At first, no one took him seriously.</t1687> <t1688>After all, they were all just children pretending to be important.</t1688>
<t1689>But one night, Arcadio went to Catarino's shop.</t1689> <t1690>When the drummer saw him, he greeted him with a tremolo.</t1690> <t1691>The people there started to laugh.</t1691> <t1692>Arcadio, losing his temper, had the drummer shot for disrespecting the authorities.</t1692> <t1693>He also imprisoned those who opposed this by locking their ankles between two logs and giving them nothing but bread and water.</t1693> <t1694>When Ursula learned of his arbitrary actions, she would shout in his face, Murderer!</t1694> <t1695>When Aureliano hears about what you've done, he'll have you shot, and I'll be the first to cheer.</t1695>
<t1696>But what was the use of her wishes?</t1696> <t1697>Arcadio increased the pressure more and more, squeezing the people tighter and tighter.</t1697> <t1698>In the end, he became the most ruthless ruler Macondo had ever seen.</t1698> <t1699>One day, Don Apolinar Moscote made a mistake, saying they should beat their heads against the wall to understand the difference.</t1699> <t1700>This is what they call the Liberal paradise, he said.</t1700> <t1701>These words reached Arcadio's ears.</t1701> <t1702>He led the patrols to raid Moscote's house, smashed his belongings, and had his daughters whipped.</t1702> <t1703>He dragged Don Apolinar Moscote outside.</t1703>
<t1704>When Ursula heard what had happened, she went mad with rage.</t1704> <t1705>Grabbing the whip, she ran to the headquarters, shouting and cursing.</t1705> <t1706>As Ursula burst into the courtyard, Arcadio was about to give the order to fire.</t1706>
<t1707>"Hit me if you dare, you son of a bitch!" he shouted.</t1707>
<t1708>Without giving Arcadio a chance to open his mouth, she shouted, "Alright, you murderer!" and brought down the whip.</t1708> <t1709>"Kill me too, son of a devilish mother."</t1709> <t1710>"Kill me so I don't have to shed tears for raising a monster."</t1710> <t1711>Ursula was bringing down the whip mercilessly.</t1711> <t1712>Whipping and whipping, she chased Arcadio to the back yard.</t1712> <t1713>Arcadio shrank into a corner like a snail retreating into its shell.</t1713> <t1714>Don Apolinar Moscote, who was tied to the place where the scarecrow they had previously shot and shattered for fun stood, had passed out.</t1714> <t1715>The children in the firing squad scattered like chicks out of fear that Ursula might beat them too.</t1715> <t1716>However, Ursula didn't even look at them.</t1716> <t1717>She left Arcadio, whose collar and cuffs were torn, screaming in pain and anger; she went and untied Don Apolinar Moscote and took him home.</t1717> <t1718>Before leaving the headquarters, she also freed the prisoners who were tied to the log.</t1718>
<t1719>From that day on, Ursula was the one who governed the village.</t1719> <t1720>She reinstated the Sunday masses, opposed the wearing of red armbands, and repealed the absurd decrees.</t1720> <t1721>Despite all her strength, she couldn't help but cry over the misfortunes that befell her.</t1721>
<t1722>She felt such great loneliness that she even sought solace in chatting with her husband forgotten under the chestnut tree.</t1722> <t1723>On days when the June rains poured as if they would destroy the arbor, she would say, Look at what we've come to.</t1723> <t1724>Look at this house, it's deserted.</t1724> <t1725>Our children have scattered in all directions.</t1725> <t1726>It's just you and me left alone.</t1726>
<t1727>Just like in the beginning.</t1727> <t1728>Jose Arcadio Buendia, who had already fallen into the abyss of unconsciousness, couldn't even hear what his wife was saying.</t1728> <t1729>In the early days of his madness, he would express his daily needs in Latin words.</t1729>
<t1730>When the clouds enveloping his mind parted, even if only for short periods, he spoke sensibly, and when Amaranta brought his meal, he would tell her what was bothering him and did not object to her applying a mustard plaster or using a cupping glass on his back.</t1730>
<t1731>When Ama Ursula started to lament to him, Jose Arcadio Buendia had already severed all ties with reality.</t1731><t1732>Ursula would slowly clean him without moving him from his place, while also informing him about what was happening within the family.</t1732><t1733>As she scrubbed her husband's back with soap, she would say, "It's been four months since Aureliano went to war, and we haven't heard a word from him."</t1733><t1734>"Jose Arcadio has grown into a big man and returned."</t1734><t1735>"Even you would look small next to him."</t1735><t1736>"He has tattoos all over his body."</t1736><t1737>"Unfortunately, he does nothing but bring us disgrace."</t1737><t1738>Upon hearing the bad news, Ursula felt as if her husband was saddened.</t1738><t1739>So she decided to lie to him.</t1739><t1740>While sprinkling ash over her husband's filth before scooping it up with a shovel, she said, "Look, I'll tell you something you won't believe."</t1740><t1741>"God wanted Jose Arcadio and Rebeca to get married, and now there's no end to their happiness."</t1741>
<t1742>He narrated these deceptions so sincerely that in the end, he began to console himself with his own lies.</t1742> <t1743>Arcadio has become a dignified man now, he would say.</t1743> <t1744>Brave, oh so brave.</t1744> <t1745>Handsome, oh so handsome.</t1745> <t1746>You should see him when he puts on his uniform and straps on his sword.</t1746> <t1747>Yet, talking to him was no different than talking to a dead man, indistinguishable.</t1747> <t1748>Because Jose Arcadio Buendia seemed so harmless, so withdrawn from the world, that Ursula decided to unravel him.</t1748> <t1749>After being unraveled, Jose Arcadio Buendia did not move from his place.</t1749> <t1750>As if bound to the trunk of the chestnut tree by invisible bonds stronger than any rope, he sat there, come rain or shine.</t1750> <t1751>Towards August, when it seemed like winter would never end, Ursula was able to give him news that resembled reality:</t1751>
<t1752>"Do you still believe our luck is holding?" he began.</t1752> <t1753>Amaranta and the Italian lantern maker are getting married.</t1753>
<t1754>Indeed, Amaranta and Pietro Crespi had advanced their friendship.</t1754> <t1755>Ursula, who no longer saw it necessary to watch over them, also supported this friendship.</t1755> <t1756>Everyone considered them as engaged.</t1756> <t1757>The Italian, arriving at dusk with a daffodil pinned to his jacket collar, would translate Petrarch's poems for Amaranta.</t1757> <t1758>They would sit on the terrace, breathless from the scent of hydrangeas and roses, -without caring whether there was a war or bad news- one reading poetry, the other sewing lace cuffs, until the mosquitoes made the terrace uninhabitable and drove them inside.</t1758> <t1759>Amaranta's sensible and modest demeanor, her measured but sincere closeness, had woven an invisible net around her fiancé.</t1759> <t1760>When Pietro Crespi reluctantly left the house at eight in the evening, it was as if he was pushing aside this net with his ringless fingers to make his way.</t1760>
<t1761>They had placed the postcards sent to Pietro Crespi from Italy into an album.</t1761> <t1762>The cards had pictures of lovers holding hands in deserted parks.</t1762> <t1763>In the corners, there were hearts pierced by arrows and golden ribbons in the beaks of doves.</t1763> <t1764>While looking at the cards, Pietro Crespi would say, I went to this park when I was in Florence.</t1764> <t1765>In that park, you take some feed in your hand, open your palm, and feed the birds.</t1765>
<t1766>Sometimes, while looking at a watercolor painting of Venice, homesickness would turn the slimy mud of the canals, the acrid smell of sticky jellyfish, into the scent of flowers in the Italian's nose.</t1766> <t1767>Amaranta would sigh, laugh, and dream of a second homeland filled with old cities where handsome men and beautiful women speaking a childlike language lived, and where only cats roamed the garbage dumps of its former glory.</t1767> <t1768>After crossing oceans to find this second homeland and confusing passion with love through Rebeca's fiery embraces, Pietro Crespi had found true love.</t1768> <t1769>Alongside happiness, he had also attained wealth.</t1769>
<t1770>At that time, his shop and warehouse extended from one street to the next.</t1770> <t1771>From a model of the bell tower in Florence, which rang tiny bells every hour, to musical boxes from Sorrento, to cabinets from China that played music when opened, the shop had everything imaginable, from every kind of musical instrument to every conceivable type of mechanical toy.</t1771> <t1772>Since Pietro Crespi could only spare time to give music lessons, the shop was run by his brother, Bruna Crespi.</t1772> <t1773>Thanks to Pietro Crespi, Turkish Street turned into an oasis of melodies that made people forget Arcadio's whimsical behavior and the nightmare of the distant war.</t1773> <t1774>When Ursula resumed the Sunday services, Pietro Crespi donated a small German organ to the church.</t1774> <t1775>He formed a children's choir and prepared a Gregorian repertoire that added grandeur to Father Nicanor's silent mass.</t1775>
<t1776>There was no doubt that Amaranta was his perfect match.</t1776> <t1777>Without forcing their emotions, letting themselves flow naturally, they reached a point where there was nothing left to do but set the wedding date.</t1777> <t1778>There were no obstacles in their way.</t1778> <t1779>Ursula, feeling guilty for having altered Rebeca's fate by continually postponing her marriage, was now careful to avoid creating new sorrows.</t1779> <t1780>The progression of the war, Aureliano's absence, Arcadio's cruelty, and the expulsion of Jose Arcadio and Rebeca from the house had pushed the mourning for Remedios into the background.</t1780> <t1781>In those days leading up to the wedding, Pietro Crespi had begun to accept Aureliano Jose, who was as attached to him as a son, as his first child.</t1781> <t1782>All the events around her seemed to herald a carefree, perfect happiness for Amaranta.</t1782> <t1783>However, unlike Rebeca, Amaranta showed no signs of excitement.</t1783>
<t1784>Just as she patiently dyes the tablecloths, quietly knits the unique lace, and embroiders peacocks from needlework, she was eagerly awaiting the moment when Pietro Crespi could no longer ignore the voice of his heart.</t1784> <t1785>The day she had been waiting for came with the autumn rains at the beginning of October.</t1785> <t1786>Pietro Crespi took the sewing box from her lap and said, "We are getting married next month."</t1786> <t1787>When his ice-cold hands touched hers, Amaranta did not shiver at all.</t1787> <t1788>Like a docile animal, she withdrew her hands and continued her work.</t1788>
<t1789>"Simplification," Crespi said, smiling.</t1789> <t1790>"I wouldn't marry you even if I died."</t1790>
<t1791>Pietro Crespi could no longer hold himself back.</t1791> <t1792>Without any shame, he was sobbing uncontrollably, rubbing his hands in despair as if he would break his fingers.</t1792> <t1793>But he couldn't soften Amaranta.</t1793>
<t1794>-Don't waste your time.</t1794> <t1795>If you really love me, you will never set foot in this house again, she said, and no other words came out of the girl's mouth.</t1795> <t1796>Ursula was dying of shame.</t1796> <t1797>Pietro Crespi did not tire of begging and pleading.</t1797> <t1798>He was willing to endure all kinds of disgrace and humiliation.</t1798> <t1799>He spent an entire day with his head resting on Ursula's lap, crying.</t1799> <t1800>Ursula was ready to give her life if it would console him.</t1800>
<t1801>On rainy nights, Crespi would open his umbrella and circle around the house to see the light in Amaranta's room.</t1801> <t1802>He had never dressed as well as he did in those days.</t1802> <t1803>He held his head, reminiscent of an emperor who had lost his throne and crown, with a peculiar dignity and nobility.</t1803> <t1804>He went to the friends who sat on the terrace with Amaranta, doing embroidery.</t1804> <t1805>He left no stone unturned to persuade Amaranta.</t1805> <t1806>He neglected his work.</t1806> <t1807>He would lock himself in the room at the back of the shop from morning till night, writing passionate letters and sending them to Amaranta with dried flower petals and butterflies.</t1807> <t1808>But Amaranta would send the letters back unopened.</t1808> <t1809>Crespi would retreat to a corner and play the lute for hours.</t1809> <t1810>One night, he even sang.</t1810> <t1811>The divine melodies from the strings of the lute, resembling a qanun, and the soulful voice that could serve as proof that no one in the world could love this much, awakened the entire Macondo.</t1811> <t1812>All the lights in the village were lit one by one, except for Amaranta's.</t1812> <t1813>On the second of November, All Saints' Day, Crespi's brother opened the shop.</t1813>
<t1814>All the lights were on.</t1814> <t1815>All the musical boxes were open.</t1815> <t1816>All the clocks were striking an endless hour.</t1816> <t1817>And amidst this crazy music, they found Pietro Crespi at the back of the shop, having slit his wrists with a razor and dipped his hands in a bowl of basil.</t1817>
<t1818>Ursula announced that the deceased would stay in their house that night, a vigil would be held, and the traditional funeral meal would be eaten.</t1818> <t1819>Father Nicanor opposed holding a religious ceremony and burying the deceased in consecrated ground.</t1819> <t1820>Ursula confronted him.</t1820> <t1821>He was a holy man in a way that neither you nor I can comprehend, she said.</t1821> <t1822>Therefore, whether you like it or not, I will bury him next to Melquiades.</t1822> <t1823>And she did; with the support of the entire village and a grand funeral ceremony.</t1823> <t1824>Amaranta did not leave her bedroom.</t1824>
<t1825>From where she lay, she could hear Ursula crying, the footsteps and whispers of the crowd filling the house, and the lamentations of the mourners.</t1825> <t1826>Then a silence began that smelled of crushed flowers.</t1826> <t1827>For a long time, as evening fell, Amaranta continued to smell the lavender scent of Pietro Crespi.</t1827> <t1828>But she was strong enough not to lose herself.</t1828> <t1829>Ursula had left her to her own devices and had stopped caring.</t1829> <t1830>Even when one afternoon, Amaranta went to the kitchen, plunged her hand into the coals in the stove, and burned her hand until she felt a pain beyond the smell of her burning flesh, Ursula did not turn her head to look.</t1830> <t1831>Remorse could not be alleviated this way.</t1831> <t1832>For a few days, Amaranta walked around the house with her hand wrapped in an egg white poultice.</t1832> <t1833>It was as if the egg white had seared not only the burn on her hand but also the wounds in her heart.</t1833>
<t1834>The only visible trace left from this tragedy was the black cloth wrapped around Amaranta's burned hand, which she never removed until her death.</t1834> <t1835>Arcadio showed an unexpected act of humanity by declaring official mourning for Pietro Crespi.</t1835> <t1836>Ursula interpreted this behavior as the return of the lamb that had strayed from the flock.</t1836> <t1837>However, she was mistaken.</t1837> <t1838>She had lost Arcadio not on the day he donned the uniform, but from the very beginning.</t1838> <t1839>She thought she had raised Arcadio just as she had raised Rebeca, without any difference, treating him no differently than her own children.</t1839> <t1840>However, during the insomnia plague, amidst Ursula's efforts to repair the house, when Jose Arcadio Buendia lost his mind, during Aureliano's days of seclusion, and throughout the deadly feud between Amaranta and Rebeca, Arcadio had always lived a lonely and timid childhood.</t1840> <t1841>Indeed, Aureliano had taught him to read and write.</t1841> <t1842>But he did so as if teaching a stranger, without genuine interest, his mind always elsewhere.</t1842>
<t1843>He also gave his clothes that had become unwearable to Arcadio, and Visitacion would alter and shorten them to fit the child.</t1843> <t1844>Throughout his childhood, Arcadio suffered the discomfort of wearing oversized shoes, patched pants, and feminine hips.</t1844> <t1845>He spoke with Visitacion and Cataure in their language, unable to communicate with anyone else as he did with them.</t1845> <t1846>The only person who truly cared for him was Melquiades, who read him his incomprehensible writings and taught him the art of photography.</t1846>
<t1847>Nobody knew that when Melquiades died, Arcadio secretly shed tears and tried to revive him by futilely examining his writings.</t1847> <t1848>The period when he was a respected and beloved teacher, followed by his time in power with endless decrees and a mottled uniform, freed him from the weight of an old sorrow.</t1848> <t1849>One night in Catarino's shop, someone dared to say, "You are not worthy of the surname you carry."</t1849> <t1850>Contrary to what was expected, Arcadio did not have the man executed by firing squad.</t1850>
<t1851>-Not being a Buendia is the greatest honor for me, he said.</t1851>
<t1852>Those who knew the secret about Arcadio's parents thought, upon hearing these words, that he also knew the truth.</t1852> <t1853>But Arcadio had never learned this truth.</t1853> <t1854>In the dark room, Pilar Ternera, who had once been an irresistible passion first for Jose Arcadio and then for Aureliano, was now the same for Arcadio: Even though her former beauty was gone and her laughter no longer echoed as it used to, Arcadio always sought her out and found her by the scent of smoky mist.</t1854> <t1855>Shortly before the war, when Pilar Ternera came a little later than usual to pick up her young son from school, she found Arcadio waiting for her in the room where he took his afternoon naps and later shackled the prisoners to the log.</t1855> <t1856>The child was playing in the garden, and Arcadio, knowing that Pilar Ternera had to pass through that room, lay in his hammock, trembling as he waited.</t1856> <t1857>The woman arrived.</t1857> <t1858>Arcadio grabbed her by the wrist and tried to pull her into the hammock.</t1858>
<t1859>Pilar Ternera was horrified.</t1859> <t1860>No! No! she was saying.</t1860> <t1861>You can't know how much I want to make you happy, but God knows, I can't do it.</t1861>
<t1862>Arcadio, with the strength inherited from his ancestors, grabbed the woman by the waist, and the moment he touched her skin, the world vanished from his sight.</t1862> <t1863>Don't try to put on an act of decency, he said.</t1863>
<t1864>Who doesn't know about her promiscuity?</t1864> <t1865>Pilar cursed her bad fate inwardly.</t1865>
<t1866>"The children might see," he whispered.</t1866> <t1867>"It's best not to bolt the door tonight."</t1867>
<t1868>That night Arcadio waited for her in his hammock, trembling with fever.</t1868> <t1869>He couldn't sleep, listening intently.</t1869> <t1870>In the endless hours of the approaching dawn, as he heard the sound of crickets and the merciless cries of curlews heralding the new day, he became increasingly convinced that he had been deceived.</t1870> <t1871>Just as his excitement turned to anger, the door suddenly opened.</t1871> <t1872>Months later, as Arcadio stood before the firing squad, he would remember the footsteps in the classroom, the slamming of desks, the weight of a body leaning against his in the darkness of the room, and the breath from another heart mingling with his own.</t1872> <t1873>Arcadio reached out his hand and in the darkness caught another hand with two rings on two fingers, just as it was about to slip away.</t1873> <t1874>He felt the veins on the hand, sensed the heartbeat of an ill-fated heart in the wrist, and touched the palm where the lifeline was cut by the claw of death at the base of the thumb.</t1874>
<t1875>Then, he realized that she was not the woman he was expecting, because she smelled of flowers, not smoky steam, and she had tiny, unseen breasts like a man's chest, a crotch as hard and round as a walnut, and a tenderness tinged with the excitement of inexperience.</t1875><t1876>She was a virgin girl and her name was Santa Sofia de la Piedad.</t1876><t1877>Pilar Ternera had given her half of the money she had saved and set aside all her life, exactly fifty pesos, to do what she did to him.</t1877><t1878>Arcadio had seen her many times while she worked at his father's small cookshop, but he had never looked at her with interest; because the girl had the rare virtue of not making her presence felt to those around her except when necessary.</t1878><t1879>But after that day, Arcadio couldn't help but snuggle up to the warmth of her armpits like a cat.</t1879><t1880>When it was time for her siesta, she went to school.</t1880><t1881>Her parents knew and didn't say anything.</t1881><t1882>Pilar Ternera had given them the other half of her life savings as well.</t1882>
<t1883>Later, when the government troops evicted them from their love nests, they began to make love between the lard cans and corn sacks at the back of the shop.</t1883> <t1884>At the time Arcadio was appointed as the civil and military leader of the village, they had a daughter.</t1884> <t1885>The only relatives who knew about this situation were Jose Arcadio and Rebeca.</t1885> <t1886>During that period, Arcadio was close to them more out of a sense of complicity than kinship.</t1886> <t1887>Jose Arcadio had surrendered himself to the yoke of marriage.</t1887> <t1888>Rebeca's strong personality, insatiable appetite, and relentless ambition were draining all the strength from her husband, who had transformed from a woman-chasing idler into a beast of burden.</t1888> <t1889>They had a neat and tidy house.</t1889> <t1890>At sunrise, Rebeca would open up the entire house, and as the wind from the cemetery blew in through the windows and out through the doors into the garden, it would infuse the walls and furniture with the scent of graveyard saltpeter.</t1890> <t1891>Rebeca's craving for eating earth, the rattling of her parents' bones, and the boiling of her blood in the face of Pietro Crespi's ineptitude were all things of the distant past.</t1891> <t1892>Rebeca would sit by the window all day, embroidering, far removed from the anxieties of the war.</t1892> <t1893>When the dishes in the cupboard began to clink against each other, she would get up and start cooking.</t1893> <t1894>Long before the giant with boots, spurs, and a shotgun, sometimes with a deer slung over his shoulder, but more often with strings of wild ducks and rabbits dangling from his hands, appeared in the distance, Rebeca would have the meal ready.</t1894>
<t1895>One afternoon, during the early days of his administration, Arcadio showed up.</t1895> <t1896>They had not seen him since they left home, but he had such a sincere and friendly demeanor that Rebeca and Jose Arcadio did not hesitate to invite him to their table.</t1896>
<t1897>While they were eating their meals and drinking their coffee, Arcadio explained the reason for his visit.</t1897> <t1898>There was a complaint about Jose Arcadio.</t1898> <t1899>It was said that Jose Arcadio had started by plowing his own field, then driving the oxen forward, crushing fences and demolishing structures, he forcibly took over the most fertile lands in the area.</t1899> <t1900>He had also imposed a tribute on the owners of the lands he did not find appealing.</t1900> <t1901>Every Saturday, he would take his hunting dogs, sling his shotgun over his shoulder, and go out to collect the tribute.</t1901> <t1902>Jose Arcadio did not deny his actions.</t1902>
<t1903>-These lands were distributed by Jose Arcadio Buendia during the establishment of the village.</t1903> <t1904>In fact, it is said that his madness began at that time because he squandered this inheritance, which actually belonged to the family, and for this reason, he saw himself justified in seizing those lands.</t1904> <t1905>Such a defense was actually unnecessary, because Arcadio had not come to see justice served.</t1905> <t1906>He had come to negotiate with Jose Arcadio.</t1906> <t1907>In order for the title deeds of the seized lands to be transferred to Jose Arcadio, Arcadio would establish a cadastral office, and in return, Jose Arcadio would agree to let the local government representatives collect the tribute.</t1907> <t1908>They reached an agreement.</t1908> <t1909>Years later, while Colonel Aureliano Buendia was examining the title records, he saw that all the lands, including the cemetery, from the hill where Jose Arcadio's garden was located to as far as the eye could see, were registered in his brother's name.</t1909> <t1910>And he learned that during Arcadio's eleven-month rule, not only had he collected the tributes, but he had also taken money from the families of the deceased so they could bury their dead on Jose Arcadio's land.</t1910>
<t1911>Ursula learned about these events, which were the talk of the entire village, only a few months later.</t1911> <t1912>Everyone was hiding what had happened to avoid adding to her sorrow.</t1912> <t1913>At first, Ursula was suspicious.</t1913> <t1914>While she was giving her husband spoonfuls of pumpkin syrup, she said, supposedly with a puffed-up chest, Arcadio is building a house.</t1914> <t1915>She said it, but then she couldn't help but sigh and added, I don't know, but I have a feeling something is going to come out of this.</t1915> <t1916>Later, when she learned that Arcadio was not only building a house but also ordering furniture from Vienna, she became convinced that he was spending the money he had collected for public services.</t1916> <t1917>After a Sunday mass, when she saw Arcadio sitting in his new house playing cards with his officials, she shouted, You are the disgrace of our family!</t1917> <t1918>Arcadio paid her no mind.</t1918> <t1919>It was only then that Ursula learned he had a six-month-old daughter and that Santa Sofia de la Piedad, with whom he was living, was pregnant again.</t1919> <t1920>Upon this, she decided to find Colonel Aureliano Buendia wherever he was and write him a letter to inform him of what had happened.</t1920>
<t1921>However, the rapidly unfolding events of the days not only prevented Ursula from implementing her plans but also made her regret even thinking about them.</t1921> <t1922>Until then, the word war, which served only to describe a vague and distant event, suddenly became a concrete and dramatic reality.</t1922> <t1923>Towards the end of February, an old woman with an ashen face, riding a donkey loaded with brooms, arrived in Macondo.</t1923> <t1924>She had such a harmless appearance that the guards, thinking she was one of the vendors from the swamp villages, let her into the village without any questioning.</t1924> <t1925>The woman went straight to the barracks.</t1925> <t1926>Arcadio received her in the room that had once been a classroom.</t1926> <t1927>Now it had been turned into a sort of rear headquarters, with hammocks coiled and folded on hooks, beds piled in the corners, and rifles, carbines, and even hunting shotguns spread out on the floor.</t1927> <t1928>Before the old woman introduced herself, she straightened up, stood at attention, and gave a military salute.</t1928>
<t1929>-Lieutenant Colonel Gregorio Stevenson.</t1929>
<t1930>He had brought bad news.</t1930> <t1931>According to him, the last strongholds of the Liberals had also begun to fall.</t1931> <t1932>Colonel Aureliano Buendia, whom he had left fighting while retreating near Riohacha, had sent a message to Arcadio.</t1932> <t1933>He would surrender the village without resistance, on the condition that the lives and property of the Liberals would be protected.</t1933> <t1934>Arcadio looked pityingly at this strange messenger who resembled lost grandmothers.</t1934>
<t1935>"Of course, you brought something written," he said.</t1935>
<t1936>"Of course, I didn't bring anything like that," said the messenger.</t1936> <t1937>"You should know that under these circumstances, one cannot carry anything that would give them away."</t1937>
<t1938>While speaking, he reached into his bosom and took out a small fish made of silver.</t1938> <t1939>I think this is enough, he said.</t1939> <t1940>Arcadio recognized the fish as soon as he saw it, knowing it had come from Colonel Aureliano Buendía's hand.</t1940> <t1941>However, someone could have taken or stolen it before the war.</t1941> <t1942>A man could not be trusted blindly just because he had a silver fish in his bosom.</t1942> <t1943>The messenger went so far as to reveal a military secret to prove his identity.</t1943> <t1944>He explained that he had gone to Curaçao on duty, would gather all the exiles in the Caribbean region, and would find enough weapons and ammunition to launch an assault by the end of the year.</t1944> <t1945>Since Colonel Aureliano Buendía believed that this plan would succeed, he was against unnecessary loss of life at that moment.</t1945> <t1946>But Arcadio was adamant and unyielding.</t1946> <t1947>He arrested the messenger and had him shackled until he could prove his identity, deciding to defend the village to the last drop of his blood.</t1947>
<t1948>He didn't have to wait long.</t1948> <t1949>News of the Liberals' defeat was increasing day by day.</t1949> <t1950>Towards the end of March, at dawn when untimely rains were pouring down, the tense silence of the past weeks was broken by the bitter sound of a trumpet and the cannon noise that destroyed the church's tower.</t1950> <t1951>In fact, Arcadio's decision to resist was sheer madness.</t1951>
<t1952>Supposedly, he had fifty armed men, and each man had twenty bullets.</t1952> <t1953>Even so, former students, spurred on by Arcadio's boasts, had committed themselves wholeheartedly to the lost cause.</t1953> <t1954>Amid the sounds of boots on the roads, clashing commands, earth-shaking cannons, whizzing bullets, and the harsh sounds of bugles, a man called Colonel Stevenson found an opportunity to speak with Arcadio.</t1954> <t1955>"Don't humiliate me by making me die in women's clothing," he said.</t1955> <t1956>"If I'm going to die, let me die fighting."</t1956> <t1957>In the end, he managed to convince Arcadio.</t1957> <t1958>Arcadio ordered that he be given a weapon and twenty bullets, left five men with him to defend the headquarters, and went out with his men to lead the resistance.</t1958> <t1959>However, he couldn't reach the main road leading to the swamp.</t1959> <t1960>The barricades had been destroyed.</t1960> <t1961>The defenders of the village were fighting in the streets.</t1961>
<t1962>They fought with their rifles until they ran out of bullets, then drew their pistols against the rifles, and finally came to hand-to-hand combat, grappling throat to throat.</t1962> <t1963>Seeing the rout, some of the women took to the streets with sticks and bread knives they had grabbed.</t1963> <t1964>In that chaos, Arcadio, in his nightshirt and with José Arcadio Buendía's two old pistols in hand, encountered Amaranta, who was frantically searching for him.</t1964> <t1965>Arcadio gave his rifle to an officer who had lost his weapon in the fight and turned into a side street to take Amaranta home.</t1965> <t1966>Ursula was standing at the door, waiting for them, indifferent to the cannon fire that had made a huge breach in the adjoining house.</t1966> <t1967>The rain was letting up, but the streets were as slippery as if soap had been spilled on them, and in the darkness, distance could only be judged by eye.</t1967> <t1968>Arcadio left Amaranta with Ursula and tried to confront two soldiers who were firing from the corner.</t1968> <t1969>The old pistols, forgotten for years at the bottom of a drawer, did not fire.</t1969> <t1970>Ursula tried to pull Arcadio into the house, shielding him with her body.</t1970>
<t1971>"For God's sake, come!" he shouted.</t1971> <t1972>"This madness is enough!"</t1972>
<t1973>The soldiers took aim at them.</t1973>
<t1974>Someone shouted, "Leave that man alone, lady."</t1974> <t1975>Or else we won't intervene!</t1975>
<t1976>Arcadio pushed Ursula towards the house, then surrendered.</t1976> <t1977>Soon after, the gunfire ceased, and the bells began to ring.</t1977> <t1978>The resistance was broken in less than half an hour.</t1978> <t1979>Not a single one of Arcadio's men survived, but they had killed three hundred soldiers from the opposing side.</t1979> <t1980>The last point of resistance was the headquarters.</t1980> <t1981>Before the attack, a man named Colonel Gregorio Stevenson had released the prisoners in the headquarters and ordered his men to go out and fight in the streets.</t1981>
<t1982>His extraordinary agility and his ability to hit all twenty of his shots without missing gave the impression that the headquarters was defended by a multitude of people, causing the attackers to bombard the headquarters with cannonballs.</t1982> <t1983>The captain leading the attack was surprised when he saw no one among the ruins except a single dead body with a shattered arm, holding an empty rifle, and wearing nothing but underwear.</t1983> <t1984>The dead man's long hair, like that of a woman, was held at the nape of his neck with a comb, and there was a small fish pendant hanging from a chain around his neck.</t1984> <t1985>When the captain turned the corpse over onto its back with the toe of his boot and shone a light on its face, his astonishment grew even more.</t1985>

<t1990>At dawn, by the decision of the hastily convened military court, Arcadio was executed by firing squad at the foot of the cemetery wall.</t1990> <t1991>He himself could not understand why the fear that had poisoned his life since childhood suddenly vanished in the last two hours of his life.</t1991> <t1992>Without attempting to show the courage he had displayed moments before, he listened indifferently to the accusations directed at him by the court.</t1992> <t1993>He remembered Ursula.</t1993> <t1994>He thought that at this hour she must be having her morning coffee with Jose Arcadio Buendia under the chestnut tree.</t1994> <t1995>He thought of his eight-month-old daughter, who had not yet been named, and the child that would be born in August.</t1995> <t1996>He thought of Santa Sofia de la Piedad, whom he had left the evening before while salting the venison for the next day's meal; he missed her hair falling on her shoulders and her eyelashes that looked artificial.</t1996>

<t2212>A new war began right there.</t2212> <t2213>Captain Roque Carnicero and six soldiers set out with Colonel Aureliano Buendia to rescue the revolutionary General Victorio Medina, who had been sentenced to death in Riohacha.</t2213> <t2214>They thought it would be shorter if they followed the path Jose Arcadio Buendia had taken to Macondo and headed into the mountains, but within a week they realized it was an impossible endeavor.</t2214> <t2215>As a result, they were forced to take the dangerous path filled with cliffs.</t2215> <t2216>They had no ammunition other than the bullets of the firing squad.</t2216> <t2217>They would camp near villages and towns, and one of them would disguise himself and go out to search for hidden Liberals with a small silver fish in hand.</t2217> <t2218>The Liberals would leave the next day under the pretense of going hunting and would not return.</t2218> <t2219>By the time they saw Riohacha through the mountains, General Victorio Medina had already been executed by firing squad.</t2219> <t2220>Colonel Aureliano Buendia's men promoted Buendia to the rank of general and made him the Commander of the Revolutionary Forces of the Caribbean Coast.</t2220> <t2221>Aureliano took on the role but stated that he could not accept the rank of general as long as the Conservative regime remained in power.</t2221>
