<t3124>His inability to die restored his lost dignity within a few hours.</t3124> <t3125>Those who gossiped that he sold the war for a room with walls built of golden bricks interpreted his suicide attempt as a display of virtue and sanctified him as a martyr.</t3125> <t3126>Later, when Aureliano refused the Medal of Merit given by the President, even his arch-enemies crowded into his room, asking him to disregard the ceasefire agreement and start a new war.</t3126> <t3127>The house overflowed with gifts from those who wanted to seek forgiveness, wishing him a speedy recovery.</t3127> <t3128>Colonel Aureliano Buendia, influenced by the intense support of his former comrades, did not reject their desires regarding the war.</t3128> <t3129>On the contrary, he became so enthusiastic at the thought of a new war for a moment that Colonel Gerineldo Marquez thought he was waiting for the slightest excuse to declare war.</t3129> <t3130>This excuse was not long in coming.</t3130>
<t3131>The President said that no pensions would be granted to any of the former warriors, whether Liberal or Conservative, until everyone's situation had been examined by a special commission and approved by the parliament.</t3131> <t3132>Colonel Aureliano Buendia roared, "This is an outrage!"</t3132> <t3133>"They will grow old and die waiting for news from the mail," he said.</t3133> <t3134>For the first time, he got up from the rocking chair that Ursula had bought for him to sit and rest until he recovered, and pacing up and down the bedroom, he dictated a harsh message to the president.</t3134> <t3135>In this telegram, which was never made public, he condemned the violation of the Neerlandia Agreement and threatened to fight until death if the pensions were not resolved within two weeks.</t3135> <t3136>He was so justified that he hoped even the former Conservative warriors would support him.</t3136> <t3137>However, the only response from the government was to increase the number of soldiers stationed at the door, supposedly to protect Aureliano, and to prohibit entry to the house for any reason.</t3137> <t3138>The same method was applied to other leaders held in custody across the country.</t3138>
<t3139>This was such a timely, comprehensive, and effective operation that, two months after the signing of the ceasefire agreement, when Colonel Aureliano Buendia recovered and got back on his feet, the most passionate and enthusiastic organizers were either dead, exiled, or had adapted to the administrative cadres.</t3139>
<t3140>Colonel Aureliano Buendia stepped out of his room in December, and a glance at the veranda was enough to erase the war from his mind.</t3140> <t3141>Ursula, with incredible vitality at her age, had rejuvenated the house.</t3141>
<t3142>When she realized that her son would live, she said, "They will see who I am."</t3142> <t3143>"There will be no house in the world better, more beautiful, and with doors open to everyone than this madhouse."</t3143> <t3144>She had the house plastered from top to bottom, painted, changed the furniture, rearranged the garden, planted new flowers, and opened all the doors and windows wide so that the dazzling summer sun could even enter the bedrooms.</t3144> <t3145>She put an end to the years of accumulated mourning and she herself took off her old clothes and wore colorful garments suitable for the young.</t3145> <t3146>The music of the lantern once again filled the house with joy.</t3146> <t3147>When Amaranta heard the sound of the lantern, she remembered Pietro Crespi, his evening arrivals with gardenias in hand, the scent of lavender, and deep within her pale heart, a pure hatred, cleansed by time, was born.</t3147> <t3148>One day, while Ursula was tidying up the living room, she asked for the help of the soldiers guarding the house.</t3148> <t3149>The young commander of the guards allowed the soldiers to help.</t3149> <t3150>Gradually, Ursula assigned them new tasks.</t3150> <t3151>She fed them, gave them clothes and shoes, and taught them to read and write.</t3151>
<t3152>After the government removed the guards, one of the soldiers stayed at home and served Ursula for many years.</t3152> <t3153>The young commander of the guards, however, went mad because Beautiful Remedios scorned and rejected him, and on New Year's Day, he was found dead under her window.</t3153>
<t3154>Years later, Aureliano Segundo, on his deathbed, would remember that rainy June day when he entered the bedroom to see his first son.</t3154> <t3155>Even though the child was screaming, lying limp like an empty sack, and bore none of the characteristics typical of the Buendias, Aureliano Segundo decided without hesitation what name to give him.</t3155>
<t3156>"His name will be Jose Arcadio," he said.</t3156>
<t3157>Fernanda del Carpio, the beautiful woman Aureliano had married a year ago, accepted what her husband said.</t3157> <t3158>On the other hand, Ursula did not hide her vague suspicion.</t3158> <t3159>According to Ursula, the repeated use of names throughout the family's long history had yielded almost certain results.</t3159> <t3160>While all the Aurelianos were introverted and sensible, the Jose Arcadios were bold and enterprising, but inevitably ran into trouble.</t3160> <t3161>The only case that couldn't be classified was the situation of Jose Arcadio Segundo and Aureliano Segundo.</t3161> <t3162>In their childhood, they were so identical and so mischievous that even Santa Sofia de la Piedad could not tell them apart.</t3162> <t3163>On the day they were baptized, Amaranta put a tag on each of the children's arms and always dressed them in clothes of different colors with their initials embroidered on them.</t3163>
<t3164>However, after the children started school, they began changing their clothes and name tags, calling each other by the wrong names.</t3164> <t3165>The teachers, including Melchor Escalona, who distinguished Jose Arcadio Segundo by his green shirt, were bewildered when they discovered that Jose Arcadio Segundo was wearing the name tag of the other, who, in turn, wore a white shirt and the name tag of Aureliano Segundo.</t3165> <t3166>From then on, they could never be sure which one was which.</t3166> <t3167>Even when the children grew up and life differentiated them, Ursula always wondered if, during that game of deception, the children had mistakenly switched identities permanently.</t3167> <t3168>Until they reached adolescence, they were like two machines operating in perfect harmony.</t3168> <t3169>They woke up at the same time, rushed to the bathroom at the same time, caught the same illnesses, and even had the same dreams.</t3169>
<t3170>The people at home thought they were acting this way to deceive everyone.</t3170> <t3171>However, one day Santa Sofia de la Piedad gave one of the twins a glass of lemonade.</t3171> <t3172>As soon as the child brought the glass to his mouth, the other one jumped in and said that the lemonade was unsweetened.</t3172> <t3173>Santa Sofia de la Piedad, who had indeed forgotten to put sugar in the lemonade, told this incident to Ursula.</t3173> <t3174>Ursula was not surprised at all, "They're all like this," she said.</t3174> <t3175>"Crazy from birth."</t3175> <t3176>Over time, the confusion lessened.</t3176> <t3177>At the end of the deception game, the one known as Aureliano Segundo became a broad-shouldered young man like his ancestors, while the one named Jose Arcadio Segundo turned into a thin, bony young man like the colonel.</t3177> <t3178>The only common trait they shared was the air of loneliness unique to the family.</t3178> <t3179>Perhaps it was this contrast in their stature, names, and personalities that gave Ursula the suspicion that the children had been shuffled like a deck of cards since they were born.</t3179>
<t3180>During the most intense days of the war, when Jose Arcadio Segundo told Colonel Gerineldo Marquez that he wanted to watch someone being executed by firing squad, a clear distinction emerged among the children.</t3180> <t3181>Ursula's opposition was in vain, and Jose Arcadio Segundo got his way.</t3181> <t3182>On the other hand, Aureliano Segundo shuddered from head to toe at the mere thought of watching someone being executed by firing squad.</t3182> <t3183>He preferred to stay at home.</t3183> <t3184>When he was twelve years old, he asked Ursula what was in the locked room.</t3184> <t3185>Ursula replied, "Papers."</t3185> <t3186>"Melquiades' books and the strange things he wrote in his final years."</t3186> <t3187>This answer only fueled Aureliano Segundo's curiosity instead of quenching it.</t3187> <t3188>He insisted so much on entering the room and promised so fervently that he wouldn't damage anything that Ursula finally handed him the keys.</t3188> <t3189>No one had entered the room since Melquiades' body had been removed.</t3189> <t3190>The padlock on the door had rusted and corroded from the inside.</t3190> <t3191>Despite all this, when Aureliano Segundo opened the windows, a light entered the room as if it were accustomed to illuminating it every day.</t3191> <t3192>There was no dust or cobwebs to be seen anywhere.</t3192>
<t3193>Everywhere had been swept and cleaned, it was even shinier than the day Melquiades was buried.</t3193> <t3194>The ink in the inkwell had not dried, the metal objects had not rusted or tarnished, and the embers under the still where Jose Arcadio Buendia boiled mercury had not yet gone out.</t3194> <t3195>On the shelves were books covered with something resembling cardboard, yellowed, and tanned, reminiscent of human skin.</t3195> <t3196>The manuscripts had also remained as they were.</t3196> <t3197>Despite being closed for years, the air in the room was cleaner than that of the other rooms in the house.</t3197> <t3198>Everything was so clean that a few weeks later, when Ursula entered the room with a bucket and a brush to scrub the boards, she found nothing to do.</t3198>
<t3199>Aureliano Segundo was dozing, reading a book.</t3199> <t3200>Even though the book had no cover and its name was written nowhere, the child still enjoyed what he was reading.</t3200> <t3201>He was reading the story of a woman who sat at a table and put nothing in her mouth except rice paste which she ate with a stick, the story of a fisherman who borrowed weights from his neighbor to catch fish and in return gifted a fish from whose belly a diamond emerged, the story of a magic lamp that granted wishes, and flying carpets.</t3201> <t3202>Aureliano Segundo, in astonishment, asked Ursula if these were real.</t3202> <t3203>Ursula also said they were true and told him that years ago, gypsies had brought magic lamps and flying carpets to Macondo.</t3203>
<t3204>Then he sighed, "Slowly, the end of the world is coming, such people no longer visit these places," he said.</t3204> <t3205>After finishing the book, most of whose stories were incomplete due to missing pages, Aureliano Segundo started to dismantle the manuscripts.</t3205> <t3206>Deciphering the writings was impossible.</t3206> <t3207>The letters resembled clothes hanging on a line, looking more like musical notes than writing.</t3207> <t3208>One hot afternoon, while Aureliano Segundo was working hunched over the manuscripts, he suddenly sensed he was not alone in the room.</t3208> <t3209>In the light coming through the window, with his hands resting on his knees, Melquiades was sitting.</t3209> <t3210>He was not yet forty years old.</t3210> <t3211>He wore that outdated vest and a hat resembling a crow's wing.</t3211> <t3212>The oil he had applied to his hair had melted in the heat and dripped down his pale temples.</t3212> <t3213>He looked exactly as he had when Aureliano and Jose Arcadio first saw him in their childhood.</t3213> <t3214>Aureliano Segundo recognized him at first glance.</t3214> <t3215>Because memories had passed from generation to generation, and his grandfather's memories had been transmitted to him through heredity.</t3215>
<t3216>Aureliano Segundo said, "Hello."</t3216>
<t3217>Melquides replied, "Hello young man."</t3217>
<t3218>After that, they met every day for several years.</t3218> <t3219>Melquiades would tell him about the world, trying to impart his knowledge to him, but he refused to translate the manuscripts.</t3219> <t3220>"No one should know what is written there before reaching the age of one hundred," he would say.</t3220> <t3221>Aureliano kept his meetings with Melquiades a secret from everyone.</t3221> <t3222>Once, when Melquiades was in the room and Ursula suddenly walked in, Aureliano Segundo thought his private world had been shattered.</t3222>
<t3223>However, Ursula did not see Melquiades.</t3223>
<t3224>-Who were you talking to? he asked.</t3224>
<t3225>-With no one, said Aureliano Segundo.</t3225>
<t3226>"Your great-grandfather used to do the same, he would talk to himself too," said Ursula.</t3226>
<t3227>Meanwhile, Jose Arcadio Segundo had satisfied his curiosity by watching someone being executed by firing squad.</t3227> <t3228>He would never forget the purple glow of the bullets fired simultaneously, the echoing sound hitting the hills, and the surprised eyes and sad smile of the man being executed for the rest of his life.</t3228> <t3229>The man stood upright even when his shirt was soaked in blood, and he was still smiling when they untied him from the post and placed him in a lime-filled coffin.</t3229> <t3230>Jose Arcadio Segundo thought, "He's alive."</t3230> <t3231>"They're going to bury him alive."</t3231> <t3232>This affected him so deeply that from that day on, he hated the military and war, not because of the executions but because the executed were buried alive.</t3232> <t3233>No one understood when Jose Segundo began ringing the church bell, assisting Peter Antonia Isabel, who replaced the Hippopotamus, during masses, and taking care of the fighting cocks in the priest's house garden.</t3233>
<t3234>When Colonel Gerineldo Marquez learned of this, he scolded the young man for engaging in activities opposed and disapproved of by the Liberals.</t3234> <t3235>Jose Arcadio Segundo replied, "To tell you the truth, I think I have become a Conservative."</t3235> <t3236>He said this with a belief as if it were his destiny.</t3236> <t3237>Colonel Gerineldo Marquez became very angry, very anxious, and explained the situation to Ursula.</t3237>
<t3238>Ursula said, "It's better this way."</t3238> <t3239>"I wish he were a priest so that God would finally enter through the door of this house."</t3239>
<t3240>It soon became clear that Father Antonio Isabel was preparing Jose Arcadio Segundo for his first communion.</t3240> <t3241>While they were shaving the necks of their roosters and preparing them for fights, the priest was teaching Jose Arcadio Segundo the necessary knowledge through 'catechism'; as they placed the hens ready to brood into the nests, he explained with simple examples how on the second day of creation, God thought that chicks should hatch from eggs.</t3241> <t3242>The priest had begun to show signs of senility at that time.</t3242> <t3243>This senility would gradually increase, and years later, the priest would claim that perhaps the devil's rebellion against God had ended in victory and that, to deceive the unwary, the devil had hidden his true identity and sat on the throne in the heavens.</t3243>
<t3244>With the guidance and persistence of his teacher, Jose Arcadio Segundo mastered the theological tricks used to outwit the devil within a few months, just as he had mastered the tricks of cockfighting.</t3244>
<t3245>Amaranta sewed a linen garment with a collar and tie.</t3245> <t3246>She bought white shoes.</t3246> <t3247>She embroidered the initials of her name in gold on the ribbon that would be tied to the candle.</t3247> <t3248>Two nights before the first communion, Father Antonio Isabel locked himself in the room where the church's valuable items were kept with Jose Arcadio Segundo to hear his confession.</t3248> <t3249>They had also brought a dictionary of sins with them.</t3249> <t3250>The list of sins was so long that the old priest, who was used to going to bed at six, couldn't stay awake until the end and fell asleep in his chair.</t3250> <t3251>The priest's questions opened up a new world for Jose Arcadio Segundo.</t3251> <t3252>He wasn't surprised when the priest asked if he had done indecent things with women and honestly answered no.</t3252> <t3253>But when he was asked if he had done the same with animals, he was confused.</t3253> <t3254>On the first Friday of May, the communion was held, but his mind was still on the priest's question.</t3254> <t3255>He immediately ran to Petronio.</t3255> <t3256>Petronio was the church's sickly caretaker.</t3256> <t3257>He lived in the bell tower.</t3257> <t3258>It was said that he ate bats.</t3258> <t3259>Jose Arcadio Segundo asked him the question that was troubling his mind.</t3259> <t3260>Petronio replied, "There are immoral Christians who do their business with female donkeys."</t3260> <t3261>Jose Arcadio Segundo wasn't satisfied with this answer, his curiosity was tormenting him, and he asked so many questions in succession that Petronio finally lost his patience.</t3261>
<t3262>"I go on Tuesday nights," he confessed.</t3262> <t3263>"If you promise not to tell anyone, I'll take you next Tuesday."</t3263>
<t3264>Indeed, the following Tuesday, Petronio came out of the bell tower with a wooden stool that no one had known the purpose of until that day and took Jose Arcadio Segundo to a nearby pasture.</t3264> <t3265>The young man became so engrossed in his nightly outings that he didn't appear at Catarino's shop for a very long time.</t3265> <t3266>He became a passionate cockfighter.</t3266> <t3267>The first day he came home with fighting cocks, Ursula scolded him, saying, "Take these damn things out of here."</t3267> <t3268>"Cocks have already brought too much sorrow to this house; there's no need for you to add to it."</t3268> <t3269>Without any resistance, Jose Arcadio Segundo took the cocks out of the house, but he continued to keep them at the house of his grandmother Pilar Ternera, who did anything to have him visit her.</t3269> <t3270>Soon enough, in the cockfighting arena, applying the tricks he had learned from Father Antonio Isabel, he not only increased the number of cocks but also made enough money to consider having some fun.</t3270> <t3271>Ursula compared him to his brother and couldn't understand how the twins, who had been like one person in their childhood, had ended up so differently.</t3271>
<t3272>Ursula's astonishment did not last long.</t3272> <t3273>Because very soon, Aureliano Segundo began to show signs of laziness and disarray.</t3273> <t3274>When he locked himself in Melquiades' room, Aureliano Segundo, like Colonel Aureliano Buendia in his youth, would withdraw into himself.</t3274> <t3275>But shortly after the Treaty of Neerlandia, by chance, he broke out of his introversion and faced the reality of the world.</t3275> <t3276>A young woman selling lottery tickets, with an accordion as the prize, greeted him warmly as if she knew him.</t3276> <t3277>Aureliano Segundo was not surprised by this because he was often mistaken for his brother.</t3277> <t3278>However, he did not attempt to correct the mistake.</t3278> <t3279>In fact, even when the girl tried to soften his heart by crying and eventually took him to her room, Aureliano Segundo did not attempt to explain the situation.</t3279> <t3280>During their very first meeting, the woman warmed up to him so much that she did everything she could to ensure Aureliano Segundo won the accordion.</t3280>
<t3281>After two weeks had passed, Aureliano Segundo realized that the woman thought the two of them were the same man, sleeping with one and then the other; nevertheless, instead of correcting the situation, he preferred to let it continue.</t3281> <t3282>He never returned to Melquiades' room again.</t3282> <t3283>In the afternoons, he would sit in the garden, learning to play the accordion by ear despite all of Ursula's shouting and scolding.</t3283> <t3284>At that time, Ursula had banned music in the house due to mourning.</t3284> <t3285>Moreover, she despised the accordion, considering it an instrument fit only for Francisco's nomadic heirs.</t3285>
<t3286>Nevertheless, Aureliano Segundo became an expert at playing the accordion and, even after getting married and having children, and becoming one of the most respected people in Macondo, he did not stop playing the accordion.</t3286>
<t3287>Aureliano Segundo shared the woman with his brother for two months.</t3287> <t3288>He would watch his brother, disrupt his plans, and after ensuring that Jose Arcadio Segundo would not visit their shared mistress that night, he would go and sleep with the woman.</t3288> <t3289>One morning, he realized he had contracted a disease.</t3289> <t3290>Two days later, he caught his brother in the bathroom, drenched in sweat and tears, clinging to a beam.</t3290> <t3291>Then he understood the situation.</t3291> <t3292>His brother confessed that the woman had thrown him out, accusing him of passing on what she called the disease of a filthy life.</t3292> <t3293>He also explained how Pilar Ternera tried to cure him.</t3293> <t3294>Aureliano Segundo secretly began using those burning permanganate baths and diuretic waters.</t3294> <t3295>After three months of suffering in secret, they both recovered.</t3295> <t3296>Jose Arcadio Segundo never saw the woman again.</t3296> <t3297>Aureliano Segundo, however, made amends and never left the woman until she died.</t3297>
<t3298>His name was Petro Cotes.</t3298> <t3299>She had come to Macondo in the midst of the war, making a living from the lottery with the man who was supposedly her husband, and after his death, she had taken over his business.</t3299> <t3300>She was a young, clean, mixed-race woman with almond-shaped yellow eyes that gave her face a wild panther-like look.</t3300> <t3301>She had a rich heart and a God-given, unparalleled tenderness.</t3301> <t3302>It was as if God had created her to make love.</t3302> <t3303>When Ursula learned that Jose Arcadio Segundo was involved in cockfighting and Aureliano Segundo played the accordion at the noisy gatherings of his mistress, she was stunned by this combination.</t3303> <t3304>It seemed as if both young men had inherited all the family's vices and not a single one of its virtues.</t3304> <t3305>Then Ursula decided that no one would ever be named Aureliano or Jose Arcadio again.</t3305> <t3306>Nevertheless, when Aureliano Segundo had his first son, Ursula could not bring herself to oppose his wish.</t3306>
<t3307>"Okay," he said.</t3307> <t3308>"But I have one condition: I will raise him."</t3308>
<t3309>Even though Ursula had reached the age of one hundred and was almost blind due to cataracts, she had not lost the slightest bit of her vitality, personality, or mental balance.</t3309> <t3310>No one could raise a virtuous man who would uphold the family's honor better than she could.</t3310> <t3311>This man would not even know the names of war, cockfighting, bad women, or daring ventures.</t3311>
<t3312>According to Ursula, these four calamities had brought ruin to their household.</t3312> <t3313>To herself, she promised, "This one will become a priest."</t3313> <t3314>"If God grants me life, one day he will even become the Pope."</t3314> <t3315>When they heard what she said, not only those in the bedroom but also Aureliano Segundo's rowdy friends who had filled the house burst into laughter.</t3315>
<t3316>The war, thrown into the cellar of bad memories, was momentarily recalled with the popping of champagne bottles.</t3316>
<t3317>Aureliano Segundo raised his glass and said, "To Papa's health."</t3317>
<t3318>The guests also raised their glasses, repeating the same words in unison.</t3318> <t3319>Then the man of the house played the accordion, fireworks were set off, and the event was celebrated by drumming in the streets throughout the town.</t3319> <t3320>At dawn, the guests, who had been immersed in champagne, sacrificed six cows and laid the meat of the cows in the street for everyone to take.</t3320>
<t3321>Nobody found it strange.</t3321> <t3322>Since Aureliano Segundo took charge of the house, such exuberance had become commonplace, even when there wasn't a sufficient reason like the birth of a Pope.</t3322> <t3323>Within a few years, Aureliano Segundo, without any effort, purely due to his good luck and the extraordinary reproductive abilities of his animals, had amassed one of the greatest fortunes in the swamp region.</t3323> <t3324>His mares were giving birth to triplets, and they were fattening up so quickly that no one could comprehend such irregular productivity, and it was said that there must be some wizardry involved.</t3324> <t3325>Ursula would say to her enterprising grandson, "Set aside a few pennies."</t3325> <t3326>"Your luck won't last a lifetime."</t3326>
<t3327>But Aureliano Segundo did not care about her.</t3327> <t3328>The more bottles he popped to give his friends champagne baths, the more wildly the animals bred, and Aureliano Segundo believed that his lucky star depended not on his own actions but on the influence of Petra Cotes, whose love conquered nature.</t3328> <t3329>Aureliano Segundo was so convinced that she was the source of his wealth that he never kept Petra Cotes away from the farm.</t3329> <t3330>Even after getting married and having children, he continued to live with Petra with the consent of his wife Fernanda.</t3330> <t3331>Aureliano Segundo, who had a monumental build like his grandfathers and an irresistible charm and vitality they lacked, didn't have much time to take care of the animals.</t3331> <t3332>Taking Petra Cotes to his farm and having her ride across it once on horseback was enough to cause an unstoppable breeding epidemic among all the animals marked with his brand.</t3332>
<t3333>Like many of the spoils in Aureliano Segundo's long life, the origin of that great fortune was also based on luck.</t3333> <t3334>Until the end of the wars, Petra Cotes lived off lottery income.</t3334> <t3335>Aureliano Segundo also continued to occasionally pilfer from Ursula's savings.</t3335> <t3336>Even on days considered sinful, they had become a carefree couple whose only concern was to revel every night until dawn.</t3336> <t3337>Whenever Ursula caught her son staggering into the house like a sleepwalker with his grandchild, she would shout at the top of her lungs, "This woman has ruined you."</t3337> <t3338>"This woman has bewitched you so much that I wouldn't be surprised if one day you start writhing in pain and a black frog comes out of your belly."</t3338> <t3339>Jose Arcadio Segundo, who realized too late that he had lost his place, could never understand his brother's passion.</t3339>
<t3340>The Petra Cotes he knew was an ordinary woman with no special qualities, quite clumsy in bed, and knew nothing about love games.</t3340> <t3341>Aureliano Segundo, having turned a deaf ear to Ursula's yelling and his brother's mockery, could think of nothing but begging Petra Cotes for money to support himself, finding a job, and dying beside, on top of, or underneath her in a night of fiery passion.</t3341> <t3342>When Colonel Aureliano Buendia finally succumbed to the peace of old age and reopened his silver workshop, Aureliano Segundo thought it would be good to devote himself to making ornamental fish.</t3342> <t3343>He would sit for hours in the hot room, watching how the metal sheets, shaped with the incredible patience born of the colonel's disillusionment, transformed into golden fish scales.</t3343> <t3344>This work seemed so laborious and tedious to him, and the thought of Petra Cotes was so persistent in his mind, that after three weeks Aureliano Segundo stopped visiting the workshop.</t3344> <t3345>It was around that time that Petra Cotes came up with the idea of a rabbit lottery.</t3345>
<t3346>Due to the rapid breeding and quick growth of the rabbits, they couldn't keep up with selling the lottery tickets.</t3346> <t3347>Initially, Aureliano Segundo did not notice that the animals were multiplying at an alarming rate.</t3347> <t3348>But one night, when the town was so fed up with rabbits that no one wanted to hear about the rabbit lottery anymore, Aureliano Segundo heard a sound in front of the garden gate.</t3348>
<t3349>Petra Cotes said, "Don't worry."</t3349> <t3350>"They are rabbits."</t3350> <t3351>They couldn't sleep because of the noise from the animals.</t3351> <t3352>At dawn, Aureliano Segundo opened the door and saw that the garden was completely filled with rabbits, which appeared blue in the morning twilight.</t3352> <t3353>Petra Cotes, who was laughing uncontrollably, couldn't help but tease him.</t3353>
<t3354>-Here are the ones born last night, he said.</t3354>
<t3355>Aureliano Segundo exclaimed, "Oh my God!"</t3355> <t3356>"If that's the case, why don't you hold a cow lottery?"</t3356>
<t3357>A few days later, Petra Cotes bartered a cow to rid the garden of rabbits.</t3357><t3358>Two months later, the cow gave birth to triplets.</t3358><t3359>That's how it all started.</t3359><t3360>Within fifteen days, Aureliano Segundo became a land and livestock owner and couldn't keep up with expanding the overflowing cow and pig pens.</t3360><t3361>This was a crazy abundance that even made Aureliano Segundo laugh, and he started doing whatever came to mind to express his joy.</t3361><t3362>"Stop, cows, life is short!" he would shout.</t3362><t3363>Ursula wondered what kind of shady dealings he might be involved in, whether he was stealing or swindling.</t3363><t3364>And every time she saw him popping champagne just because he enjoyed pouring the foam over his head, she would yell at him, scolding him for being wasteful.</t3364><t3365>Tired of Ursula's scoldings, Aureliano Segundo, one cheerful morning, showed up with a chest of money, a bucket of glue, and a brush.</t3365><t3366>And singing Francisco's old songs at the top of his lungs, he plastered one-peso banknotes all over the house, inside and out, from top to bottom.</t3366><t3367>The old house, which had been painted white since the days the lantern arrived, now looked like a mosque in this state.</t3367><t3368>Amid the family's excited shouts, Ursula's screams at the top of her lungs, and the laughter of those gathering in the street to watch this monument to extravagance, Aureliano Segundo papered the house from the entrance to the kitchen, from the bedrooms to the bathroom, and scattered the remaining money in the garden.</t3368>
<t3369>Aureliano Segundo curtly said, "I hope no one in this house talks to me about money anymore."</t3369>
<t3370>That's how it happened.</t3370> <t3371>Ursula had the money removed along with the large pieces of plaster they were stuck to, and the house was painted white again.</t3371> <t3372>"God, I pray that you do not take out the pain of this waste on us in the afterlife; please make us as poor as we were when we first founded this town," Ursula was praying.</t3372>
<t3373>The exact opposite of their prayers happened.</t3373> <t3374>One of the workers who was stripping the money hit a life-sized plaster statue of a saint that someone had brought and left during the last years of the war; when the statue fell to the ground, it shattered into pieces, and inside the statue was filled with glittering gold coins.</t3374> <t3375>None of them remembered who had brought this life-sized statue of the saint.</t3375> <t3376>Amaranta said, "Three men had brought it."</t3376> <t3377>"They said let it stay here until the rainy season ends, and I told them to leave it in the corner so no one would bump into it."</t3377>
<t3378>They placed it there carefully, and since no one came to take it, the statue remained there.</t3378>
<t3379>Later on, Ursula placed candles on the statue and knelt in front of it to pray, without even considering that she was worshipping almost two hundred kilos of gold rather than a saint.</t3379> <t3380>The evidence of this unintended idolatry, scattered all over the place, further confused Ursula's mind.</t3380> <t3381>She spat on the heap of coins, filled a sack with them, and buried it in a secret place.</t3381> <t3382>She hoped that those three unknown people would eventually come and ask for the money.</t3382> <t3383>Much later, during the arduous years of her old age, Ursula began to interrupt the conversations of travelers passing by her house, asking if they had come to her home during the war and left a plaster statue of a saint to be picked up after the rainy season.</t3383>
<t3384>These events, which so surprised and upset Ursula, were considered very ordinary in those days.</t3384> <t3385>Macondo was buried in unprecedented wealth.</t3385> <t3386>In place of the adobe houses of the town's founders, brick structures with cement floors and wooden shutters, which made the oppressive midday heat a bit more bearable, had been erected.</t3386>
<t3387>The only thing left of Jose Arcadio Buendia's village at that time was the river, with its dusty almond trees destined to endure the harshest conditions and the prehistoric stones shattered by Jose Arcadio Segundo's sledgehammer.</t3387>
<t3388>When Jose Arcadio Segundo set his mind on operating a ship to Macondo, he had not left a single stone unturned in the river.</t3388> <t3389>This was a dream that could only be compared to the craziness of his great-grandfather's dreams.</t3389>
<t3390>Because the rocky nature of the riverbed and the presence of currents in places made it impossible to reach the sea from Macondo by water.</t3390> <t3391>But Jose Arcadio Segundo, stubbornly and with a mad audacity, kept insisting.</t3391> <t3392>Until then, he had shown no signs of imagination.</t3392> <t3393>Besides his rootless adventure with Petra Cotes, he had not known any woman.</t3393> <t3394>During the days when Colonel Aureliano Buendia was telling Jose Arcadio Segundo about the Spanish galleon lying about twelve kilometers inland from the sea, whose decayed skeleton he had seen with his own eyes during the war, Ursula knew him as the quietest person in the family, someone who couldn't even manage to stand out in cockfights.</t3394> <t3395>For years, this galleon story, which seemed like a fairy tale to most people, sparked a new idea in Jose Arcadio Segundo's mind.</t3395> <t3396>He sold his roosters at auction, hired men, bought tools, and embarked on the daunting task of breaking rocks, opening channels, clearing currents, and even taming waterfalls.</t3396> <t3397>Ursula was shouting, -I know all of this by heart.</t3397> <t3398>-It's as if time has reversed and we've come back to the very beginning, she was saying.</t3398>

<t3405>There was a heap of beautiful women beside him.</t3405> <t3406>The women had applied colorful creams to their faces, adorned their hair with real flowers, wrapped serpentine gold bracelets around their arms, and embedded diamonds in their teeth.</t3406>

<t3416>Beautiful Remedios was chosen as the carnival queen.</t3416> <t3417>Ursula, frightened by the intoxicating beauty of her grandson's daughter, could not prevent this selection.</t3417> <t3418>Until then, she had managed to keep the girl away from the street except for going to church with Amaranta, and even then, she made her cover her face tightly with a black veil.</t3418> <t3419>The most godless of men, those who dressed as priests and recited lewd prayers in Catarino's shop, and even those who had the least regard for religion, had started to frequent the church just to catch a glimpse of the legendary beauty of Beautiful Remedios, whose fame had spread throughout the swamp region.</t3419> <t3420>It was only after a very long time that they were able to see Beautiful Remedios's face.</t3420> <t3421>They wished they hadn't, because most of the men were deprived of sleep ever after.</t3421> <t3422>The stranger who made Beautiful Remedios unveil her face lost his mind completely, became miserable and wretched, and years later, fell asleep on the tracks and was torn apart by a train.</t3422> <t3423>When the man appeared in the church in his green velvet suit and embroidered vest, no one doubted that he had come from far away, perhaps from a distant town in a foreign country, drawn by the enchanting allure of Beautiful Remedios.</t3423> <t3424>The young man looked so handsome, so elegant, so polite, so dignified that Pietro Crespi would have seemed like a gaudy dandy next to him.</t3424>
<t3425>The women began to whisper, saying that with such beauty, it was she who should be wearing the veil.</t3425> <t3426>The man did not speak to anyone in Macondo.</t3426> <t3427>At dawn on Sunday morning, he would arrive like a fairy tale prince on a horse with silver stirrups and a velvet-covered saddle, and after the church service, he would leave the town.</t3427>
<t3428>The presence of the young man was so powerful that the moment he stepped into the church, everyone acknowledged that a silent and tense duel had begun between him and Beautiful Remedios, a challenge that would result not only in love but also in death, a secret pact.</t3428> <t3429>On the sixth Sunday, the young man appeared with a yellow rose in his hand.</t3429> <t3430>He watched the ceremony standing, as usual, and when it ended, he went to Beautiful Remedios and extended the rose.</t3430> <t3431>Beautiful Remedios took the rose with a natural gesture as if she had been prepared for this act of respect, then unveiled her face and smiled instead of thanking him.</t3431> <t3432>That was all she did.</t3432> <t3433>For not only the young man but also for all the men who had the unfortunate privilege of seeing her, it was an unforgettable, eternal moment.</t3433>

<t3455>He reached the age of twenty without knowing how to read or write, or how to use a fork and knife.</t3455> <t3456>Since his nature was against all traditions, he would walk around the house completely naked.</t3456> <t3457>When the young commander of the guards told her that he loved her, she rejected him because she was startled by his nonsense.</t3457> <t3458>While telling this to Amaranta, she said, "Do you see, what a simple man."</t3458> <t3459>"As if I were an incurable stomach ache, he says he will die because of me."</t3459> <t3460>When they found the commander's dead body in front of her window, Beautiful Remedios' thoughts about him were confirmed.</t3460>

<t3486>Actually, what interested Colonel Aureliano Buendia was not the business aspect, but the work itself.</t3486> <t3487>Connecting the scales, setting tiny rubies into the eyes, flattening the gills, attaching the fins required such intense concentration that there was no time left to think about the disappointments brought by the war.</t3487> <t3488>Because of this, he deteriorated more in a short time than he had during all those years of war.</t3488> <t3489>Constant sitting had hunched his back, and bending over the work had ruined his eyes.</t3489>
<t3490>However, the extreme intensity granted peace to his soul.</t3490> <t3491>The last time he dealt with a matter related to the war was when a mixed group of fighters from both sides asked for his support.</t3491> <t3492>They had been promised a lifelong pension, but this had never been implemented.</t3492> <t3493>The former warriors wanted the colonel to support them in securing the pension.</t3493>
<t3494>The Colonel said, "It's best to just forget it."</t3494> <t3495>"Look, I didn't accept the pension because I wanted to escape the torture of waiting for it to come, whether it would come or not, until I die."</t3495>

<t3501>Once, someone dared to break the solitude in which the colonel was immersed.</t3501> <t3502>While passing by the door, they asked, "How are you, Colonel?"</t3502>

<t3516>-Long live the Liberal Party! he shouted.</t3516> <t3517>-Long live Colonel Aureliano Buendia!</t3517>
<t3518>The sounds of gunfire, fireworks, and screams of fear drowned out the music, and the joy gave way to an atmosphere of panic.</t3518> <t3519>Even years later, they continued to insist that the aides of the foreign queen were soldiers hiding their rifles in ample Bedouin clothing.</t3519> <t3520>The government denied these accusations with a special statement and promised a thorough investigation into the bloody incident.</t3520> <t3521>But the truth of the matter was never clarified, and the belief persisted that the guards in Bedouin disguise had drawn their rifles and opened fire on the crowd without any provocation.</t3521> <t3522>By the time the commotion settled, the fake Bedouins had left the town.</t3522> <t3523>A heap of dead and wounded lay in the area: Nine clowns, four concubines, seventeen kings of cards, a devil, three folk poets, two French nobles, and three Japanese empresses were sprawled on the ground.</t3523>

Six months after the massacre, when the wounded had healed and the last flowers on the grave where those who died that day were buried together had withered, Aureliano Segundo went to the distant town where she lived to ask her father for her hand, and they got married in Macondo with a wedding that lasted twenty days and twenty nights.</t3530>

<t3568>He didn't have to wait as long as he thought.</t3568> <t3569>Aureliano Segundo realized on his wedding night that he would go to Petra Cotes' house long before he reached the age to pull on his boots and wait for death.</t3569>
<t3570>Fernanda was a living dead.</t3570> <t3571>She was born and raised in a city about a thousand kilometers away.</t3571> <t3572>It was a gloomy city where the sounds of the carriages of long-gone governors could still be heard on the cobblestones of deserted streets on dark nights.</t3572> <t3573>At six in the evening, death bells would ring from exactly thirty-two towers.</t3573> <t3574>In the mansion with wooden floors resembling a funeral slab, the sun was never seen.</t3574> <t3575>The cypress trees in the garden, the pale, heavy curtains of the bedrooms, and the arches covered with climbing roses in the garden would completely suffocate the air.</t3575> <t3576>Until she reached her teenage years, Fernanda grew up unaware of the world, except for the melancholic hum of piano lessons that a neighbor had persistently continued for years, resisting taking a nap.</t3576> <t3577>Fernanda would sit in the room of her sick mother, whose skin appeared greenish-yellow in the light seeping through the shutters, listening to the methodical, monotonous, emotionless sounds of the keys, and while she withered away weaving funeral wreaths, she thought that music was the world.</t3577> <t3578>Her mother, burning with evening fever and sweating, would tell her how unparalleled the beauty of the past was.</t3578> <t3579>When Fernanda was very young, on a moonlit night, she had seen a very beautiful woman dressed in white walking towards the small church in the garden.</t3579>

<t3594>His classmates were quite surprised that he sat in a separate corner on a very high-backed chair and did not join them even during breaks.</t3594> <t3595>The nuns would say, "He is not like you."</t3595>
<t3596>-She will be the queen.</t3596> <t3597>Her classmates immediately believed this statement because they had never seen a girl as beautiful, elegant, and distinguished as her.</t3597>
<t3598>After eight years of education, during which she learned to write Latin poetry, play the piano, discuss hunting with young men, theology with bishops, world affairs with foreign heads of state, and matters of the afterlife with the Pope, Fernanda returned to her father's house to spend her days weaving garlands.</t3598> <t3599>She found the house completely empty.</t3599>
<t3600>After staying at home for so long, only a few essential pieces of furniture, silver candlesticks, and a silver dinner set remained; the everyday items had been sold off one by one to cover his school expenses.</t3600>
<t3601>Her mother's fever had started to rise every evening.</t3601> <t3602>Her father, Don Fernando, who dressed in black, wore stiff collars, and carried a gold chain, would give her a silver coin on Mondays to cover the household expenses and would send off the wreaths prepared the previous week.</t3602> <t3603>Her father spent most of his time shut away in his study, and although he occasionally left the house, he would return at prayer time to pray with his daughter, using a rosary.</t3603>
<t3604>Fernanda had no close friends.</t3604>
<t3605>She was unaware of the ongoing bloody wars.</t3605> <t3606>Every day at three o'clock, she continued her piano lessons.</t3606> <t3607>Just as she was beginning to lose her dreams of becoming a queen, one day, the door knocker was struck twice.</t3607> <t3608>When Fernanda opened the door, she saw an officer standing there with a formal demeanor, well-dressed, a scar on his cheek, and a gold medal on his chest.</t3608> <t3609>The officer and her father shut themselves in the study.</t3609> <t3610>Two hours later, her father came to the sewing room and called for her.</t3610> <t3611>-Pack your things, he said.</t3611>
<t3612>-You will go on a long journey.</t3612> <t3613>This is how they brought him to Macondo.</t3613>
<t3614>Life, in a single day, with a merciless slap, crushed him under the full weight of the truths his family had been hiding for years.</t3614>
<t3615>After Fernanda returned home, she locked herself in her room and began to cry, ignoring Don Fernando's explanations and pleas as he tried to erase the traces of this meaningless joke.</t3615> <t3616>She swore not to leave her room until she died.</t3616> <t3617>At that moment, Aureliano Segundo came to get her.</t3617> <t3618>This was an incomprehensible twist of fate.</t3618>
<t3619>Because Fernanda, in that anger and shame, had lied to Aureliano Segundo so that he wouldn't learn her true identity.</t3619> <t3620>When Aureliano Segundo set out to look for her, he knew nothing more than that the girl spoke with the accent typical of the mountainous region and that she wove funeral wreaths.</t3620> <t3621>He searched for her tirelessly.</t3621> <t3622>With the same terrible determination that led Jose Arcadio Buendia to cross the mountains to find Macondo, with the blind pride that drove Colonel Aureliano Buendia into futile wars, and with the mad diligence Ursula showed in continuing her lineage, Aureliano Segundo searched for Fernanda.</t3622>
<t3623>He searched without stopping for a moment, without losing hope for a moment.</t3623> <t3624>When he asked anyone he came across where funeral wreaths were sold, they took him from house to house so he could choose the best one and buy the one he liked.</t3624> <t3625>When he asked for the most beautiful woman in the world, all the women pointed to their own daughters.</t3625>
<t3626>Aureliano Segundo lost himself in foggy paths, wasted times, and the dead-end of disappointment.</t3626> <t3627>He passed through a yellow plain.</t3627>
<t3628>Here, a person's thoughts echoed, and what crossed their mind stood before them like a mirage.</t3628> <t3629>After wandering for weeks without any results, he entered a foreign city where funeral bells were ringing in all its towers.</t3629> <t3630>Although Aureliano Segundo had never seen or heard of it, as soon as he saw the worn walls, the moss-covered broken wooden balconies, and the world's most sorrowful 'Wreaths for Sale' sign on the street door, so faded by the rain that it was almost unreadable, he realized this was the place he had been searching for.</t3630>
<t3631>In that short period, from the moment Fernanda left the house on the arm of the abbess on that freezing morning, the nuns only had enough time to sew the wedding dress and pack countless useless items left over from a family tragedy two hundred years overdue, silver candlesticks, silver tableware, and a golden chamber pot into six chests.</t3631>
<t3632>Don Fernando did not accept the call to go along with the bride.</t3632>
<t3633>After putting his affairs in order, he promised to come in the future, and after blessing and wishing happiness for his daughter, he shut himself in his room, drafting announcements more suited for obituaries, and wrote wedding announcements bearing the family crest.</t3633> <t3634>Throughout their lives, this was the first humane relationship between Fernanda and her father.</t3634> <t3635>That day was truly the day Fernanda was born, and for Aureliano Segundo, it was the day happiness both began and ended.</t3635>
<t3636>Fernanda never went anywhere without her elegant calendar, adorned with gold leaf.</t3636> <t3637>The religion teacher had marked the days of sexual abstinence on this calendar with purple ink.</t3637> <t3638>Once the holy week, Sundays, fasting days, the first Friday of each month, the three months, sacrifices, and the days hindered by menstruation were excluded, Fernanda's useful days were reduced to forty-two, squeezed between purple marks.</t3638> <t3639>Believing that this hostile web of marks would eventually unravel, Aureliano Segundo prolonged the wedding longer than expected.</t3639> <t3640>Ursula, tired of constantly throwing away empty bottles of cognac and champagne to make room to walk in the house, was surprised that the newlyweds slept at different times and in different rooms while fireworks were set off, music played, and lambs were roasted; she wondered if, like herself, Fernanda wore a chastity belt that would eventually become a subject of ridicule in the town and cause a disaster at home.</t3640>

<t3648>After a month had passed and he still couldn't get his wife to take off her nightgown, Aureliano Segundo had Petra Cotes' picture taken dressed as a queen.</t3648> <t3649>Later, when he managed to persuade Fernanda to return home, although his wife yielded to his desires in the excitement of reconciliation, the lovemaking Aureliano Segundo had envisioned while going to fetch her from the town with thirty-two bell towers never materialized.</t3649>

<t3662>"-Bugu," he said, "-from the stories told by the old people."</t3662>

<t3664>-I was saying that you are one of those who stir up your own mess.</t3664>

<t3700>Despite everyone except their mother smiling under their breath, the children began to see their grandfather as a legendary figure who wrote religious poems to them in his letters and sent gift boxes so large they barely fit through the front door every Christmas.</t3700> <t3701>These were actually the last remnants of their grandfather's inheritance.</t3701> <t3702>With the gifts that arrived, they set up a shrine in the children's room adorned with life-sized statues of saints.</t3702>

<t3782>Aureliano Triste, with a cross mark drawn in ash on his forehead, established the ice factory he had dreamed of amidst the creative madness of Jose Arcadio Buendia on the outskirts of the town.</t3782>
<t3783>In a short time, Aureliano Triste, who became well-known and beloved, started looking for a house to bring his mother and his unmarried sister (who was not the colonel's daughter) a few months after his arrival, and he became interested in the large, dilapidated house at the corner of the area.</t3783> <t3784>He asked who the owner was.</t3784>

<t3807>In a low voice, -For God's sake, he said.</t3807> <t3808>-They have no right to bring this memory before me.</t3808>

<t3810>The woman pointed the gun, aiming at the cross mark on the young man's forehead without her hand trembling.</t3810> <t3811>She was very determined.</t3811> <t3812>"Get out!" she said.</t3812>

<t3871>Because someone who died and was buried in one film, causing the audience to shed tears, would come back to life in the next film and appear this time in the guise of an Arab.</t3871> <t3872>The audience, who paid two cents per person for tickets to share in the calamities that befell the actors, were outraged by this deceit and broke the chairs.</t3872> <t3873>Under pressure from Bruna Crespi, the mayor issued a statement explaining that cinema was a visual machine that did not require emotional outbursts from the audience.</t3873> <t3874>After this discouraging announcement, many people considered themselves victims of a new and flashy gypsy trick and decided that their own troubles were enough for them, deeming it unnecessary to shed tears over the fabricated calamities of imaginary people, and thus boycotted the cinema.</t3874>
