<t4020>The rotten tiles broke with a noise and the man only had time to let out a scream of fear before hitting the cement floor and breaking his head.</t4020> <t4021>He died immediately and lay sprawled on the ground.</t4021> <t4022>When strangers, who had heard the noise from the dining room, came running, they smelled the breathtaking scent of Beautiful Remedios lingering on the man's skin as they lifted his body.</t4022> <t4023>This scent had permeated him so deeply that from the cracks in his head, instead of blood, an amber-colored oily liquid exuding the scent of Beautiful Remedios flowed.</t4023> <t4024>Then they realized that the scent of Beautiful Remedios did not leave men alone even after killing them, penetrating their marrow and bones.</t4024> <t4025>Nevertheless, they did not connect this horrifying accident with the other two men who had died because of Beautiful Remedios.</t4025> <t4026>A new victim was needed for the strangers and the people of Macondo to believe in the legend that Remedios Buendia spread death, not love, around her and to pass this word from mouth to mouth.</t4026> <t4027>The event that would prove this rumor happened a few months later when Beautiful Remedios went to look at the newly built structures with her girlfriends.</t4027>
<t4028>For the girls of Macondo, this had become a new game.</t4028> <t4029>The things they saw made them laugh, surprised them, scared them, and entertained them.</t4029> <t4030>At night, they would sit and recount these outings as if they had dreamed them, talking about them again.</t4030>
<t4031>Faced with the silence of Beautiful Remedios, Ursula's heart couldn't bear to deprive her of this amusement, and one day, on the condition that she wore proper clothing and a hat, she allowed her to go with the girls.</t4031> <t4032>As soon as the girls entered among the structures, a deadly smell filled the air.</t4032> <t4033>The workers were seized by a strange enchantment, sensing they were facing an invisible danger, and most of them felt a desire to cry uncontrollably.</t4033> <t4034>When the men, their eyes glazed over, advanced towards them, Beautiful Remedios and her friends, surprised, took refuge in one of the structures.</t4034>
<t4035>Shortly after, four Aurelianos saved them.</t4035> <t4036>The ash crosses on the foreheads of the Aurelianos endowed them with a sacred dignity, considered a mark of a certain caste, a sign of inviolability.</t4036>
<t4037>Beautiful Remedios did not tell anyone that in the chaos, one of the men clutched his stomach with a hand that resembled an eagle's claw gripping the edge of a cliff.</t4037> <t4038>She briefly locked eyes with the man attacking her, and Beautiful Remedios saw the pain in his eyes.</t4038> <t4039>Those eyes, filled with pain, burned her heart like two hot coals.</t4039> <t4040>That night, while boasting about his daytime audacity and strutting around in Turks Street, the man suddenly received a kick to his chest from a horse.</t4040> <t4041>Strangers who gathered around watched as the man, coughing up blood and choking on his own blood, died in the middle of the street.</t4041>
<t4042>After that, the assumption that Beautiful Remedios possessed deadly powers was proven by four undeniable events.</t4042> <t4043>Although some men who spoke without thinking claimed they would sacrifice their lives for a night of lovemaking with such an attractive and seductive woman, it was clear that no one dared to attempt such a thing.</t4043> <t4044>Perhaps not only to possess her but also to eliminate the dangers she created, a very primitive and simple emotion was sufficient.</t4044> <t4045>Falling in love would have been enough.</t4045> <t4046>But such a simple thing did not occur to anyone's mind or heart.</t4046> <t4047>Ursula no longer worried about her.</t4047> <t4048>At a time when she had not yet lost hope in raising her, she had tried to teach the girl household chores.</t4048> <t4049>"Men expect more than you think," she had said.</t4049> <t4050>"You need to cook, sweep the place, and deal with trivial matters and make them your concern."</t4050>
<t4051>Ursula was keeping herself busy by trying to teach her household chores and make her enjoy them.</t4051> <t4052>Because she believed that no man would be able to tolerate the girl's incredible sloppiness for even a day after satisfying his passion.</t4052>
<t4053>The birth of her son Jose Arcadio and Ursula's intention to raise him to become a pope distracted her from worrying about her great-grandson.</t4053>
<t4054>Ursula decided to leave Beautiful Remedios to her own fate.</t4054> <t4055>She believed that in this world where everything exists, there would also be a lazy man willing to put up with her sloppiness.</t4055> <t4056>Amaranta, on the other hand, had long given up on turning her into a useful woman.</t4056> <t4057>On the days when her niece couldn't properly turn the machine's handle, Amaranta had decided that the girl was mentally retarded.</t4057> <t4058>Seeing that Beautiful Remedios was not affected by the words of men, Amaranta would tease her, saying, -We'll have to put you in the lottery and sell you.</t4058>
<t4059>Later, when Ursula forced the girl to wear a veil over her face while going to church, Amaranta thought that this mysterious demeanor would be very enticing and that soon enough, a man who got captivated would show patience until he found a weak spot in the girl's heart.</t4059> <t4060>But when she saw that Beautiful Remedios rejected a suitor who, in many ways, could not be exchanged even for princes, Amaranta lost all hope.</t4060> <t4061>Fernanda, on the other hand, did not make the slightest effort to understand Beautiful Remedios.</t4061> <t4062>When she saw Beautiful Remedios dressed as a queen at that carnival, she thought she was an extraordinary creature.</t4062> <t4063>But when she saw her diving into her food with her hands, unable to give a single response that wasn't an example of foolishness, she did nothing but lament the long lives of the fools in the family.</t4063>
<t4064>No matter how much Colonel Aureliano Buendia tried to say that Beautiful Remedios was the smartest person he had ever seen in his life and that she constantly demonstrated this power with her ability to blame everything on others, no one listened to him and they left Beautiful Remedios to her own devices.</t4064> <t4065>Beautiful Remedios began to wander in the desert of loneliness without carrying her cross on her back, and to mature in her dreams without seeing nightmares.</t4065> <t4066>Her endless baths, untimely meals, and long, pensive silences continued on.</t4066> <t4067>Nothing changed until one day in March when Fernanda wanted to fold the sheets in the garden and called the women in the house to help her.</t4067> <t4068>They had just started folding the sheets when Amaranta saw that Beautiful Remedios had turned completely pale.</t4068>
<t4069>"Aren't you well?" he asked.</t4069>
<t4070>Beautiful Remedios, clinging to the other end of the sheet, replied with a pitiable smile, "No, I've never been this good."</t4070>
<t4071>Beautiful Remedios had just finished speaking when Fernanda heard a slight breeze billow and open the sheets in her hand.</t4071> <t4072>Amaranta felt a mysterious shiver in the lace of her petticoats and had to cling tightly to the sheet to avoid falling to the ground.</t4072> <t4073>Because at that moment, Beautiful Remedios' feet had lifted off the ground, and she had started to fly.</t4073> <t4074>At that time, Ursula, who was almost completely blind, was the only one who understood what this distinct breeze was, and she let go of the edges of the sheets and began to watch Beautiful Remedios wave among the fluttering sheets as she ascended with them.</t4074> <t4075>The sheets and Beautiful Remedios rose among the dahlias and cicadas, and by four o'clock, they had disappeared at a height unreachable even by the highest-flying birds.</t4075>
<t4076>Of course, the outsiders did not believe what was told and thought that Beautiful Remedios had succumbed to the inevitable fate of the queen bees, and that her family was trying to save her honor by fabricating this tale of flight.</t4076>
<t4077>Driven mad by jealousy, Fernanda finally had to accept this miracle.</t4077> <t4078>In the meantime, she did not forget to pray and ask God to send her sheets back.</t4078>
<t4079>Most of the people of Macondo believed in miracles.</t4079> <t4080>They even lit candles and held prayer ceremonies.</t4080> <t4081>This event would have been talked about for a long time, but the barbaric slaughter of the Aurelianos replaced astonishment with horror.</t4081>
<t4082>Colonel Aureliano Buendia, although he had never seen a definite sign, had foreseen the tragic ends of his sons.</t4082> <t4083>When his two sons, Aureliano Serrador and Aureliano Arcaya, who had come along with those flocking to Macondo, said they wanted to stay in Macondo, their father tried to dissuade them.</t4083> <t4084>He couldn't understand what his sons were doing in this town, which had become a dangerous place overnight.</t4084>
<t4085>However, Aureliano Centeno and Aureliano Triste, supported by Aureliano Segundo, gave them work.</t4085> <t4086>Colonel Aureliano Buendia opposed this decision, saying that they were not yet able to think clearly.</t4086> <t4087>Especially when he saw Mister Brown in the orange car with a canopy, scaring the dogs with the horn of the first automobile to arrive in Macondo, and witnessed the people running around with a slave-like excitement, the old soldier went mad with rage.</t4087> <t4088>He realized that since the days when men left their wives and children and went to war with rifles on their shoulders, people's morals and structures had changed.</t4088>
<t4089>After the Treaty of Neerlandia, the mayors were always figureheads, lacking initiative, chosen from among the docile and weary Conservatives of Macondo.</t4089> <t4090>Whenever Colonel Aureliano Buendia saw the barefoot policemen with their wooden batons, he would say, "This is a regime of the miserable."</t4090> <t4091>"A bunch of slackers have gathered," he would say.</t4091> <t4092>"We fought and worked so hard just to avoid painting our houses blue," he would say.</t4092> <t4093>However, after the banana company set foot in Macondo, the local officials were replaced by foreigners with dictatorial aspirations.</t4093> <t4094>According to Mister Brown, they were placed in an area surrounded by electric chicken wire to ensure they gained the respect their positions required and were not disturbed by the heat, mosquitoes, countless discomforts, and deprivations in the town.</t4094> <t4095>The old policemen were replaced by hired killers armed with machetes used for cutting cane.</t4095>
<t4096>Colonel Aureliano Buendia had shut himself in his workshop, pondering these changes.</t4096>
<t4097>For the first time during the years he spent buried in loneliness, he felt pain, horrified by these events that determined how wrong it was not to carry the war to the end, and he felt regret.</t4097> <t4098>While the Colonel was thinking about these things, the brother of Colonel Magnifico Zlisbal, who had long been forgotten, wanted to give sherbet to his seven-year-old grandson from one of the itinerant vendors roaming the field with their carts.</t4098> <t4099>The child accidentally bumped into a policeman and spilled the sherbet on the policeman's uniform.</t4099> <t4100>The barbarian brute chopped the child into pieces with the cleaver in his hand and with one blow, decapitated the grandfather who tried to stop him.</t4100> <t4101>While a few people were taking the dismembered man to his home, the entire town witnessed this incident.</t4101> <t4102>A woman was dragging the man's severed head by the hair and carrying the child's dismembered parts, which she had stuffed into a bloody sack, in her hand.</t4102>
<t4103>This was the last straw for Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t4103>
<t4104>He was now experiencing a similar rage to what he had felt when he saw the corpse of a woman who had been beaten to death because she was bitten by a rabid dog in her youth.</t4104>
<t4105>He looked at those gathered in front of the house and, with a deep voice filled with anger and disgust towards himself, he unleashed the hatred he could no longer bear in his heart upon them with all its intensity.</t4105>
<t4106>"These days, I will arm my sons so that we can get rid of these damned gringos!" he roared.</t4106>
<t4107>That week, in various places along the coastal region, the colonel's seventeen sons were shot like rabbits by unknown killers aiming at the ashen cross marks on their foreheads.</t4107><t4108>Aureliano Triste was shot by a bullet fired in the dark that pierced through the middle of his forehead as he was leaving the house with his mother at seven in the evening.</t4108><t4109>Aureliano Centeno was lying in his hammock at the factory, as usual.</t4109><t4110>An ice axe was embedded between his eyebrows.</t4110><t4111>After leaving the cinema with his lover, Aureliano Serrador dropped the girl off at her home and was walking through the brightly lit Turkish Street when someone from the crowd, whose identity could not be discerned, drew his gun and shot the young man in the forehead, causing him to fall into a boiling cauldron of pig fat.</t4111><t4112>A few minutes later, the door of the room where Aureliano Arcaya had secluded himself with a woman was pounded on.</t4112><t4113>Someone shouted, "Get out, they are killing your brothers."</t4113><t4114>According to the woman who was with him, Aureliano Arcaya jumped out of bed, and as soon as he opened the door, his head was shattered by the Mauser bullets that rained down on him.</t4114>
<t4115>On that night when death was prowling, while the household was preparing to light candles and pray for the four dead, Fernanda was frantically running through the streets searching for Aureliano Segundo.</t4115> <t4116>However, Petra Cotes, thinking that everyone named after the colonel would be killed, had hidden Aureliano Segundo in a cupboard.</t4116> <t4117>From the telegrams coming from various places in the coastal region, it was understood that the wrath of the unseen enemy was directed only at the brothers with the cross mark on their foreheads, and for four days and four nights, Petra Cotes did not take Aureliano Segundo out of the cupboard.</t4117>
<t4118>Amaranta took out the notebook in which she wrote information about her nephews and crossed out the names as telegrams arrived.</t4118> <t4119>Only the eldest remained.</t4119> <t4120>They remembered him very well because of the contrast between his very dark skin and his bright green eyes.</t4120> <t4121>His name was Aureliano Amador, he worked as a carpenter and lived in a village squeezed between the mountains.</t4121> <t4122>After waiting two weeks for the telegram that would bring news of his death, Aureliano Segundo sent a man to warn him, thinking he might not be aware of the danger looming over him.</t4122> <t4123>The man brought news that Aureliano Amador was alive.</t4123> <t4124>On the night of the massacre, two men had gone to his house, emptied their guns on him, but had missed the mark of the ashen cross.</t4124> <t4125>Aureliano Amador had jumped over the garden wall and escaped through the mountain crevices he knew like the back of his hand, befriending the Indians from whom he used to get wood.</t4125>
<t4126>He was never heard from again.</t4126>
<t4127>Those days were dark days for Colonel Aureliano Buendia.</t4127>
<t4128>The President sent a telegram expressing his condolences and promised that a wide-ranging investigation and inquiry would be launched.</t4128> <t4129>Again, by the President's order, the mayor bought four wreaths and attended the funeral.</t4129> <t4130>While trying to place the wreaths on the coffins, the colonel drove him away.</t4130> <t4131>After the funeral, he wrote a harsh telegram to the President and took it to the post office by hand.</t4131>
<t4132>The telegraph operator reported that he could not send this.</t4132> <t4133>Upon this, the colonel put the telegram in an envelope, expanding and enriching it with personal attacks and insults, and mailed it.</t4133> <t4134>As in the case of his wife's death, and the deaths of his closest friends during the war, this time too, the emotion he felt was not sorrow, but a blind and aimless rage, a sense of helplessness.</t4134> <t4135>He even accused Father Antonio Isabel of being an accomplice to the murderers for marking his sons with identifying signs that helped their enemies recognize them.</t4135>
<t4136>The senile priest, who could no longer organize his thoughts and bewildered the congregation with his nonsensical comments during his sermons, came home on that Wednesday when the children went to church, clutching the ash jar he had prepared, and attempted to mark the foreheads of the household members to prove that the ash would wash off.</t4136> <t4137>However, this disaster had instilled such terror in everyone that even Fernanda did not consent to this experiment.</t4137> <t4138>After that, it was never seen that anyone from the Buendia family knelt before the altar on Ash Wednesday before Easter.</t4138>
<t4139>Colonel Aureliano</t4139> <t4140>Buendia couldn't pull himself together for a long time.</t4140> <t4141>He stopped making fish.</t4141> <t4142>He could barely eat a few bites, dragging his blanket, grinding his teeth in anger, wandering around the house like a sleepwalker.</t4142> <t4143>After three months, his hair turned white, and the mustache he used to oil and curl so brightly hung down from the sides of his colorless lips.</t4143> <t4144>But his eyes regained that fiery, coal-like intensity that had surprised those present at his birth and had made chairs shake with his gaze when he was a child.</t4144> <t4145>The Colonel, driven mad with pain and anger, tried in vain to revive the intuitions that had guided him through the swamps of fame and glory in his youth.</t4145>
<t4146>He was utterly lost, left all alone in a house that felt completely foreign to him, where nothing and no one inside stirred even the slightest flicker of affection in his heart.</t4146> <t4147>Once, in the hope of finding a trace from the pre-war years, he opened Melquiades' room and was met with a heap of dust, filth, and debris accumulated from years of neglect.</t4147>
<t4148>Among the covers of books that no one reads anymore, within the moldy manuscripts decayed from dampness, a flower of a leaden color tinged with purple had bloomed, and this room, which was once the brightest and most airy place in the house, had filled with a heavy air of decayed memories.</t4148> <t4149>One morning, the Colonel found Ursula under the chestnut tree, crying, clinging to the knees of her dead husband.</t4149> <t4150>The strong old man, worn out from being in the open air for half a century, was Colonel Aureliano Buendia, the only one in the big house who could not see.</t4150> <t4151>Ursula said, -Say hello to your father.</t4151>
<t4152>The colonel paused for a moment in front of the chestnut tree and once again realized that the emptiness before him did not evoke any love within him.</t4152>
<t4153>-What is my father saying? he asked.</t4153>
<t4154>Ursula said, "I am very sad."</t4154> <t4155>"She is sad because she thought you were going to die."</t4155>
<t4156>The colonel responded with a smile: "Tell him," he said, "a person dies not when the time comes, but when they are able to die."</t4156>
<t4157>The prophecy of his dead father stirred the last remnants of pride in the colonel's heart.</t4157> <t4158>However, the colonel mistook this for a new source of strength.</t4158>
<t4159>Therefore, he tried to pressure Ursula to find out where in the garden she had buried the money that came out of the plaster saint statue.</t4159> <t4160>With the determination she had learned from past lessons, Ursula said, "You will never learn this."</t4160> <t4161>Then she added, "The owner of that fortune will come one day, and only they will retrieve the money."</t4161> <t4162>No one could understand why a man who was always extremely generous suddenly became so concerned about money.</t4162> <t4163>Moreover, the money he wanted was not to meet an urgent need.</t4163> <t4164>When Aureliano Segundo heard the amount of the fortune the Colonel was looking for, he was astounded.</t4164> <t4165>The former party friends he asked for money from were pretending not to be home and hiding everywhere to avoid meeting the Colonel.</t4165> <t4166>It was around that time that Colonel Aureliano Buendia was heard saying, "Today, the only difference between the Liberals and the Conservatives is that the Liberals go to church at five o'clock, while the Conservatives go at eight o'clock."</t4166>
<t4167>Despite all this, the colonel insisted so much, begged so much, and humiliated himself so much that, by getting help from here and there, by going everywhere and asking for money, he collected more money in eight months than Ursula had buried.</t4167> <t4168>Then he went to the bedridden Colonel Gerineldo Marquez to ask him to be by his side and assist him when he went to war.</t4168>
<t4169>In a certain period, even if he was in a wheelchair, the only person who could truly pull the rusty strings of the uprising was Colonel Gerineldo Marquez.</t4169>
<t4170>Neerlandia; after the armistice agreement was made, while Colonel Aureliano Buendia immersed himself in his golden fish, Colonel Gerineldo Marquez maintained his relationship with the rebel officers who had remained loyal to him until the final defeat.</t4170>
<t4171>He fought the battle of humiliation and degradation with them every day.</t4171> <t4172>Requests, wishes, 'come today, go tomorrow's, 'it will be done's, 'it's imminent's, 'we are carefully reviewing your situation's joined the battle with them.</t4172> <t4173>He struggled with them in the lost battles against the letters ending with 'respectfully' from those who should have signed the lifelong pension decisions but didn't.</t4173>
<t4174>The other war, that bloody war which lasted twenty years, had not worn them out as much as this war of endless postponements.</t4174> <t4175>Even Colonel Gerineldo Marquez, who had survived three assassinations, five wounds, and countless battles, could not withstand the relentless siege of waiting and succumbed to old age, dreaming of Amaranta among the diamond-shaped light patches seeping through the windows of a rented house.</t4175>
<t4176>He saw the latest news about the old soldiers in a newspaper.</t4176> <t4177>His former comrades-in-arms, whose names and identities he didn't know, had shamelessly posed for pictures next to the President.</t4177> <t4178>The President had given them each a badge with his own picture on it and a flag stained with blood and gunpowder to be draped over their coffins.</t4178> <t4179>The more honorable ones among them, however, were trying to get by with the help of others while waiting for the letter informing them of their pension, dying of hunger, cleansing themselves of anger, and decaying while crawling through the remnants of their former glorious days.</t4179>
<t4180>This is why Colonel Gerineldo Marquez couldn't help but pity Colonel Aureliano Buendia, who wanted him to join the life-and-death struggle to sweep away this corrupt, rotten, despicable regime supported by foreign invaders.</t4180>
<t4181>"Ah, Aureliano," he sighed.</t4181> <t4182>"I knew you were getting old, but now I see that you have aged much more than you appear."</t4182>
<t4183>In the bewilderment of her later years, Ursula didn't have much opportunity to raise Jose Arcadio in a manner befitting a Pope, and in the blink of an eye, the time came for the child to enter the seminary.</t4183> <t4184>His sister Meme, meanwhile, spent her days caught between Fernanda's rigidity and Amaranta's bitterness, and reached the age to attend the convent school.</t4184> <t4185>At this school, they would train her to become a piano virtuoso.</t4185> <t4186>Ursula couldn't quite determine whether the methods she used to shape and mold the soul of the papal candidate were effective.</t4186> <t4187>Nevertheless, she did not accept that she hadn't been able to devote enough attention to Jose Arcadio because she was now very old, barely able to move around, or unable to see her surroundings clearly due to the cataracts over her eyes; if there was any flaw in the child's upbringing, she believed it stemmed from the corruption of the times they lived in.</t4187>
<t4188>As I hear that daily realities and events slip away from my grasp,</t4188>
<t4189>"Years don't pass like they used to nowadays," she was saying.</t4189> <t4190>Ursula used to think that it took a long time for children to grow up.</t4190> <t4191>To prove this thought, it was enough for her to remember the past of her own children.</t4191>
<t4192>How much time had passed since his eldest son, Jose Arcadio, left with the gypsies.</t4192> <t4193>How many things had happened until Jose Arcadio returned, covered in snake-like mottled paints, speaking like an astronomer.</t4193> <t4194>How many changes had occurred in the house until Amaranta and Arcadio forgot the Indian language and learned Spanish.</t4194>
<t4195>Poor Jose Arcadio Buendia, how many times had he sat under the chestnut tree, baking in the sun, getting soaked by dew drops.</t4195> <t4196>They had mourned for a long time thinking Colonel Aureliano Buendia was dead, but when the colonel was finally brought back in a critically wounded state, they saw that despite all the years filled with wars and suffering, the colonel was not even fifty yet.</t4196> <t4197>In the past, Ursula would spend her entire day making animal-shaped sweets, yet she would still find time to take care of the children, looking into their eyes and determining when they needed castor oil.</t4197>
<t4198>However, now she had no work or occupation, she could wander around from morning till night with Jose Arcadio leaning on her hip, but time had become so corrupted that every task she started remained unfinished.</t4198> <t4199>The truth was different, of course: although Ursula had lost track of her own age, she could not accept that she had grown old, wanting to meddle in everything and unsettling those around her, disturbing the peace by asking strangers who came to the house if they had left a statue of a saint there to be picked up after the rainy season during the war.</t4199>
<t4200>No one knew for sure when Ursula's eyes had started to fail.</t4200> <t4201>Even in the years when she was so frail that she couldn't get out of bed, everyone thought she was bedridden because she was physically weak, not realizing she was blind.</t4201>
<t4202>Ursula had noticed this even before Jose Arcadio was born.</t4202> <t4203>At first, she thought the defect in her eyes was a temporary result of fatigue, so without letting anyone know, she boiled bones and drank their marrow, and applied honey to her eyes.</t4203>
<t4204>However, he soon realized that this was not something temporary, but that he was being constantly engulfed in darkness.</t4204>
<t4205>The deterioration in her eyesight reached such a point that she couldn't fully grasp even an invention like the electric bulb, because when the electricity was turned on, Ursula's eyes could only perceive a faint light.</t4205><t4206>Ursula didn't mention this to anyone, because once people realized she couldn't see, they would be convinced that she had become useless.</t4206><t4207>To see the things obscured by the curtain that had fallen over her eyes with the help of her memory, Ursula firmly imprinted in her mind where objects were placed, the distances between them, and people's voices.</t4207><t4208>Later, she found an unexpected helper.</t4208><t4209>In the dark world, smells became more defining than the color and mass of objects.</t4209><t4210>By distinguishing smells, Ursula freed herself from the shame of admitting that she had succumbed to nature.</t4210>
<t4211>In the darkness of her room, she could thread a needle and sew on a button, and she could tell when the milk was about to boil.</t4211> <t4212>She knew the location of everything with such precision that sometimes she forgot she was blind.</t4212> <t4213>Once, Fernanda lost her engagement ring, turned the house upside down, couldn't find it, but Ursula found the ring on a shelf in the children's bedroom.</t4213> <t4214>The reason was simple.</t4214> <t4215>While the others wandered around the house without paying attention to what they were doing, Ursula mobilized all her senses with full force to avoid being caught off guard.</t4215> <t4216>Soon, she noticed that everyone in the family unconsciously performed the same actions every day, followed the same paths, and repeated almost the same words at nearly the same times.</t4216> <t4217>It was only when they deviated from their daily routines that they risked losing something.</t4217>
<t4218>So when Fernanda lost her ring and panicked, Ursula remembered that on that day, unlike usual, she had entered the children's room, found a bedbug in Meme's bed, and put the mattresses out in the sun.</t4218> <t4219>Since the children were in the room, she guessed that Fernanda might have placed her ring on the shelf, the only place they couldn't reach.</t4219> <t4220>However, Fernanda was searching in vain in the places she frequented daily and didn't realize that daily habits made it harder to find lost items.</t4220>
<t4221>Taking care of and raising Jose Arcadio helped Ursula keep track of the smallest changes in the house.</t4221> <t4222>When she noticed that Amaranta was sewing clothes for the saint statues in her bedroom, Ursula, pretending to teach Jose Arcadio the colors, would say, "Come on, tell me, what color is the garment on Archangel Raphael's back?"</t4222>
<t4223>Ursula, by doing this repeatedly, learned from the child what she couldn't discern with her eyes.</t4223> <t4224>When Jose Arcadio reached the age to go to theology school, Ursula had learned to distinguish the colors by touching the garments of the saints.</t4224>
<t4225>Unexpected accidents happened from time to time, of course.</t4225>
<t4226>One day, while Amaranta was doing embroidery on the veranda with begonias, Ursula bumped into her.</t4226>

<t5120>Fernanda was indifferent to the chaos of those days.</t5120> <t5121>Since the day she fought with Aureliano Segundo for deciding Meme's fate without consulting him, she had cut off her connection with the outside world.</t5121> <t5122>Aureliano Segundo was determined to rescue his daughter with the help of the police if necessary, but Fernanda produced and showed some documents indicating that Meme had entered the convent of her own free will.</t5122>
<t5123>After the iron door closed behind Meme, she had indeed signed the papers of her own free will, and she did it with the indifference she showed while following the novice nun.</t5123> <t5124>Regardless, Aureliano Segundo could never believe in the validity of the documents his wife presented as evidence; just as he couldn't believe that Mauricio Babilonia had entered the garden to steal chickens.</t5124> <t5125>But the apparent evidence made it easier for him to silence his conscience, and without feeling any remorse, Aureliano Segundo took refuge in Petra Cotes' embrace and resumed his lavish spending and noisy festivities.</t5125>
<t5126>Fernanda, who remained oblivious to the unrest in the town and turned a deaf ear to Ursula's silent prophecies, tightened the last screw of her premeditated plan.</t5126> <t5127>She wrote a long letter to her son Jose Arcadio, who was about to enter the priesthood, informing him that his sister Renata had coughed up blood and finally found peace in the presence of God.</t5127> <t5128>Then, leaving the care of Amaranta Ursula to Santa Sofia de la Piedad, she resumed the correspondence she had paused due to Meme's troubles, dedicating all her time to communicating with invisible doctors.</t5128> <t5129>Her first task was to set a date for the postponed telepathic surgery.</t5129> <t5130>However, the invisible doctors advised that it would not be wise to proceed with the surgery as long as the turmoil in Macondo continued.</t5130> <t5131>Fernanda was in such a hurry for the surgery to be performed as soon as possible and was so unaware of what was happening in the town that she immediately wrote another letter to the doctors.</t5131>
<t5132>He reported that there was no confusion, no provocative action, and that everything that happened was the doing of his brother-in-law, who once obsessed over cockfighting and riverboats, and now was fixated on the nonsense of unionism.</t5132>
<t5133>Until that hot Wednesday when the old nun with a small basket on her arm knocked on the door, Fernanda and the doctors had not yet come to an agreement.</t5133> <t5134>When Santa Sofia de la Piedad opened the door, she thought a gift had arrived and wanted to take the small basket covered with a beautiful lace cloth.</t5134> <t5135>However, the nun did not give her the basket, because she had been strictly instructed to give it to Dona Fernanda del Carpio de Buendia with great secrecy.</t5135> <t5136>Inside the basket was Meme's son.</t5136>
<t5137>Fernanda's former religion teacher, in the letter he wrote, was informing that the child was born two months ago and since the mother did not open her mouth to speak, they baptized the child with his grandfather's name, calling him Aureliano.</t5137>
<t5138>Although Fernanda inwardly rebelled against this twist of fate, she found the strength to hide it from the nun.</t5138>
<t5139>Smiling, "We will say that we found the child in a basket floating on the river," he said.</t5139>
<t5140>The nun replied, "No one will believe this."</t5140>
<t5141>Fernanda said, "When it's written in the Bible, they believe it. Why don't they believe it when I say it?"</t5141>
<t5142>The nun sat at home, ate, and did not mention the child's words in the way they twisted her ear until the return train arrived.</t5142>
<t5143>However, Fernanda saw him as a witness to her disgrace and cursed the abolition of the tradition of hanging bearers of bad news in the Middle Ages.</t5143> <t5144>That's when, as soon as she went to the nun, she decided to drown the child in the water tank.</t5144> <t5145>But her heart was not strong enough.</t5145>
<t5146>He preferred to patiently wait for the day when God's infinite goodness would save him from this trouble.</t5146>
<t5147>When the tension among the people suddenly erupted without any prior warning, the new Aureliano was one year old.</t5147> <t5148>Until then, Jose Arcadio Segundo and the other union leaders, who had been underground, suddenly emerged one weekend and organized demonstrations in the towns of the banana region.</t5148>
<t5149>The police only maintained public order.</t5149> <t5150>But on Monday night, the leaders were individually gathered from their homes, shackled with one-kilogram weights on their feet, and thrown into the central prison of the province.</t5150> <t5151>Among them were Jose Arcadio Segundo and Lorenzo Gavilan, who was a colonel in the Mexican revolution and was in exile in Macondo.</t5151>
<t5152>Gavilan would constantly recount witnessing the heroics of his comrade Artemio Cruz.</t5152> <t5153>Since the government and the Banana Company could not reach an agreement on who would take responsibility for the care of the prisoners, the detainees were released after three months.</t5153> <t5154>This time, the workers mobilized to protest the lack of plumbing in their homes, the absence of healthcare services, and the harsh working conditions.</t5154> <t5155>The workers claimed that their wages were not paid in cash, but instead they were given coupons, which could only be used to buy Virginia ham at the company's store.</t5155> <t5156>Jose Arcadio Segundo was imprisoned for exposing the truth behind the coupon system.</t5156> <t5157>If the company's fruit-carrying ships did not bring goods to the store, they would have to return empty from the ports where they loaded bananas in New Orleans.</t5157> <t5158>However, the coupons given to the workers led to demands for goods that would provide cargo for the company's ships on their return journey.</t5158> <t5159>The other demands and complaints of the workers were well-known to everyone.</t5159> <t5160>The company's doctors would not examine sick workers; they would line them up in a single file at the dispensary, and the nurse, without any distinction, would place a copper sulfate-colored pill on the tongue of each one, whether they had malaria, gonorrhea, or constipation.</t5160>
<t5161>This method of treatment was so customary that children would get in line several times in a row, not swallow the pills placed on their tongues, and take them home to use as bingo chips.</t5161> <t5162>The workers at the company lived in overcrowded, deplorable barracks.</t5162> <t5163>Instead of building toilets where the workers lived, the engineers would bring portable field latrines, one for every fifty people, every Christmas and show everyone how to use them so they would last longer.</t5163>
<t5164>The once black-clad, now elderly lawyers who surrounded Colonel Aureliano Buendia and are now managed by the Banana Company, were rejecting the workers' demands with decisions that seemed neither magical nor miraculous.</t5164> <t5165>The workers prepared a collective agreement outlining their common demands.</t5165> <t5166>However, it took quite some time for them to officially notify the company of these demands.</t5166> <t5167>As soon as Mister Brown learned that the workers had signed the agreement, he attached his luxurious private carriage to the train, took the company's top representatives with him, and fled from Macondo.</t5167>
<t5168>However, a few of the workers cornered them in a brothel the following Saturday, and while Mister Brown was having a wild time completely naked with the women who helped set him up, they made him sign a copy of the contract.</t5168> <t5169>The lawyers in black suits proved in court that the man who signed the contract had no connection with the company, and to leave no doubt about it, they had the man imprisoned for fraud.</t5169> <t5170>Later, while Mister Brown was traveling under another name and in third class, the workers found him again and made him sign another copy of the contract.</t5170>
<t5171>Mister Brown appeared in court the next day with his hair dyed black and speaking with a perfect Spanish accent.</t5171>
<t5172>The lawyers said that he was not the manager of the Banana Company, Mister Jack Brown, born in Prattville, Alabama, but rather a modest pharmaceutical propagandist named Dagoberto Fonseca, who was born and raised in Macondo.</t5172> <t5173>After a while, faced with a new attempt by the workers to get the contract signed, the lawyers made Mister Brown's death report public.</t5173> <t5174>In the report, signed by consuls and foreign representatives as witnesses, it was documented that Mister Brown had died on the ninth of last June, being run over by a fire truck in Chicago.</t5174>
<t5175>The workers, weary of these sleights of hand, lost hope in the authorities in Macondo and presented their complaints to the supreme court.</t5175> <t5176>Here too, the lawyers swiftly proved that the demands put forward were invalid.</t5176>
<t5177>Because it was proven with documents that the Banana Company neither in the past, nor now, nor in the future has employed or will employ workers, and that the complainants were hired as temporary and contract laborers.</t5177> <t5178>Therefore, the Virginia ham story, the miracle cure stories, and the makeshift desert latrines fabrications held no meaning.</t5178> <t5179>This situation was determined by a court decision and it was grandly recorded in the minutes that the workers did not exist.</t5179>
<t5180>The big strike began.</t5180> <t5181>Work in the banana plantations came to a halt halfway, fruits rotted on the trees, and trains with one hundred and twenty wagons waited empty on the sidetracks.</t5181> <t5182>The workers who were not working filled the towns.</t5182>
<t5183>The Turkish Street experienced the hustle and bustle of a Saturday for several days.</t5183> <t5184>Hotel Jacob's billiard room had to remain open twenty-four hours a day.</t5184> <t5185>At the time it was announced that the army was tasked with restoring order, Jose Arcadio Segundo was playing billiards.</t5185> <t5186>Although Jose Arcadio Segundo was not one to believe in prophecies, this news still seemed to him like the harbinger of the death he had been expecting since the day he watched the man executed by firing squad with Colonel Gerineldo Marquez's permission.</t5186>
<t5187>But he did not lose his composure due to this bad premonition.</t5187> <t5188>He made the shot he had planned and sent the billiard ball to the desired spot.</t5188> <t5189>The sound of drums and trumpets heard shortly after, along with the people's rush, made Jose Arcadio Segundo realize that not only the billiard game but also the game he had been playing by himself since the execution scene had come to an end.</t5189> <t5190>Then he went out to the street and saw them.</t5190> <t5191>Three regiments were marching in step with the drumbeat.</t5191> <t5192>Their breaths, resembling the growl of a many-headed monster, seemed to emit poisonous vapor into the air shining with the midday sun.</t5192> <t5193>The soldiers were short, stocky, and animal-like in build.</t5193> <t5194>They were sweating like horses, smelling of sunburnt animal hide, and enduring with the stubborn and silent resistance typical of mountain people.</t5194> <t5195>Although the passage of the regiments lasted more than an hour, they were no different from a few squads circling in successive loops.</t5195>
<t5196>Because they were all equals, all children of the same whore, and they carried the weight of their backpacks and canteens, the shame of their bayoneted rifles, the incurable boil of blind obedience, and their so-called sense of honor with the same indifference.</t5196> <t5197>Ursula heard the passing of the soldiers from her shadowy bed and crossed herself.</t5197> <t5198>Santa Sofia de la Piedad leaned on the freshly ironed embroidered tablecloth, thinking of her son Jose Arcadio Segundo, and existed for a moment.</t5198> <t5199>At that moment, Jose Arcadio Segundo, without changing the expression on his face, was watching the passing soldiers from the door of Hotel Jacob.</t5199>
<t5200>Martial law was declared and all the powers to resolve the conflict were given to the army, but the army made no effort towards reconciliation.</t5200> <t5201>As soon as the soldiers arrived in Macondo, they put their rifles aside, cut bananas, loaded them, and operated the trains.</t5201> <t5202>The workers, who had been waiting without doing anything until that moment, entered the plantations with nothing but banana machetes in their hands and began to sabotage the sabotage.</t5202>
<t5203>They burned plantations and sales depots, dismantled railway tracks to stop trains advancing with machine gun fire, and cut telegraph and telephone lines.</t5203> <t5204>The irrigation canals were stained with blood.</t5204> <t5205>Mister Brown, who lived securely behind electric chicken wire with his family and fellow countrymen, was taken out of Macondo and transported to a safe place under military protection.</t5205> <t5206>When the situation showed the danger of turning into a civil war that would continue under bloody and unstable conditions, the authorities called for the workers to gather in Macondo.</t5206> <t5207>It was announced that the martial law commander, who had taken over the civil and military administration, would arrive on Friday to find a solution to the conflict.</t5207>
<t5208>Jose Arcadio Buendia was among the crowd gathered at the station early Monday morning.</t5208> <t5209>He had attended the meeting held by the union leaders and, along with Colonel Gavilan, was tasked with mingling with the crowd and managing it according to the developments of the events.</t5209>
<t5210>He was not in a good mood.</t5210> <t5211>His mouth felt dry.</t5211> <t5212>As he looked around, he saw soldiers lined up with machine guns around the small area and the Banana Company's site surrounded by barbed wire and protected by cannons.</t5212> <t5213>By around twelve o'clock, the crowd of over three thousand people, men and women, young and old, waiting for the train that never came, had filled the open space in front of the station and started to spill into the streets held by soldiers with machine guns.</t5213> <t5214>They had the air of people having fun at a fair rather than a waiting crowd.</t5214> <t5215>They had brought in pita carts and lemonade vendors from Turkish Street, and despite the tension of waiting and the scorching sun, they had not lost their cheerfulness.</t5215>
<t5216>As it approached three o'clock, rumors spread that the train would not arrive before the next day.</t5216>
<t5217>The crowd sighed in disappointment.</t5217> <t5218>Upon this, a lieutenant climbed onto the roof of the station where four machine guns were aimed at the crowd and told them to be quiet.</t5218> <t5219>Next to Jose Arcadio stood a fat, barefoot woman with two children, one four and the other seven years old.</t5219>
<t5220>She had taken the smallest of the children in her arms.</t5220> <t5221>Without knowing who Jose Arcadio Segundo was, she turned to him and asked him to take the other child in his arms so she could hear better.</t5221> <t5222>Jose Arcadio Segundo took the child onto his shoulder.</t5222> <t5223>Years later, that child would insistently try to convince those who did not believe his words and tell that he had listened to the Martial Law Command's declaration No. 4</t5223> <t5224> being read from an old gramophone horn used as a megaphone</t5224> <t5225>The proclamation bore the signatures of General Carlos Cortes Vargas and his secretary Major Enrique Garcia Isaza.</t5225>
<t5226>General, in the three-item statement consisting of eighty words, described the strikers as a handful of rascals and granted the soldiers the authority to kill.</t5226>
<t5227>After the declaration was read, amidst deafening protest noises, a captain climbed onto the station's roof, took the lieutenant's place, and indicated that he wanted to speak by shaking the megaphone.</t5227> <t5228>The crowd fell silent again.</t5228>
<t5229>The captain, in a tired and low voice, slowly began to speak, -Ladies and gentlemen.</t5229> <t5230>-I am giving you five minutes to disperse.</t5230>
<t5231>The starting whistle that marked the beginning of the given time was drowned out amidst boos and shouts.</t5231> <t5232>No one moved from their place.</t5232>
<t5233>The captain said in the same tone, "The five minutes are up."</t5233> <t5234>"I am giving you one more minute, after that we will open fire."</t5234>
<t5235>Jose Arcadio Segundo, who was breaking out in cold sweats, took the child off his shoulder and handed it to the woman.</t5235> <t5236>The woman murmured, "These rascals might just open fire."</t5236>
<t5237>Jose Arcadio Segundo did not have a chance to open his mouth.</t5237> <t5238>Because at that moment, he heard Colonel Gavilan's thick, hoarse voice shouting out the woman's words.</t5238> <t5239>Jose Arcadio Segundo, who was dizzy from the tension, affected by the miraculous depth of the silence, and convinced that nothing could move the crowd, which was gripped by a passion for death, stood on his tiptoes, raised his head higher than those in front of him, and for the first time in his life, raised his voice:</t5239>
<t5240>"You scoundrels!" he shouted.</t5240> <t5241>"Take that one minute you gave and shove it up your ass!"</t5241>
<t5242>What happened after his shouting did not scare anyone.</t5242> <t5243>Everyone thought they were seeing things.</t5243> <t5244>The captain gave the order to fire, and fourteen machine guns executed the command at that moment.</t5244> <t5245>But all of this seemed like a ridiculous game.</t5245>
<t5246>It was as if the machine guns were loaded with blank cartridges.</t5246> <t5247>Because although the rattling of the guns was heard and it was seen that they were spewing bullets without stopping, there was not the slightest reaction from the crowd.</t5247> <t5248>Not a sound, not a breath was heard from the crowd that suddenly stood as if turned to stone.</t5248> <t5249>Suddenly, a death scream rising from the direction of the station broke the spell.</t5249> <t5250>The sound heard was a cry of "Aaah, my mother," a voice that shook the earth and sky, a volcanic breath, a roar that changed worlds, and it exploded like a bomb in the middle of the crowd.</t5250> <t5251>As the crowd, seething in panic, swallowed and dragged the woman with the child in her arms, Jose Arcadio Segundo only had the chance to catch the other child.</t5251>
<t5252>Years later, that child would recount that day, without caring that they considered him a senile old man, and he would vividly describe how Jose Arcadio Segundo lifted him up and carried him through the terrified crowd as if floating, to a nearby street.</t5252> <t5253>Because the child was above everyone else, he could see the frenzied crowd in the square running towards the corner and the machine gunners opening fire, and at the same moment, several people shouting at once, -Get down!</t5253>
<t5254>He could hear them shouting, "Get down on the ground!"</t5254>
<t5255>The ones in front had already been mowed down by the first wave of bullets and had fallen to the ground.</t5255> <t5256>The survivors tried to retreat to a small area instead of lying down on the ground.</t5256> <t5257>However, the machine gunners across the street had also started firing.</t5257> <t5258>Trapped between two fires, the crowd resembled a dragon's tail flailing in two directions.</t5258>
<t5259>The crowd began to turn like a massive vortex.</t5259> <t5260>They were diminishing towards the center like an onion being peeled layer by layer by the unceasing fire of the machine guns.</t5260> <t5261>The child saw a woman kneeling in the open, holding her arms in a cross shape, mysteriously protected from the bullets.</t5261> <t5262>At the moment Jose Arcadio Segundo fell to the ground with his face covered in blood, he left the child there.</t5262> <t5263>Immediately afterward, the large crowd covered that void, the kneeling woman, the light in the arid sky, and this whore world where Ursula Iguara sold candies in the shape of animals in heaps.</t5263>
<t5264>When Jose Arcadio Segundo came to, he was lying on his back in the dark.</t5264> <t5265>He sensed that he was on an endless journey on a silent train.</t5265> <t5266>Clots of blood had dried on his head.</t5266> <t5267>All his bones ached.</t5267> <t5268>He felt an irresistible desire to sleep.</t5268> <t5269>Preparing to sleep for hours, far from that atmosphere of horror and terror, he turned to the least painful side and, as he turned, realized he was lying among the dead.</t5269> <t5270>Inside the wagon, there was no space except for the narrow path to pass through.</t5270> <t5271>Several hours must have passed since the massacre; because the corpses had reached the coldness and dullness that plaster would take on in the autumn air.</t5271> <t5272>Moreover, those who had placed them in the wagon had found enough time to stack them one on top of the other, just like they loaded banana bunches.</t5272>
<t5273>Jose Arcadio Segundo, from this nightmare</t5273>
<t5274>He started to move forward in the direction the train was going, dragging himself from car to car to escape and survive.</t5274> <t5275>As they passed through sleeping towns, in the light seeping through the boards, he saw the corpses of men, women, and children that would be thrown into the sea like unwanted rotten bananas.</t5275> <t5276>Among the corpses, he could only recognize a woman who sold lemonade in the square and General Gavilan, who still held the belt with a Marelya silver buckle that he had swung to clear a path through the panic.</t5276> <t5277>When he reached the frontmost car, he threw himself into the dark void and lay down beside the tracks, waiting for the train to pass.</t5277>
<t5278>This was the longest train Jose Arcadio Segundo had ever seen.</t5278> <t5279>It had nearly two hundred freight cars and three locomotives: one in the front, one in the back, and one in the middle.</t5279>
<t5280>The train was pitch dark, it hadn't even turned on its red and green lights.</t5280> <t5281>It was moving rapidly.</t5281> <t5282>The dark silhouettes of soldiers with machine guns could be seen on the carriages.</t5282>
<t5283>After midnight, it started raining cats and dogs.</t5283> <t5284>Jose Arcadio Segundo didn't know where he jumped off the train, but he figured that if he headed in the opposite direction of the train's path, he would reach Macondo.</t5284> <t5285>After walking for more than three hours with a headache and soaked to the bone, he could make out the first houses of the town as dawn broke.</t5285> <t5286>Unable to resist the smell of coffee, he entered a kitchen where a woman with a child in her lap was sitting by the stove.</t5286>
<t5287>In a weary voice, "Hello," he said.</t5287> <t5288>"I am Jose Arcadio Segundo Buendia."</t5288>
<t5289>To convince the woman that he was alive, he had spelled out his name by pressing each letter individually.</t5289> <t5290>It was a good thing he did, because when the woman saw this dirty and dark shadow covered in blood, she thought she had seen a ghost and froze as if death had stepped inside the door.</t5290> <t5291>Then she recognized him.</t5291> <t5292>She brought a blanket for him to wrap himself in until his clothes dried by the fire; she heated water to clean his wound, which was just a minor scratch, and gave him a clean cloth to wrap his head.</t5292> <t5293>Then, because she had heard that the Buendias drank it that way, she offered him a cup of black coffee and laid his clothes out in front of the fire.</t5293>
<t5294>Jose Arcadio Segundo did not speak until he finished his coffee.</t5294>
<t5295>"There were about three thousand," he murmured.</t5295>
<t5296>Jose Arcadio Segundo said, "The dead."</t5296> <t5297>"Everyone at the station must be dead."</t5297>
<t5298>The woman looked at him as if she pitied him.</t5298> <t5299>"No one has ever died here," she said.</t5299> <t5300>"Since the time of your great uncle the colonel, nothing has happened in Macondo."</t5300> <t5301>In the kitchens of the other three houses Jose Arcadio Segundo visited before reaching his own, they said the same thing: "No one has ever died."</t5301> <t5302>Jose Arcadio Segundo stepped out into the small area in front of the station.</t5302> <t5303>The trays of the pie vendors were stacked on top of each other.</t5303> <t5304>There was no trace of the massacre.</t5304> <t5305>The streets were completely empty under the constant rain.</t5305> <t5306>It seemed as if there was no one behind the closed doors and windows of the houses.</t5306> <t5307>The only thing indicating the presence of life in the town was the sound of the church bells calling for mass.</t5307> <t5308>Jose Arcadio Segundo stopped by Colonel Gavilan's house.</t5308> <t5309>A pregnant woman he had seen a few times before slammed the door in his face.</t5309> <t5310>In a fearful voice, she said, "Gavilan is gone."</t5310> <t5311>"He returned to his homeland."</t5311>
<t5312>The local police were on guard at the main entrance of the chicken wire fence, as usual.</t5312>
<t5313>They stood like stones in the rain with their rubber boots and oilskins.</t5313>
<t5314>When Jose Arcadio Segundo came home, he jumped over the garden wall and entered through the kitchen.</t5314> <t5315>Santa Sofia de la Piedad, afraid to raise her voice, said, "Don't let Señora Fernanda see you."</t5315> <t5316>"She will wake up soon," Santa Sofia de la Piedad, as if adhering to a secret agreement, led her son to the 'sitting room</t5316><t5317>'</t5317>
<t5318>he took it, fixed Melquiades' broken bed, and when Fernanda went to sleep at two in the afternoon, he put his son's meal on a plate and handed it out the window.</t5318>
<t5319>Aureliano Segundo had stayed overnight because he was at home when the rain started.</t5319> <t5320>Even though it was three o'clock, he was still waiting for the weather to clear up.</t5320> <t5321>When Santa Sofia de la Piedad secretly told him that his brother had arrived, Aureliano Segundo went into Melquiades' room to see him.</t5321> <t5322>Aureliano Segundo also did not believe the massacre story that Jose Arcadio Segundo told and the nightmarish journey of the corpse-filled train heading towards the sea.</t5322> <t5323>The previous evening, he had read in an extraordinary bulletin addressed to the nation that the workers had left the station and quietly dispersed in small groups to return to their homes.</t5323> <t5324>The bulletin also mentioned that the patriotic union leaders had reduced their demands to two points: they wanted nothing more than a reform in health services and the construction of toilets in the houses.</t5324> <t5325>In a later statement, it was mentioned that when the military authorities reached an agreement with the workers, they informed Mister Brown, who not only responded positively to these demands but also announced that he would hold a three-day and three-night celebration to mark the end of the dispute and would pay for the festivities out of his own pocket.</t5325>
<t5326>The only event not mentioned in the report was when the soldiers asked when the agreement would be signed, Mister Brown looked at the sky illuminated by lightning and shrugged as if to say, "How should I know?" and said, "When the rain stops."</t5326> <t5327>"As long as it rains, we are halting all activities."</t5327>
<t5328>It hadn't rained for three months, and there was a drought.</t5328> <t5329>But as soon as Mister Brown announced his decision, rain started pouring down in the banana region as if from buckets.</t5329> <t5330>Jose Arcadio Segundo got caught in this rain while returning to Macondo.</t5330> <t5331>A week later, it was still raining.</t5331> <t5332>The official account of the event had been repeated perhaps a thousand times, spread to every corner of the country through all the communication tools at the government's disposal, and eventually accepted.</t5332> <t5333>According to the authorities, there were no casualties; the workers, whose demands were met, had returned to their families, and the Banana Company had suspended its operations until the rains stopped.</t5333>
<t5334>Although martial law was maintained to take necessary precautions against a potential disaster caused by the incessant rain, the soldiers withdrew to their barracks.</t5334> <t5335>During the day, the soldiers roamed the waterlogged streets with their pant legs rolled up, floating paper boats in puddles along with the children.</t5335>
<t5336>At night, after getting drunk, they would pound on doors with the butts of their rifles, rouse suspicious individuals from their beds, and take them on journeys of no return.</t5336> <t5337>The process of hunting down and eradicating the ruffians, subversives, arsonists, murderers, and rebels mentioned in the 4th proclamation continued unabated.</t5337>
<t5338>However, the military authorities denied this situation, telling the relatives of the victims who filled the commanders' rooms to get some news, "You must have been dreaming."</t5338> <t5339>"Nothing happened in Macondo."</t5339> <t5340>"Just as nothing has happened until today, nothing will happen in the future."</t5340> <t5341>"How fortunate for this town," they said.</t5341>

<t5551>"I can't do anything right now," he was saying, "this rain won't last until the end of our lives."</t5551> <t5552>As the sacks in the pantry reached their bottoms, Fernanda's anger was reaching its peak.</t5552> <t5553>When her complaints and grumblings proved useless, one morning, all her anger broke loose and her speech, which started in a monotone like the monotonous strumming of a guitar, rose and rose as the hours passed.</t5553> <t5554>Aureliano Segundo only became aware of the situation the next morning after breakfast, when he was disturbed by the buzzing that drowned out the sound of the rain and was more fluid than it.</t5554> <t5555>The irritating buzzing was coming from Fernanda.</t5555> <t5556>She was wandering around the house, saying that she had been raised to be a queen, but in the end, she had become a servant in this madhouse, and while she was exhausting herself to keep this crumbling house standing, her lazy, debauched, immoral husband was lying on his back waiting for bread to rain down from the sky.</t5556>
<t5557>There was a heap of work to be done, she had reached her limit, she couldn't bear it anymore, from dawn to nightfall there were a myriad of things to repair and do, and when she went to bed at night, her eyes would sting from exhaustion, yet not a single soul would come up to her and ask -Good morning Fernanda, how are you, did you sleep well?</t5557> <t5558>No one even asked out of courtesy why she was so pale and wan, or why there were dark circles around her eyes when she got up in the morning.</t5558> <t5559>What more could be expected from this family who always saw her as a nuisance, a fool, and whispered behind her back?</t5559> <t5560>They mocked her, called her a church mouse, a prude, a know-it-all, a fraud.</t5560> <t5561>God forgive her sins, even Amaranta had said she was someone who stirred up her own mess, God forbid, she had uttered those words.</t5561> <t5562>Fernanda, because she had the fear of God in her heart, endured all this, but when that scoundrel Jose Arcadio Segundo said that the family had been doomed ever since this constipated woman, this conceited highlander, set foot in their house, she couldn't take it anymore.</t5562>
<t5563>God help us, what kind of words were these, she had said that she was a girl born from the seed of the highlanders sent by the government to kill workers, and she had said these words to her, to herself, who was the godchild of the Duke of Alba.</t5563>
<t5564>She was the daughter of such a noble family that the wives of the presidents were terrified when they heard of her lineage.</t5564> <t5565>They were going to insult a lady like her, a lady who carried noble blood in her veins, who had the right to line up eleven names in a row when signing her name, and who was the only one in this town full of ruffians who didn't lose her composure when she saw sixteen pieces of silver cutlery together!</t5565>
<t5566>Her husband, that filthy womanizer, would make fun of her, saying that so many forks and knives would be of no use to people, and that at most, centipedes would use them, and he would have a good laugh!</t5566> <t5567>There was no one else who knew, with eyes closed, when and from which side and with which glass white wine should be served, and when and from which side and with which glass red wine should be served.</t5567>
<t5568>That peasant who thought white wine should be drunk during the day and red wine at night -may God forgive his sins- was he like Amaranta?</t5568> <t5569>Who else in the whole coastal region had pissed in a golden chamber pot?</t5569> <t5570>Yet, -may God forgive his sins- Colonel Aureliano Buendia, with his Masonic head, had confronted him, mocking him by asking where he got that right, or if he shat basil instead of shit -yes, with those exact words.</t5570> <t5571>Upon this, Renata, his own daughter, who had secretly seen the chamber pot in his room, said that even if the pot was solid gold and had a crest on it, what was inside was the same old shit, and the worst kind because it was the shit of constipated highlanders.</t5571> <t5572>Think about it, if a man's own daughter spoke like that, what could be expected from the other members of the family?</t5572> <t5573>But no matter what, it was her right to expect her husband to be a bit more considerate, a bit more respectful.</t5573> <t5574>After all, by God's command, he was her husband, her spouse, the man who legally claimed her property.</t5574> <t5575>He had come to her father's house to take her, so he should have willingly taken on this responsibility.</t5575>
<t5576>In her father's house, she lacked nothing; she had everything she could eat in front of her, and what she didn't eat was behind her. She never had to lift a finger; her godfather had only sent a letter in a sealed envelope with his signet ring pressed into the wax, asking that his dear goddaughter's delicate hands not be injured by any work other than playing the piano.</t5576> <t5577>To kill time, she would weave funeral wreaths.</t5577> <t5578>However, that lunatic who was to be her husband had brought her from her home by making countless promises and assurances, bringing her to this hellish place where one could hardly breathe from the heat. Before she could even complete her fast, her husband had packed his trunk, taken his accordion, and gone to the arms of a despicable woman.</t5578> <t5579>According to what people said, to understand what kind of woman she was, it was enough to look at her from behind and see how she swayed her hips like a wild mare.</t5579> <t5580>That woman was a...</t5580> <t5581>she was something else entirely, the complete opposite of herself. Whether in a palace or a pigsty, she never lost her ladylike demeanor, remaining a lady both at the table and in bed. She was well-bred, knew what the fear of God was, and submitted to God's laws—everything that she herself was not.</t5581>
<t5582>Of course, her husband couldn't perform the acrobatics and reckless antics he did with the other woman with her.</t5582> <t5583>The other woman, like French courtesans, was ready for anything.</t5583> <t5584>She was even worse than them.</t5584> <t5585>At least they had the decency to hang a red lantern on their doors.</t5585>
<t5586>This was the behavior that Dona Renata Argote and Don Fernando del Carpio deemed worthy of their one and only beloved daughter.</t5586> <t5587>Yet her father was such a respectable man.</t5587>
<t5588>He was a devout Christian, a knight who had received the holy tomb insignia.</t5588> <t5589>Those who received this insignia, by the grace of God, would lie in their graves uncorrupted, their cheeks as fresh as a bride's, and their eyes as clear as emeralds.</t5589>
<t5590>Aureliano Segundo cut off his wife, "Now you're exaggerating. When they brought him here, your father was already starting to smell."</t5590>
<t5591>Watching for the moment when the woman would be off guard; he had listened to her patiently for a whole day.</t5591> <t5592>Although Fernanda did not pay attention to what he said, she lowered her voice.</t5592> <t5593>That evening at dinner, Fernanda's constant nagging drowned out the sound of the rain.</t5593> <t5594>Aureliano Segundo, without lifting his head from his plate, took a few bites and retired to his room early.</t5594> <t5595>The next morning at breakfast, Fernanda was exhausted.</t5595>
<t5596>He seemed not to have slept and appeared to be worn out from his own anger.</t5596> <t5597>Nevertheless, when her husband asked for a soft-boiled egg, instead of saying that the eggs had run out a week ago, Fernanda opened her mouth and closed her eyes, berating men whose minds were in their stomachs, complaining that they sat idly by and then demanded the moon when they came to the table.</t5597>
<t5598>Aureliano Segundo, as usual, took the children and withdrew to the room to look at the encyclopedia.</t5598> <t5599>So that Fernanda could continue to nag him, she pretended to tidy up Meme's room.</t5599> <t5600>While she was grumbling there, it took courage for Aureliano Segundo to open the encyclopedia for the children and say that they had printed a picture of Colonel Aureliano Buendia.</t5600>
<t5601>In the afternoon, after the children went to sleep, Aureliano Segundo went out to the veranda.</t5601>
<t5602>Fernanda did not give up, stepping on his branch, clinging to him with her nagging that resembled the buzz of a horsefly.</t5602> <t5603>Even though there was nothing left to eat at home but stones, she kept saying that her husband was lounging around like a Persian shah, watching the rain, and that he was useless anyway.</t5603>
<t5604>He was a wretch, only knew how to exploit like a sponge, was useless, had a softer disposition than cotton, was accustomed to living off women, did he think he was married to the wife of the Prophet Jonah who amused himself by listening to whale stories?</t5604> <t5605>Aureliano Segundo, as if he hadn't heard a word, listened to his wife for more than two hours without losing his composure.</t5605> <t5606>Towards evening, he didn't interrupt his wife until he could no longer bear the incessant noise in his head, like a drum beating.</t5606>
<t5607>Then, he politely requested, "Please stop now."</t5607>
<t5608>Fernanda, on the contrary, raised her voice.</t5608> <t5609>"Why should I cut it?" she said.</t5609>
<t5610>Those who don't want to listen to me can leave and go somewhere else.</t5610> <t5611>Then Aureliano Segundo lost control of himself.</t5611> <t5612>He slowly stood up.</t5612> <t5613>He had an indifference as if he was getting up to stretch.</t5613> <t5614>Then, with an extremely methodical and orderly rage, he grabbed the begonia pots, the fern barrels, and the hydrangea pots one by one and smashed them on the ground.</t5614> <t5615>Fernanda was scared.</t5615> <t5616>Because she hadn't really understood the tension caused by the nagging she had maintained until that moment.</t5616>
<t5617>Now it was far too late to calm things down and fix the situation.</t5617> <t5618>In the drunkenness brought on by this release, Aureliano Segundo, still not hurrying at all, broke the glass of the cabinet where the porcelain sets were kept, took out the porcelains one by one, and smashed them on the floor.</t5618> <t5619>Just as he had once covered the house from top to bottom with banknotes, with the same calmness and systematic work, he now began to throw the Bohemian crystals against the wall.</t5619> <t5620>Then he shattered everything that could be broken, from hand-painted vases, pictures of young girls in flowered boats, gilded framed mirrors, in short, everything from the living room to the pantry; finally, he threw the large jar in the kitchen into the garden.</t5620> <t5621>The jar shattered in the middle of the garden with a hollow explosion.</t5621> <t5622>Then Aureliano Segundo washed his hands, pulled the oilcloth over his head, and left.</t5622> <t5623>Before midnight, he returned with a few bundles of dried meat, a few bags of rice, worm-eaten corn, and a couple of bunches of rotten bananas.</t5623> <t5624>After that, there was never a shortage of food in the house.</t5624>
<t5625>Amaranta Ursula and little Aureliano would always remember their rainy days as a happy period.</t5625> <t5626>Despite all of Fernanda's strictness, the children would dive into and out of the puddles in the garden, catch lizards and cut them, and when Santa Sofia de la Piedad turned her back, they would play a game of supposedly poisoning the soup by adding the dust from butterfly wings.</t5626> <t5627>Their main entertainment was Ursula.</t5627> <t5628>The children saw her as a big, unbreakable doll, wrapping her in colorful blankets, painting her face with soot and coal, and carrying her from place to place.</t5628> <t5629>Once, just like they did with the frogs, they almost gouged out Ursula's eyes with the gardener's shears.</t5629> <t5630>Nothing amused them as much as Ursula's ramblings.</t5630> <t5631>In the third year of the rain, Ursula's brain really started to soften.</t5631> <t5632>She was gradually losing her grasp on reality, mixing up today and yesterday.</t5632> <t5633>She saw events from her distant past as if they had just happened.</t5633> <t5634>Once, she even cried non-stop for three days because she thought her great-grandmother Petronila Iguaran, who had been dead for over a century, had just died.</t5634>
<t5635>His mind became so confused that he began to think of little Aureliano as his own son, the colonel, and to see him as if he were at the age when he went to see ice with his father.</t5635> <t5636>At that time, he also thought of Jose Arcadio, who was studying at the seminary, as his eldest son who had run away with the gypsies.</t5636> <t5637>He constantly talked about the family, listing all the relatives.</t5637> <t5638>Eventually, the children learned to dream of being together not only with relatives who had died long ago but also with people who had lived at different times.</t5638> <t5639>Ursula, with ashes sprinkled in her hair and a red scarf tied around her face, would sit on her bed and listen with pleasure as the children vividly described these unreal relatives as if they had seen them with their own eyes.</t5639> <t5640>Ursula would talk about events that had happened long before she was born, rejoice in the good news they brought, and weep with them over the deaths of people who had lived long after the times of these imaginary guests.</t5640>
<t5641>Not long after, a situation that caught the children's attention occurred.</t5641> <t5642>Ursula had a question that she directed to each of the people she imagined, one by one.</t5642> <t5643>She was investigating who had brought the life-sized statue of Saint Joseph into the house to keep it there until the rains stopped.</t5643> <t5644>Thus, the buried treasure, known only to Ursula, came to Aureliano Segundo's mind.</t5644> <t5645>However, no matter how roundabout his questions were, no matter how much he tried to coax her, he couldn't make the woman talk.</t5645> <t5646>Despite all her confusion, Ursula still retained the awareness to not reveal the secret of the buried gold to anyone other than its true owner.</t5646> <t5647>She was so strict and cunning on this matter that when Aureliano Segundo tried to pass off one of his friends as the owner of the gold, Ursula bewildered the man with her questions, making him look ridiculous.</t5647>
<t5648>After concluding that Aureliano Segundo would definitely take Ursula's secret to the grave, he hired a bunch of workers under the pretext of digging water channels in the front and back yards and started having the garden excavated.</t5648>
<t5649>He himself took iron rods and various types of metal detectors in hand and examined the soil.</t5649> <t5650>Despite their exhaustive efforts for three months, they found nothing resembling gold.</t5650> <t5651>Upon this, Aureliano Segundo, thinking that playing cards might be more useful than the workers, went to Pilar Ternera.</t5651> <t5652>However, Pilar said that unless Ursula cut the cards, they would not reach a conclusion.</t5652> <t5653>At the same time, not hesitating to specify the treasure down to the last penny, she stated that there were exactly seven thousand two hundred and fourteen gold coins in three sacks tied with copper wire; and that the sacks were buried within a circle with a radius of one hundred meters, centered on Ursula's bed.</t5653> <t5654>For this treasure to be found, the rains had to stop and the sun of three consecutive Junes had to dry the piles of mud and turn them into dust.</t5654>
<t5655>Aureliano Segundo, who compared the roundness and ambiguity of the fortune teller's words to those of the spirit summoners, preferred to continue the excavations despite being in August and needing to wait at least three years for the fortune to come true.</t5655> <t5656>The first thing that surprised and confused him was that the distance between Ursula's bed and the back garden wall was exactly three hundred meters.</t5656> <t5657>When Fernanda saw her husband meticulously measuring the garden and heard him telling the workers to dig the channels one meter deeper, she believed that he, like his twin brother, was also a madman.</t5657> <t5658>However, Aureliano Segundo, who was caught up in a passion for research comparable to the effort his great-grandfather showed while searching for a path, melted the last of the fat layers he had accumulated in this endeavor.</t5658> <t5659>He began to resemble his twin brother again as before.</t5659> <t5660>This resemblance came not only from his thinning but also from adopting a pensive and introverted demeanor like his brother.</t5660> <t5661>He no longer dealt with the children.</t5661> <t5662>He wandered around covered in mud from head to toe, snacking in the kitchen corner whenever he felt like it, and only answered Santa Sofia de la Piedad's questions once in a blue moon.</t5662> <t5663>Seeing her husband working so hard, Fernanda, who thought he was incapable of doing any work, attributed his sullenness to deprivation and fatigue, his silence to asceticism, and his stubbornness to a resilient faith, feeling regret for having scolded him as a loafer all this time.</t5663>
<t5664>However, Aureliano was in no mood to compromise at that time.</t5664> <t5665>He was burying himself in the decayed branches and flowers up to his neck, and after finishing the front and back gardens, he started cleaning the flower beds.</t5665> <t5666>He had dug so deeply under the eastern wing of the house, reaching the foundation, that one night the household, awakened by the shaking of the house and the sounds coming from underground, thought there was an earthquake.</t5666> <t5667>Three of the rooms were collapsing.</t5667> <t5668>A deep crack opened between Fernanda's room and the veranda.</t5668> <t5669>Even though the house was collapsing, Aureliano Segundo did not pause his investigation.</t5669> <t5670>Even when their last hopes were extinguished and the wisest thing seemed to be to wait for what the fortune would say, Aureliano Segundo did not give up.</t5670> <t5671>He repaired the collapsing foundation, filled the crack with mortar, and continued digging along the side of the house towards the west.</t5671> <t5672>In the second week of June the following year, when the rain had lightened, the clouds began to disperse, and the weather was about to clear up, Aureliano Segundo was still digging along the side of the house.</t5672>
<t5673>Indeed, the weather cleared up.</t5673> <t5674>On a Friday at two o'clock, the world was illuminated by a sun as red as brick dust and as cool as water.</t5674> <t5675>After that, it didn't rain for ten years.</t5675>
<t5676>Macondo had turned into ruins.</t5676> <t5677>In the mud-swamped streets, broken furniture and animal skeletons covered with red lilies stood as the last memories of those who left Macondo as crazily as they had come.</t5677> <t5678>The houses hastily built during the banana plague were abandoned.</t5678> <t5679>The Banana Company dismantled its facilities.</t5679> <t5680>All that remained of the old wire-fenced site was a heap of ruins.</t5680>
