<t5681>Wooden houses, breezy cool terraces thought for afternoon card parties, had scattered and flown away as if they were the harbinger of the divine hurricane that would erase Macondo from the earth years later.</t5681> <t5682>The only human trace left from that relentless wind was Patricia Brown's glove, found in a car crushed among wild violets.</t5682> <t5683>The magical area discovered by Jose Arcadio Buendia during the town's founding days, and later used for banana plantations, became a swamp covered with decaying roots.</t5683>
<t5684>Even the silent foam of the sea could now be seen on the horizon.</t5684> <t5685>Aureliano Segunda put on his dry clothes and went out into the street on the first Sunday to rediscover the town, feeling great sorrow.</t5685> <t5686>Those who survived the disaster, that is, the Macondolans from before the Banana Company storm, were sitting in the middle of the street, enjoying the first sunny weather.</t5686>
<t5687>The moss green that the rain hit on their faces and the bitter smell of being cornered were still on them, yet the joy of reclaiming the town they were born in warmed their hearts.</t5687>
<t5688>The Turkish Street had regained its old appearance.</t5688> <t5689>In the days when Arabs with sandaled feet and ringed ears traveled the world bartering trinkets with parrots and saw Macondo as a place to pause their age-old wanderings, the Turkish Street was in the same condition as it is now.</t5689> <t5690>The goods that had survived the rain were scattered on the stalls, the fabrics hanging over the doors were moldy in places, the stalls had been gnawed by worms, and the walls were eroded by dampness.</t5690> <t5691>Yet, the Arabs of the third generation, just like their fathers and grandfathers, sat in the same places and in the same manner as their ancestors, indifferent, carefree, and immune to time and disasters.</t5691>
<t5692>Even after the insomnia plague and after Colonel Aureliano Buendía's thirty-two wars, they continued to sit like this, whether alive or dead.</t5692> <t5693>Aureliano Segundo, amazed at their resilience in the face of the collapse and disintegration of the gambling tables, the pita stands, the shooting galleries, and the passage where they interpreted dreams and told fortunes, approached the Arabs with his usual boldness and went door to door asking which mysterious sources they relied on to avoid being swept away by the storm and which devilish inventions they used to avoid drowning.</t5693>
<t5694>The Arabs, laughing slyly and rolling their eyes, gave the same answer in unison: -By swimming.</t5694>
<t5695>Perhaps the only person among the locals who had an Arab heart was Petra Cotes.</t5695>
<t5696>He had watched his barns being razed to the ground, seen his granary collapse in the storm, but he had managed to keep his house standing.</t5696> <t5697>During the second year, he had sent multiple messages to Aureliano Segundo, who replied that he did not know when he would go to his house, but whenever he did, he would bring a chest of gold to lay on the bedroom floors.</t5697><t5698>-</t5698> <t5699>While Petra Cotes was searching her heart for the strength to resist this disaster, she encountered a profound and justified anger deep within and, with that anger, vowed to rebuild the fortune that her lover had squandered and that the deluge had swept away.</t5699> <t5700>Her decision was so resolute that, eight months after Aureliano Segundo received the last message, he went to her house and found her with disheveled hair, a greenish complexion, and skin covered with scabies marks, working feverishly at the table preparing raffle tickets for household items.</t5700>
<t5701>Aureliano Segundo was taken aback when he saw the situation.</t5701> <t5702>When Petra Cotes saw that he was dirty and silent, she thought that the one who came to see her was not her great love, but his twin brother.</t5702>
<t5703>Aureliano Segundo said, "You must be crazy, are you going to put animal bones in the lottery?"</t5703>
<t5704>Then Petra Cotes told him to take a look at the bedroom.</t5704> <t5705>And Aureliano Segundo saw the mule.</t5705> <t5706>It was just as skin and bones as its mistress, but it still looked as lively and determined as she did.</t5706> <t5707>Petra Cotes had fed the mule with her own wrath, and when the hay, corn, and dry stalks ran out, she brought it into her bedroom and fed it with those crisp linen sheets, Persian carpets, fringed bedspreads, velvet curtains, and the silk canopy with gold embroidery over the bed.</t5707>
<t5708>Ursula had to make a great effort to fulfill the promise she made when the weather improved.</t5708> <t5709>In August, with the dry wind that began to blow, wilting the rose bushes, hardening the mud piles like stone, and raising angry dust clouds that would never lift from the rusty zinc roofs and century-old almond trees, Ursula's waves of consciousness, which had thinned during the rains, increased, and she began to gather her wits.</t5709>
<t5710>When Ursula realized that she had been the children's toy for more than three years, she raised hell, started cursing and swearing.</t5710> <t5711>She washed her face, threw off the colorful pieces of fabric on her back, discarded the dried lizards, frogs, amulets, and Arab necklaces that the children had attached here and there, and for the first time since Amaranta's death, she got out of bed without asking for anyone's help and joined the household.</t5711>
<t5712>His heart never faltered, it guided him in the dark.</t5712> <t5713>Those who saw him stumble or those who bumped into his arm, which he always kept at head level, thought there was something wrong with Ursula's body, never considering that he might be blind.</t5713>
<t5714>After the first repair of the house, it wasn't necessary for Ursula to see to understand that the carefully tended flower beds had been ruined by the rain, then overturned by Aureliano Segundo's excavations; that the walls and floor cements had cracked, the furniture had aged and faded, the doors had come off their hinges, and that the whole family had, with a despair unimaginable in her youth, let everything go to ruin.</t5714>
<t5715>While wandering through the empty bedrooms with her cane, she could hear the constant ticking of the wood-eating worms, the rustling of the moths in the wardrobes, and the nerve-wracking noise of the huge red ants that had emerged during the flood and were gnawing at the house's foundations.</t5715> <t5716>One day, she opened the chest where the saint statues were kept, then called Santa Sofia de la Piedad and told her to clean out the cockroaches that had shredded the clothes in the chest and swarmed over her as soon as the lid was opened.</t5716> <t5717>"A person cannot live in such neglect," she said.</t5717> <t5718>"If this continues, we will become food for the animals."</t5718>
<t5719>From that moment on, Ursula did not stop for a moment.</t5719> <t5720>She would wake up before dawn and put anyone she could catch, whether child or adult, to work.</t5720> <t5721>She laid out the few usable clothes in the sun and eradicated the cockroaches with various insecticides.</t5721>
<t5722>He plugged the wormholes in the doors and windows and the ant nests with quicklime.</t5722> <t5723>This repair frenzy eventually brought Ursula to the forgotten rooms.</t5723> <t5724>She removed the cobwebs from the room where Jose Arcadio Buendia lost his mind over alchemy, cleaned its filth; she straightened the silver workshop that the soldiers had ransacked, and finally, to understand the state of the room, she asked for the keys to Melquiades' room.</t5724> <t5725>Santa Sofia de la Piedad, trying to comply with Jose Arcadio Segundo's wish that no one enter the room until it was certain he was dead, went to great lengths to deceive Ursula.</t5725> <t5726>However, since Ursula was determined not to surrender even the farthest corners of the house to an invasion of insects, she overcame all the obstacles put in her way.</t5726> <t5727>After insisting for three days, she finally managed to get the door opened.</t5727> <t5728>When the door opened, she had to hold onto it to avoid fainting from the stench that hit her face.</t5728> <t5729>It took her only two seconds to remember that the seventy-two chairs bought for the girls coming from school were there and that the soldiers who raided the house on a rainy night did not find Jose Arcadio Segundo.</t5729>
<t5730>As if he were seeing everything, he cried out, "May God have mercy on us!"</t5730> <t5731>"We put in so much effort to discipline you, and in the end, you live in filth like pigs, is this acceptable?"</t5731>
<t5732>Jose Arcadio Segundo was reading the manuscripts.</t5732> <t5733>The only distinguishable things on his face, which was covered in hair, were his motionless eyes and his teeth, which had turned green at the roots.</t5733> <t5734>When Jose Arcadio Segundo recognized his great-grandmother's voice, he turned his head towards the door, tried to smile, and unknowingly repeated an old saying of Ursula's.</t5734>
<t5735>"What were you expecting?" he murmured.</t5735> <t5736>"Time is passing by."</t5736>
<t5737>Ursula said, "Yes, it is."</t5737> <t5738>"But it doesn't pass quickly either."</t5738>
<t5739>As soon as he said it, he realized that he had repeated the words of Colonel Aureliano Buendia in his execution cell, and as he had said, he couldn't help but shudder at this moment, which proved that time did not pass but circled around in a loop.</t5739> <t5740>But even at that moment, he didn't let himself go, scolded Jose Segundo like a child, told him to wash up and shave, and to help with the house repairs.</t5740>
<t5741>The idea of leaving the room that had brought him peace horrified Jose Arcadio Segundo.</t5741> <t5742>He shouted that no human being had the power to make him leave the room, that he did not want to see the two hundred-car train loaded with corpses departing from Macondo every evening.</t5742> <t5743>"Everyone at the station was on the train," he shouted.</t5743> <t5744>"There were exactly three thousand four hundred and eight of them."</t5744> <t5745>It was only then that Ursula realized he was living in a more insurmountable world of shadows than her own, an unreachable and desolate realm like that of his great-grandfather.</t5745>
<t5746>Ursula left Jose Arcadio Segundo in the room without bothering him.</t5746> <t5747>But she did not have a lock installed.</t5747> <t5748>She kept one of the chairs and had the others removed.</t5748> <t5749>She started having the room cleaned every day.</t5749> <t5750>She tried to keep Jose Arcadio Segundo as clean and presentable as during his great-grandfather's long captivity under the chestnut tree.</t5750> <t5751>At first, Fernanda interpreted this intense activity as a result of senility and struggled to hide her weariness and frustration.</t5751> <t5752>It was around that time that Jose Arcadio wrote that he would come to Macondo from Rome before taking his priestly vows.</t5752> <t5753>This time, Fernanda gathered her skirt around her waist.</t5753> <t5754>She was at a loss about what to do to prevent her son from seeing the house as gloomy, sometimes watering the flowers four times a day.</t5754> <t5755>With the same concerns, she sped up her correspondence with the invisible doctors and restored the ferns, begonias, and hydrangeas that Aureliano Segundo's destructive rage had scattered, even before Ursula noticed the pots were out of place.</t5755> <t5756>Then she sold the silverware and replaced it with ceramic plates, tin bowls, iron spoons, and cotton tablecloths.</t5756> <t5757>Thus, the sideboards, accustomed to the porcelain of the India company and Bohemian crystals, were left forlorn.</t5757>
<t5758>Ursula was always looking to take things one step further.</t5758> <t5759>"Open the windows and doors," she shouted, "Cook some meat and fish, get the largest turtles you can find, let strangers come and sprawl in every corner they find, let them pee at the base of the rose bushes, let them eat as much as they want, let them stain and muddy everything with their boots, let them do whatever they want to us, only then can we stop the ruin," she said.</t5759> <t5760>It was an empty dream.</t5760> <t5761>Ursula was now excessively old and could no longer repeat the miracle of the animal-shaped candies.</t5761> <t5762>None of her children or grandchildren had inherited her strength.</t5762> <t5763>In the end, Fernanda's words came true and the house remained closed.</t5763>
<t5764>Aureliano Segundo, who had moved his trunks back to Petra Cotes' house, could only find time to take care of his family just enough to keep them from starving.</t5764> <t5765>With the money they obtained by putting the mule in the lottery, Petra Cotes and Aureliano Segundo bought a few more heads of cattle and established a primitive lottery system.</t5765>
<t5766>Aureliano Segundo went door to door selling tickets that he had colored with ink to make them look more appealing and convincing, and he perhaps did not realize that many people bought tickets out of an old sense of gratitude or simply out of pity.</t5766> <t5767>Undoubtedly, even a buyer driven by a sense of pity did not forget that by spending twenty cents, they had a chance to win a pig, and by spending thirty-two cents, they could win a cow.</t5767>
<t5768>They became so invested in this matter that Petra Cotes' garden started to be packed on Tuesday nights.</t5768>
<t5769>Everyone eagerly awaited the moment when a child randomly selected from the crowd would draw the tickets from the bag.</t5769> <t5770>Soon, these Tuesday draws took on the appearance of a weekly fair.</t5770> <t5771>As night fell, food and drink stalls were set up in the garden.</t5771> <t5772>Most of the winners, on the condition that someone else would buy drinks and play music, would immediately slaughter and roast the animals they had won right there on the spot.</t5772>
<t5773>Thus, without understanding how it happened, Aureliano Segundo found himself once again in the midst of festivities with an accordion in his hand.</t5773>
<t5774>These gatherings, which were humble copies of the festivities of old days, proved to Aureliano Segundo how much he had lost his former cheerfulness and how his skill in organizing entertainment had become bland.</t5774> <t5775>He had become a completely different man.</t5775> <t5776>His one hundred and twenty-kilogram body from the days when he challenged the elephant had dropped to seventy-five kilograms, and his swollen, red face that resembled a turtle had wrinkled like iguana skin.</t5776> <t5777>He would quickly get bored and tired easily.</t5777> <t5778>However, in Petra Cotes' eyes, he had never been as unique a person as he was in those days.</t5778>
<t5779>Perhaps the feeling of pity the woman felt was mixed with love, or perhaps the sense of loneliness and helplessness that poverty had made them both experience was bringing them closer.</t5779> <t5780>The broken bed had ceased to be a scene of wild lovemaking and had become a warm refuge.</t5780> <t5781>In the room stripped of the mirrors lined up for sale to buy lottery animals and the silks and velvets devoured by the mule, they sat up late like sleepless grandmothers and grandfathers, valuing their sleepless nights by keeping accounts and adding up the pennies they once squandered for pleasure.</t5781> <t5782>Sometimes the crowing of roosters caught them while they were stacking money.</t5782> <t5783>They would take a little from one of the money towers they had stacked and add it to another, calculating that this much would be enough to keep Fernanda's mouth shut, this much would buy shoes for Amaranta Ursula, with this they could buy clothes for Santa Sofia de la Piedad who hadn't worn anything new since all the commotion, this would be Ursula's coffin money if she died, this was the coffee money that increased by two cents per kilo every three months, this was the sugar share that tasted less every day, with this they could buy lumber that hadn't dried yet after the rain, and this would be the money for the paper and colored ink needed to make tickets.</t5783> <t5784>The rest, after all the tickets were sold, would be the consolation prize for the lottery winner because the week's prize calf turned out to be scrawny and they were left with nothing but the animal's hide.</t5784>
<t5785>These modest accounts were so free from ulterior motives that they almost always allocated the largest share to Fernanda, not because they wanted to silence their consciences or because they pitied her, but because her comfort was more important to them than their own.</t5785> <t5786>In fact, without realizing it, both of them had started to see Fernanda as the daughter they had always wanted but could never have.</t5786> <t5787>Once, just so Fernanda could buy a Dutch tablecloth, Petra Cotes and Aureliano Segundo ate nothing but dry bread for three days.</t5787> <t5788>No matter what they did, no matter how hard they tried, no matter how many pennies they managed to save, they never heard the words "Go ahead, my servant," and they could only barely stay afloat by spending their savings little by little.</t5788>
<t5789>On the days when accounts reached an impasse, they were deeply pondering how the world had changed so much, why animals no longer reproduced as they used to, why money slipped through their fingers, and why those who recently lit bonfires with banknotes for fun now considered it robbery to ask for twelve cents for a lottery ticket worth six chickens.</t5789> <t5790>Aureliano Segundo thought that the evil was not in the world but in Petra Cotes' heart, that the animals became infertile and money lost its value because of a change in the woman's heart during the deluge, but he couldn't reveal this.</t5790> <t5791>Consumed by this suspicion, Aureliano Segundo began to scrutinize the woman's feelings; he delved so deep that, while seeking interest, he found love.</t5791> <t5792>Because while trying to make the woman love him, he ended up falling in love with her.</t5792>
<t5793>As Petra Cotes saw Aureliano Segundo's love growing, she began to love him more and more, and in the autumn of her life, she started to believe once again that when two hearts unite, even a barn can become a paradise.</t5793> <t5794>Both of them began to view their past excesses, that vast fortune, and their unrestrained lovemaking as unnecessary and troublesome, and they regretted wasting so many years of their lives to find the paradise of shared solitude.</t5794> <t5795>After living for years in a barren chaos, they began to savor the miracle of being madly in love and being able to love each other as much at the table as in bed.</t5795> <t5796>They became so happy that even when they were like two worn-out ping-pong balls, they did not stop rejoicing like children and playing like puppy dogs.</t5796>
<t5797>The lottery was not very profitable.</t5797> <t5798>At first, Aureliano Segundo would lock himself in the room that had once been the farm's office for three days a week, cut the tickets, and draw pictures on them that were not too amateurish: a red cow, a green pig, or a few blue chickens, according to the animal featured in that week's lottery.</t5798>
<t5799>Then, with great care, he would put the numbers on the tickets with handwriting that he tried to make resemble printing press letters, and he would write "Divine Providence Lottery," which was Petra Cotes' invention.</t5799> <t5800>But as he began to get very tired over time, he had rubber stamps made with pictures of animals, the ticket numbers, and the name of the lottery on them.</t5800> <t5801>Now, his only task was to press the stamps, in various colors, onto the papers.</t5801> <t5802>In the last years of his life, he thought of putting puzzles on the tickets instead of numbering them.</t5802>
<t5803>Thus, the gift could be shared among those who correctly solved the puzzle.</t5803>
<t5804>However, because this system was both very complex and aroused suspicion, Aureliano Segundo abandoned this endeavor after the second attempt.</t5804>
<t5805>Aureliano Segundo was working tirelessly to maintain the reputation and popularity of his lottery, so much so that he didn't even have time to see his children.</t5805> <t5806>Fernanda enrolled Amaranta Ursula in a private school that only accepted six girl students.</t5806> <t5807>However, she did not send Aureliano to a public school.</t5807> <t5808>She thought they had already gone too far by allowing him to leave the room.</t5808> <t5809>Moreover, at that time, schools only accepted children born from legal Catholic marriages.</t5809> <t5810>However, when they brought Aureliano home, the identification card attached to him indicated that he was a foundling.</t5810> <t5811>Thus, the child began to grow up in the narrow world of the house, between the affectionate eyes of Santa Sofia de la Piedad and the senility of Ursula, learning from his grandmothers.</t5811> <t5812>He was a thin, delicate child.</t5812> <t5813>He would ask questions with a curiosity that drove adults crazy: Unlike the colonel, who was a keen observer and could make predictions with intuition at his age, Aureliano was a mischievous child with somewhat surprising habits.</t5813>
<t5814>When Amaranta Ursula went to school, Aureliano would play in the garden, hunt worms, and torture insects in a thousand different ways.</t5814> <t5815>Once, Fernanda caught him collecting scorpions to put in Ursula's bed.</t5815> <t5816>She immediately locked him up in Meme's old room.</t5816>
<t5817>Aureliano began to spend his lonely hours at home looking at the pictures in the encyclopedia.</t5817> <t5818>One day, while Ursula was walking around the house sprinkling a bunch of nettles with distilled water, she found Aureliano in the room and, despite having been together many times, she still asked who he was.</t5818>
<t5819>The child said, "I am Aureliano Buendia."</t5819>
<t5820>Ursula replied, "Yes, you are."</t5820> <t5821>"It's time to learn silversmithing."</t5821>
<t5822>She was confusing him with her own son again.</t5822> <t5823>Because the warm wind that blew after the flood and occasionally illuminated Ursula's mind had ceased.</t5823> <t5824>Ursula had now completely lost her consciousness.</t5824> <t5825>Whenever she entered the bedroom, she found Petronila Iguaran with her beaded jacket and hoop skirt that she wore when going out for a walk; her grandmother Tranquilina Marina Miniata Alacoque Buendia sitting in the rocking cripple chair, fanning herself with a peacock feather fan; her great-grandfather Aureliano Arcadio Buendia with his make-believe Dolaman robe from his sensitive officer days; her father Aureliano Iguaran, who had found a prayer that dried up wolves and made cows fall; her cousin with a pig's tail; Jose Arcadio Buendia and her deceased sons sitting on chairs lined up along the wall.</t5825> <t5826>All these relatives seemed to have come not to see her, but to keep vigil over a dead person.</t5826> <t5827>Ursula tried to bring up cheerful topics, talking to them by recounting events from very different times and various places.</t5827>
<t5828>When Amaranta Ursula returned from school and Aureliano grew tired of the encyclopedia, they would find Ursula sitting on her bed, lost in the maze of the dead, talking to herself.</t5828> <t5829>Once, in a fit of terror, she screamed -Fire! and chaos ensued.</t5829> <t5830>However, Ursula was talking about a barn fire she had seen when she was four years old.</t5830> <t5831>She mixed up the past and the present so much that, in the few moments of clarity before her death, no one could tell if she was recounting what she had heard or what she remembered.</t5831> <t5832>Ursula was slowly withering away, becoming like a child.</t5832>
<t5833>In her final months, she turned into a cherry pit lost in her nightgown.</t5833> <t5834>The arm she always kept in the air resembled a monkey's paw.</t5834> <t5835>She lay still for several days.</t5835> <t5836>Santa Sofia de la Piedad had to shake her to see if she was still alive, then sat her on her lap and poured a few spoonfuls of sugary water into her mouth.</t5836>
<t5837>Ursula looked like an old woman who had just been born.</t5837> <t5838>Amaranta, Ursula, and Aureliano would carry her around in their arms, lay her on the altar to measure her height, wondering if she was bigger than the statue of the child Jesus.</t5838> <t5839>One day, they put her in a cupboard in the pantry.</t5839> <t5840>The poor woman was almost eaten by the rats.</t5840> <t5841>On the Sunday before Easter, while Fernanda was at church, the children entered Ursula's bedroom, grabbed her by the neck and ankles, and carried her outside.</t5841>
<t5842>Amaranta Ursula, "Oh, my poor great-grandmother," she cried out.</t5842> <t5843>"She died of old age."</t5843>
<t5844>Ursula shuddered, "I am not dead, I am alive!" she said.</t5844>
<t5845>Amaranta Ursula, trying hard to hold back her laughter, said, "Look, look, he's not even breathing."</t5845>
<t5846>Ursula shouted, "Look, I'm talking!"</t5846>
<t5847>Aureliano Segundo exclaimed: "He can't even speak."</t5847> <t5848>"The poor thing died just like a grasshopper."</t5848>
<t5849>In the face of so much evidence, Ursula gave up resisting.</t5849> <t5850>Slowly, she said, "Oh my God."</t5850> <t5851>"So this is what it's like to die."</t5851> <t5852>Then she began an uninterrupted prayer that lasted more than two days.</t5852> <t5853>She got carried away and by Tuesday, the prayer was no longer a prayer.</t5853> <t5854>From time to time, she murmured her requests to God, sometimes offering practical solutions to prevent the red ants from destroying the house, asking not to extinguish the lamp illuminating Remedios' picture, babbling that the Buendias should not marry those of the same blood, otherwise their children would have pig tails.</t5854> <t5855>Aureliano Segundo took advantage of Ursula's babblings to learn the location of the gold, but once again came up empty-handed.</t5855> <t5856>Ursula said, "When the rightful owner of the gold appears, God will reveal the location of the treasure to him."</t5856>
<t5857>Santa Sofia de la Piedad had no doubt that Ursula could die at any moment, because in those days there was a disturbance in nature: Roses smelled like marigolds, peas were drying up and falling off, and the scattered bush beans were laid out in an extremely orderly starflower pattern.</t5857>
<t5858>As if these weren't enough, one night Santa Sofia de la Piedad also saw bright orange flying saucers passing through the sky.</t5858>
<t5859>On the Friday morning before Easter, they found Ursula dead.</t5859> <t5860>During the Banana Company era, when they last calculated Ursula's age, they concluded that she was between one hundred fifteen and one hundred twenty-two years old.</t5860> <t5861>They placed Ursula in a coffin not much larger than the basket in which Aureliano had been brought home.</t5861> <t5862>There were very few people at the funeral.</t5862>
<t5863>There were very few people left who remembered him, and the day of the funeral was so hot that birds were crashing into walls like targets thrown into the air during shooting practice, piercing through window screens and dying in bedrooms.</t5863>
<t5864>At first, everyone thought there was an epidemic.</t5864> <t5865>Housewives, especially during nap times, got tired of sweeping up dead birds and collecting them in dustpans, and men got tired of loading the dead birds into wheelbarrows and dumping them into the river.</t5865> <t5866>On Easter day, while giving a sermon, the hundred-year-old Father Antonio Isabel said that the death of the birds was caused by the evil spirit of the Wandering Jew, and that he had seen the Wandering Jew the night before.</t5866>
<t5867>He described the rogue Jew as a creature between a heretic woman and a goat, resembling a hellish beast that burns the air with its breath, and causing new brides to give birth to monsters with his gaze.</t5867> <t5868>All the townspeople did not take his words seriously because they believed the priest had become senile and was talking nonsense.</t5868>
<t5869>However, at dawn on Wednesday, a woman raised the town to its feet with her screams.</t5869> <t5870>She claimed to have seen the footprints of a cloven-hoofed and bipedal creature.</t5870> <t5871>The tracks were so distinct that those who saw them believed in the existence of the terrifying creature described by the priest and began working together to set traps in their gardens.</t5871> <t5872>And that’s how they caught it.</t5872> <t5873>Two weeks after Ursula's death, Petra Cotes and Aureliano Segundo were startled awake by the bellowing of a cow coming from nearby.</t5873> <t5874>When they went to the source of the sound, they found a few people prodding the trapped creature, which was covered with dry leaves, out with sharp sticks.</t5874>
<t5875>The monster, though as big as a young bull, was as heavy as an ox.</t5875> <t5876>A greasy, green liquid was oozing from its wounds.</t5876> <t5877>Its body was covered with coarse hair and clusters of ticks here and there.</t5877> <t5878>Its skin had thickened with an armor resembling fish scales.</t5878>
<t5879>Contrary to what the priest said, his body parts that resembled a human were more akin to a delicate angel than a man.</t5879> <t5880>His hands were trembling and small.</t5880>
<t5881>His eyes were large and sorrowful.</t5881> <t5882>He had strong wing bases at his shoulders, which had fallen victim to a woodcutter's axe.</t5882> <t5883>To make sure everyone could see, they tied the monster by its ankles and hung it from one of the almond trees in the field: When it started to decay, unable to decide whether to throw it into the river like an animal carcass or bury it in the ground like a human corpse, they set a huge bundle of brushwood on fire and burned the monster.</t5883>
<t5884>Although it was never really understood whether he was truly the cause of the birds' deaths, neither did new brides give birth to monsters, nor did the temperature rise.</t5884>
<t5885>Rebeca died at the end of that year.</t5885> <t5886>Argenida, the maid who had never left her side throughout her life, reported to the authorities that her mistress had not come out of her room for three days, and when they broke the door, they found Rebeca curled up in her bed.</t5886> <t5887>She had gone bald from ringworm.</t5887>
<t5888>His finger was also in his mouth.</t5888> <t5889>Aureliano Segunda took it upon himself to handle the funeral.</t5889> <t5890>Then he wanted to repair the house to sell it.</t5890> <t5891>However, the house had become so dilapidated that the whitewash on the walls would immediately blister, and the mortar was not enough to stop the weeds that cracked the floors and the poisonous ivy that rotted the beams.</t5891>
<t5892>So this is how things went after the flood.</t5892> <t5893>As forgetfulness weighed heavily with a speed completely opposite to the sluggishness of people, memories were being forgotten.</t5893> <t5894>In the end, forgetfulness reached such an extreme point that on the umpteenth anniversary of the Neerlandia Agreement, the representatives of the President, who came to give the medal that Colonel Aureliano Buendia had refused countless times, knocked on every door to find a descendant of the colonel.</t5894> <t5895>Although Aureliano Segundo, thinking the medal was pure gold, was eager to take it immediately, Petra Cotes convinced him that it would not be appropriate to jump at the medal as the envoys wanted to hold a ceremony and had prepared declarations and speeches.</t5895> <t5896>Around that time, the gypsies, the last heirs of Melquiades' science, came back again.</t5896> <t5897>Seeing the town so dilapidated and the townspeople so disconnected from the world, just like during their first visit, they went door to door showing magnetic ingots, claiming them to be the latest discovery of the Babylonian sages.</t5897>
<t5898>They reflected the sun's rays onto a single point with large magnifying glasses.</t5898> <t5899>And once again, the crowd that gathered around, watching with open mouths as the teapots somersaulted and the dishes leaped, and pressing fifty cents to look in astonishment at the false teeth a gypsy woman put in and took out of her mouth, was not missing this time either.</t5899>
<t5900>All that was left of the train to which Mister Brown had attached his private carriage, filled with throne-like chairs and a glass ceiling, and the one hundred and twenty-car banana train that took an entire afternoon to pass, was a shabby yellow train.</t5900> <t5901>This train no longer brought anyone to Macondo, nor did it take anyone away from Macondo.</t5901> <t5902>It stopped at the deserted station once in forty years.</t5902> <t5903>The religious authorities who came to investigate the mysterious death of the birds and the report on the sacrifice of the Wandering Jew found Father Antonio Isabel playing blind man's buff with the children and, concluding that the report he had written was fabricated, took him to the asylum.</t5903> <t5904>Very soon, they sent Father Augusto Angel in his place.</t5904> <t5905>Father Augusto Angel was a young, dynamic, and proactive man.</t5905> <t5906>To keep the townspeople's spirits from becoming dull, he rang the bells himself several times a day and went door to door to call the people to church.</t5906> <t5907>However, in less than a year, the priest too succumbed to the indifference that came with the air, the scorching dust that wore everything down, and the lethargy brought on by the unbearable heat during siesta hours and the meatballs eaten during that time.</t5907>
<t5908>With Ursula's death, the house once again fell into disrepair.</t5908>
<t5909>It turned into a ruin that not even someone as lively and determined as Amaranta Ursula could save.</t5909> <t5910>Years later, when Amaranta Ursula grew up to be a grounded, unprejudiced, happy, and modern woman, she opened the doors and windows to clean up this wreckage, rearranged the garden, eradicated the red ants that had begun to roam the veranda in broad daylight, and vainly tried to revive the long-forgotten atmosphere of hospitality.</t5910>
<t5911>Fernanda's passion, buried deep in her heart as if she were locked away in a monastery, became an insurmountable obstacle against Ursula's flowing century.</t5911> <t5912>After the dry and angry wind had passed, Fernanda not only kept the doors shut but also closed the windows, following the ritual of being buried alive, and nailed them shut with crosswise boards.</t5912>
<t5913>Correspondence with the invisible doctors yielded no results.</t5913> <t5914>After the surgery date was postponed for the umpteenth time, he withdrew to his room at the agreed-upon day and time.</t5914> <t5915>He covered himself with only a white sheet and lay down with his head turned north.</t5915> <t5916>Around one in the morning, he felt a cloth soaked in icy liquid being placed on his head.</t5916>
<t5917>When he woke up, the sun was shining, and there was a rough stitch in the shape of an arc extending from between his legs to his breastbone.</t5917> <t5918>However, before completing the predetermined bed rest period, he received a concerned letter from the invisible doctors.</t5918> <t5919>The doctors wrote that despite examining him for six long hours, they could not see any of the symptoms of the illness he persistently mentioned.</t5919>
<t5920>Fernanda's habit of not being able to describe events accurately had created this confusion.</t5920> <t5921>Doctors who operated through telepathy found nothing in Fernanda except a minor uterine prolapse that could be resolved with a pessary placed on the cervix.</t5921> <t5922>Although Fernanda, who did not find what she hoped for, wanted to obtain more detailed and precise information, the doctors no longer responded to her letters.</t5922> <t5923>Fernanda began to writhe under the weight of a word she did not know.</t5923> <t5924>In the end, she couldn't bear it anymore, put aside her embarrassment, and decided to ask what a pessary meant.</t5924> <t5925>It was only then that Fernanda learned that the French doctor had hanged himself with the rope he had dangled from the ceiling three months earlier and that, despite the townspeople's opposition, he had been buried by one of Colonel Aureliano Buendia's comrades.</t5925> <t5926>She then confided her problem to her son, Jose Arcadio.</t5926> <t5927>He sent his mother the rubber rings called pessaries and the instructions on how to use them from Rome.</t5927> <t5928>After Fernanda memorized how to use the rings, she threw the paper into the toilet because she didn't want anyone in the house to know what her problem was.</t5928> <t5929>In fact, such a precaution was unnecessary; the household was already not interested in her.</t5929>
<t5930>Santa Sofia de la Piedad, despite her old age, was bustling around, cooking a few bites of food, and dedicating herself entirely to the care of Jose Arcadio Segundo.</t5930> <t5931>Amaranta Ursula, who had a charm somewhat reminiscent of Beautiful Remedios, devoted the time she used to spend tormenting Ursula to her studies.</t5931>
<t5932>His mind was working well.</t5932> <t5933>He had developed a keen interest in learning.</t5933> <t5934>This attitude of his rekindled the great hopes that Aureliano Segundo once had for Meme.</t5934>
<t5935>Following a custom that became fashionable and established during the Muz Company period, he promised to send his daughter to Brussels to complete her education.</t5935> <t5936>This dream led him to revive the lands devastated by floods.</t5936> <t5937>Aureliano Segundo's rare visits home, once every forty years, were solely to see Amaranta Ursula.</t5937> <t5938>Because over time, he had become completely estranged from Fernanda.</t5938> <t5939>Meanwhile, young Aureliano became more introverted as he reached adolescence.</t5939> <t5940>Aureliano Segundo hoped that as Fernanda aged, she would soften and not object to the child integrating into the town's life.</t5940>
<t5941>None of the townspeople would bother to investigate the child's true identity anyway.</t5941> <t5942>However, it seemed that Aureliano also preferred solitude, showing no curiosity or interest in the world that began at the front door of the house.</t5942>
<t5943>After Ursula had the room of Melquiades opened, Aureliano began to wander around the room.</t5943> <t5944>He was peeking inside through the slightly open door.</t5944>
<t5945>Nobody understood when the mutual closeness between Jose Arcadio Segundo and them began.</t5945> <t5946>Aureliano Segundo only learned about their friendship much later, when the child spoke of the massacre at the station.</t5946> <t5947>One day at the dinner table, when someone complained that the town had not recovered since the Banana Company left, the child mentioned the massacre at the station and opposed with the mature views of an adult.</t5947> <t5948>Contrary to the general opinion, Aureliano argued that Macondo was a prosperous and abundant place before the Banana Company came, corrupted the town, disrupted its order, and imposed its dominance.</t5948> <t5949>He also claimed that the engineers of the company caused the flood to avoid fulfilling the promises made to the workers.</t5949> <t5950>He spoke so logically and beautifully that Fernanda, hearing him speak like that in the presence of intelligent people, compared him to false prophets trying to disrespect religion.</t5950>
<t5951>The child described in detail how the soldiers mowed down more than three thousand workers trapped in front of the station with machine guns, and how they loaded the corpses onto a two hundred-car train and dumped them into the sea.</t5951> <t5952>Like most people who believed the official statements and thought nothing had happened, Fernanda, upon hearing this, told the child to be quiet, fearing that he had adopted Colonel Aureliano Buendia's anarchist views.</t5952> <t5953>However, Aureliano Segundo recognized his brother's views in the child's account.</t5953> <t5954>Despite everyone thinking he was crazy at the time, Jose Arcadio Segundo was actually the most sensible person in the house.</t5954>
<t5955>He taught young Aureliano to read and write, then started having him decipher the manuscripts.</t5955> <t5956>At the same time, he explained what the Banana Company meant for Macondo and what the company did, according to his personal interpretations.</t5956> <t5957>When Aureliano, who grew up learning these things, joined the world years later, no one believed what he said.</t5957> <t5958>Because what he recounted was completely contrary to the fabrications that historians concocted and wrote in textbooks.</t5958> <t5959>In the room where the dry heat, dust, and wind did not enter, both Jose Arcadio Segundo and Aureliano reached the insights of an old man whose characteristics had been passed down through generations, an old man sitting by the window with a crow-wing hat.</t5959> <t5960>That man had determined his worldview long before they were born.</t5960>
<t5961>The uncle and the nephew would arrive at the same conclusions at the same time, always realizing simultaneously what it meant for it to always be March and always be Monday.</t5961>
<t5962>Then, they realized that, as it was said at home, Jose Arcadio Buendia was not crazy, and that time could occasionally stumble and sprain its ankle, leaving a slice of itself in a room that would remain forever unchanged as it got back up. Only Jose Arcadio Buendia had comprehended this truth.</t5962> <t5963>Jose Arcadio Segundo had also deciphered the letters of the manuscripts that resembled a code.</t5963> <t5964>He had deduced that this writing had an alphabet of forty-seven or fifty-three letters.</t5964> <t5965>When the letters were written individually, they looked like scribbles.</t5965> <t5966>Especially with Melquiades' fine handwriting, they resembled clothes hanging on a line.</t5966>
<t5967>Aureliano remembered seeing pictures of similar letters in an English encyclopedia and brought it to the room to compare it with the code that Jose Arcadio Segundo had deciphered.</t5967> <t5968>Indeed, the two alphabets matched.</t5968>
<t5969>Aureliano Segundo, during the time he was organizing the lottery puzzle, started waking up with a knot in his throat.</t5969> <t5970>It was as if he wanted to cry but couldn't.</t5970> <t5971>Petra Cotes attributed this discomfort to the nervous breakdown caused by bad days.</t5971> <t5972>For over a year, every morning she would smear honey on the roof of Aureliano Segundo's mouth with the tip of a spoon and make him drink radish juice.</t5972> <t5973>When the condition in his throat made it difficult for him to breathe, Aureliano Segundo ran to Pilar Ternera and asked if she knew of an old folk remedy to cure him.</t5973> <t5974>His audacious grandmother, who had reached the age of a hundred by secretly running a small brothel, didn't believe in folk remedies and turned to the cards.</t5974> <t5975>Seeing that the leg of the spade had injured the queen of hearts by the neck, she said that Fernanda had cast a spell to bring her husband back home, resorting to an outdated method like sticking needles into her husband's picture, but since she couldn't manage the spell, it had caused a tumor in his throat.</t5975> <t5976>Aureliano Segundo had no other pictures except those taken at the wedding, and they were in the family album.</t5976>
<t5977>Aureliano Segundo, without his wife noticing, started searching the house and found half a dozen pesos at the bottom of one of the drawers, still in their original box.</t5977> <t5978>Thinking the small, red rubber rings were related to magic, Aureliano Segundo put them in his pocket to show Pilar Ternera.</t5978> <t5979>Pilar Ternera couldn't understand what the rings were, but since she didn't trust them at all, she decided there was something fishy about them and lit a fire in the garden to burn the rings.</t5979> <t5980>To break Fernanda's spell, she told Aureliano Segundo to wet a broody hen and bury it alive under the chestnut tree.</t5980> <t5981>Aureliano Segundo had so much faith in her words that he felt his breathing ease even before he could cover the mound where he buried the hen with more dry leaves.</t5981> <t5982>Fernanda, on the other hand, interpreted the disappearance of the rings as a retaliation by the invisible doctors and placed the new rings sent by her son in a hidden pocket she sewed into her blouse.</t5982>
<t5983>Six months after burying the chicken, Aureliano Segundo woke up in the middle of the night with a terrible coughing fit.</t5983> <t5984>It felt as if a crab had wrapped around his throat and was squeezing it.</t5984> <t5985>At that moment, despite all the magical rings he had burned and the chickens he had buried, he realized there was only one truth and that he was about to die.</t5985> <t5986>He didn't tell anyone anything.</t5986> <t5987>Fearing he would die before sending Amaranta Ursula to Brussels, he threw himself into an unprecedented amount of work and started holding the lottery three times a week instead of once.</t5987> <t5988>He would go out early in the morning, touring the whole town to sell tickets with the urgency and excitement befitting someone nearing death, reaching even the most remote neighborhoods.</t5988> <t5989>"Divine Providence Lottery," he would shout.</t5989> <t5990>"Don't miss the chance, such an opportunity comes only once in a hundred years."</t5990>
<t5991>She was trying to appear cheerful, to chatter, and to show delicacy.</t5991>
<t5992>But it was enough to see the pallor of his face and the beads of sweat to understand that he wasn't doing these things wholeheartedly.</t5992> <t5993>Sometimes he would retreat to a secluded corner or collapse where he stood just to catch his breath, escaping the claws tearing at his insides.</t5993> <t5994>Even at midnight, he would wander around, go to the red-light district, and try to comfort the lonely women crying over gramophones by inviting them to try their luck.</t5994> <t5995>He would take out the tickets and say, -Look, this number hasn't won the lottery for four months.</t5995> <t5996>-Don't miss this chance; life isn't as long as you think.</t5996>
<t5997>In the end, he lost his respectability.</t5997> <t5998>They started to mock him.</t5998> <t5999>Especially in his final months, they stopped calling him Don Aureliano as they used to and started calling him 'Mr. Divine Providence' to his face.</t5999> <t6000>Aureliano Segundo's voice became increasingly hoarse and off-key.</t6000> <t6001>In the end, it turned into a dog's growl.</t6001> <t6002>Nevertheless, it did not extinguish the hope brought by those gathered in Petra Cotes' garden.</t6002> <t6003>When he lost his voice and foresaw that the pain would soon become unbearable, he realized that the lottery of pigs and goats would not be enough to send his daughter to Brussels.</t6003> <t6004>Thereupon, thinking of putting his land, which someone with money could easily restore to its former state, into a lottery, he organized a legendary lottery.</t6004> <t6005>This was such a comprehensive initiative that even the mayor did not hesitate to help by announcing the lottery with an official proclamation.</t6005> <t6006>He formed associations to buy the tickets, each costing a hundred pesos, and within a week, the tickets were sold out.</t6006> <t6007>On the night the lottery was drawn, the winners organized a grand celebration.</t6007> <t6008>It was a fanfare comparable only to the festivities during the Banana Company era.</t6008> <t6009>And Aureliano Segundo played the long-forgotten songs of Francisco the Devil-Teaser on the accordion for the last time, but this time he couldn't sing the songs.</t6009>
<t6010>Two months later, Amaranta Ursula went to Brussels.</t6010> <t6011>Aureliano Segundo not only gave her the money collected from the big lottery, but also added the money he had been able to set aside in previous months and the money he gathered by selling the piano, accordion, and a heap of old knick-knacks.</t6011> <t6012>According to his calculations, this money would be enough for his daughter's education.</t6012>
<t6013>Only the fare for the return trip was missing.</t6013> <t6014>Fernanda, who went mad at the thought of Brussels being too close to Paris and Paris's scandals, opposed her daughter's departure until the last moment.</t6014> <t6015>But when Father Angel wrote a letter to a dormitory run by nuns and established for Catholic young girls, and when Amaranta Ursula promised to stay there until she completed her education, Fernanda calmed down.</t6015> <t6016>Moreover, the priest entrusted the girl to a group of Franciscan nuns going to Toledo and got their promise to find someone reliable going to Belgium and to hand over Amaranta Ursula to them.</t6016>
<t6017>While the correspondence for organizing these matters continued, Aureliano Segundo, with the help of Petra Cotes, was preparing Amaranta Ursula's belongings.</t6017> <t6018>The night they placed the clothes in one of Fernanda's dowry chests, everything was so well determined that Amaranta Ursula already knew by heart that she would wear her suits, fabric slippers while crossing the Atlantic, and the blue coat with copper buttons and fine leather shoes when she disembarked from the ship.</t6018> <t6019>She had also memorized how she should walk to avoid falling into the sea when disembarking, that she would never leave the nuns' side, that she would not step out of her cabin except during meal times, and that she would not answer any questions from anyone on the ship, whether man or woman.</t6019>
<t6020>Next to Amaranta Ursula, there was a medicine to be dropped into water to prevent seasickness, and a notebook containing six prayers written by Father Angel himself to be read in case of a storm.</t6020> <t6021>Fernanda sewed her a belt made of canvas to keep her money in and advised her not to take it off even while sleeping.</t6021> <t6022>Although she wanted to give her a chamber pot washed with lye water and disinfected with alcohol, Amaranta Ursula did not take it because she feared her classmates would make fun of her.</t6022>
<t6023>A few months later, as Aureliano Segundo was taking his last breath, he would remember his daughter as he last saw her, struggling to lower the window of the second-class compartment to hear what advice her mother was giving again.</t6023>
<t6024>Amaranta Ursula was wearing a silk dress of pink color.</t6024> <t6025>She had pinned a bunch of artificial violets to her left shoulder.</t6025> <t6026>On her feet were low-heeled leather shoes with large buckles, and she wore silk stockings held up by garters.</t6026> <t6027>She was very slender.</t6027> <t6028>She had left her long hair loose.</t6028>
<t6029>Her lively eyes resembled Ursula's appearance at that age, and the fact that she neither cried nor smiled when leaving also proved that she was as strong as Ursula.</t6029>
<t6030>While walking alongside the accelerating carriage and holding Fernanda's arm to prevent her from falling, Aureliano Segundo could only manage to wave back to his daughter who was blowing kisses with her hand.</t6030>
<t6031>The husband and wife stood motionless under the scorching sun, watching the train until it became a black line on the horizon.</t6031> <t6032>For the first time since their wedding, they had linked arms.</t6032>
<t6033>On the ninth of August, before any letter had arrived from Brussels, while Jose Arcadio Segundo was talking with Aureliano in Melquiades' room, he said, without even realizing what he was saying, "Never forget that there were more than three thousand of them and that they were all thrown into the sea."</t6033>
<t6034>Then he fell on his back onto the manuscripts and died with his eyes open.</t6034> <t6035>At the same time, in Fernanda's bed, his twin brother reached the end of his long struggle with the steel pincers that had been gnawing at his throat.</t6035>
<t6036>A week ago, with his voice completely gone, struggling to breathe and reduced to skin and bones, he had begun to fulfill the promise he made to die beside his wife, gathering his trunks and his wandering accordion, dizzy from going back and forth between two houses.</t6036> <t6037>Petra Cotes helped him gather his trunks and saw him off without shedding a single tear.</t6037> <t6038>However, she had forgotten to give him the shoes he had requested to be buried in.</t6038> <t6039>Therefore, when she heard that Aureliano Segundo had died, Petra Cotes dressed in mourning, wrapped the shoes in a newspaper, and asked Fernanda for permission to see the deceased.</t6039> <t6040>Fernanda did not let her step inside the threshold.</t6040>
<t6041>Petra Cotes pleaded, "Put yourself in my place."</t6041>
<t6042>Think about how much I must have loved him to accept such humiliation, he said.</t6042>
<t6043>Fernanda replied, "Metresler deserves all kinds of humiliation."</t6043> <t6044>"Wait, and when another friend dies, you can put the shoes on them."</t6044>
<t6045>Santa Sofia de la Piedad, in order to keep her promise to her son and ensure he wasn't buried alive, slit Jose Arcadio Segundo's throat with a kitchen knife.</t6045> <t6046>The bodies were placed in identical coffins.</t6046> <t6047>When they died, the two brothers looked so much alike that they were indistinguishable, just like in their childhood.</t6047>
<t6048>Aureliano Segundo's companions from his days of revelry placed a wreath with a purple ribbon on the coffin, which read, "Hold on cows, life passes by quickly."</t6048> <t6049>Fernanda, driven mad by this insolence, had the wreath thrown away.</t6049> <t6050>In the chaos of the final moment, the grieving drunkards who were taking the coffins out of the house mixed them up and buried them in the wrong graves.</t6050>
<t6051>Aureliano did not leave Melquiades' room for a long time.</t6051>
<t6052>He memorized the tales from the book that had become thoroughly worn and tattered, the results of Topal Hermann's research, the notes on the science of jinn and demons, the mysteries of the philosopher's stone, Nostradamus's prophecies, and the research on the plague.</t6052> <t6053>Thus, by the time he reached adolescence, he knew nothing about his own era but had learned the basic knowledge of medieval people.</t6053> <t6054>Whenever Santa Sofia de la Piedad entered his room, she found the child engrossed in reading.</t6054> <t6055>At sunrise, she would bring him a cup of plain coffee, and at noon, a plate of rice with fried plantains.</t6055> <t6056>Since the death of Aureliano Segundo, nothing else was eaten in the house.</t6056> <t6057>Santa Sofia de la Piedad cut Aureliano's hair, picked out his lice, and altered the clothes she found in the trunks to fit him.</t6057>
<t6058>When the child's mustache began to sweat, she brought him Colonel Aureliano Buendia's razor and the gourd he used as a shaving bowl.</t6058> <t6059>None of the Colonel's children, not even Aureliano Jose, resembled the Colonel as much as Aureliano did.</t6059> <t6060>His prominent cheekbones and full lips, which gave his face a merciless expression, were just like the Colonel's.</t6060> <t6061>Just as Ursula had once been surprised to see Aureliano Segundo working in that room, now Santa Sofia de la Piedad was surprised, thinking Aureliano was talking to himself.</t6061> <t6062>But the child was talking to Melquiades.</t6062>
<t6063>Shortly after the twins' death, during a scorching midday, Aureliano saw the old man with the crow-wing hat in front of the window.</t6063>
<t6064>It was like the materialization and animation of a memory etched into his mind before he was born.</t6064> <t6065>Aureliano had deciphered the alphabet of the manuscripts.</t6065> <t6066>When Melquiades asked him if he knew what language the writings were in, Aureliano replied without hesitation, Sanskrit.</t6066>
<t6067>Melquiades explained that the possibility of returning to the room was limited.</t6067> <t6068>But he said that he could rest comfortably in the lands of eternal death, without any worries.</t6068> <t6069>Because since Aureliano had learned the alphabet, by the time the manuscripts would become readable, that is, a hundred years after they were written, he would have learned enough Sanskrit to read them.</t6069> <t6070>Melquiades told Aureliano that there was now a Catalonian's bookstore in the narrow street leading to the river, where dream interpretations were made during the Banana Company era, and that there was a Sanskrit reading book in that store, which would be devoured by moths within six years if he did not hurry.</t6070> <t6071>When Aureliano described the store to Santa Sofia de la Piedad and asked her to bring him the book standing on the right edge of the second shelf, between Milton's poems and Jerusalem Delivered, Santa Sofia de la Piedad revealed an emotion for the first time in her life: She was surprised.</t6071> <t6072>Since she was illiterate, she memorized what Aureliano said, and after the night the soldiers searched the house, she sold one of the seventeen gold fish, whose location was known only to her and Aureliano, to get the money needed for the book.</t6072>
<t6073>Melquiades' visits became less frequent, and in the midday sun, he appeared increasingly distant and faint.</t6073> <t6074>Meanwhile, Aureliano was improving his Sanskrit.</t6074> <t6075>During Melquiades' last visit, Aureliano did not see him, but he heard him whispering in the room, "Death from fever on the scorching sands of Singapore."</t6075> <t6076>From that day on, he lost his immunity to dust, heat, worms, red ants, and the moths that would turn the wisdom in the manuscripts into sawdust.</t6076>
<t6077>There was no shortage of food at home.</t6077> <t6078>The day after Aureliano Segundo's death, one of the friends who had brought that disrespectful wreath came to Fernanda and said he wanted to settle his debt to her husband.</t6078>
<t6079>After that day, every Wednesday, a child started bringing a basket full of food that would last a week.</t6079> <t6080>Nobody knew that Petra Cotes was sending this food and that she did so because she believed helping the person who had humiliated her would, in turn, humiliate them.</t6080> <t6081>Although Petra Cotes's anger towards Fernanda subsided faster than she had expected, Petra continued to send food initially to preserve her honor and later out of pity for them.</t6081>
<t6082>Sometimes there were no animals left to put in the lottery, and sometimes no one was interested in the lottery.</t6082> <t6083>At such times, just so Fernanda wouldn't go hungry, Petra Cotes would go without food for days.</t6083> <t6084>Petra continued this until the day she saw Fernanda's funeral procession.</t6084>
<t6085>For Santa Sofia de la Piedad, the reduction in the household crowd meant finding the opportunity for the rest she deserved after working like a slave for half a century.</t6085> <t6086>The woman, who had added the angelic seed of Beautiful Remedios and the mysterious stillness of Jose Arcadio Segundo to the family, had never uttered a single complaint.</t6086> <t6087>She had spent her entire life in seclusion, dedicating herself to raising children she was confused about whether they were her children or grandchildren.</t6087> <t6088>She had cared for little Aureliano as if he were her own child, embracing him without ever knowing she was his great-grandmother.</t6088> <t6089>Santa Sofia de la Piedad laying a bed in the pantry where mice ran rampant was something that could only be comprehended within the atmosphere of that house.</t6089>
<t6090>The poor woman had woken up in fear one night, sensing that someone was watching her in the dark, and had seen a huge, poisonous snake coiled on her stomach. She had not mentioned this incident to anyone.</t6090>
<t6091>He knew that if he told Ursula, she would take him into her embrace.</t6091>
<t6092>But they were going through such days that unless someone stepped out onto the veranda and shouted at the top of their lungs, no one knew anything about anyone else.</t6092>
<t6093>The frantic work in the oven, then the bewilderment of the war, then the care of the children, no one had found the opportunity to think about the happiness of others.</t6093> <t6094>The only person who did not forget him was Petra Cotes, whom he had never even seen.</t6094> <t6095>Petra Cotes made sure that Santa Sofia de la Piedad lacked nothing, always thinking of a new pair of shoes she could wear when going out, and clothes to put on her back.</t6095> <t6096>Even in the days when lotteries were run only through miraculous efforts, Petra did not forget her.</t6096> <t6097>When Fernanda came as a bride, she thought Santa Sofia de la Piedad was a servant of indeterminate age, and although she had heard several times that she was her husband's mother, she found it so unbelievable that she preferred to immediately forget that she was her mother-in-law.</t6097> <t6098>Santa Sofia de la Piedad, on the other hand, never minded being treated as a servant.</t6098> <t6099>On the contrary, she seemed to enjoy staying in the background: She worked tirelessly and without complaint to keep this house, where she had lived since her youth and which had especially turned into a barracks during the Banana Company period, tidy and clean.</t6099>
<t6100>When Ama Ursula died, the superhuman efforts and inexhaustible energy of Santa Sofia de la Piedad began to wane.</t6100> <t6101>The reason for this decline was not just because she had aged and grown tired.</t6101> <t6102>When Ursula died, the house collapsed overnight.</t6102> <t6103>A soft layer of moss appeared on the walls.</t6103>
<t6104>The weeds that covered the garden, having left no space as small as the eye of a needle, began to press against the cement of the veranda, breaking through the cement flooring like glass and sprouting shoots.</t6104> <t6105>In the split cracks, buds of yellow flowers identical to those Ursula had found in Melquiades' false teeth a century ago burst open.</t6105> <t6106>Santa Sofia de la Piedad, who had neither the time nor the means to stop nature's defiance, spent her entire day chasing away the lizards that had invaded the bedrooms.</t6106>
<t6107>As for the lizards, they would come back once it was night again.</t6107>
<t6108>Santa Sofia de la Piedad saw one morning that red ants were emerging from the foundations they had hollowed out, crossing the garden, climbing the veranda railings where the begonias had turned the color of earth, and then entering the house.</t6108> <t6109>She first tried to kill the ants with a broom.</t6109>
<t6110>Then she tried insecticides.</t6110> <t6111>When they didn't work either, she poured soapy water.</t6111> <t6112>But the next day, the ants came again, walking inside without any hindrance.</t6112> <t6113>Fernanda, who was engrossed in writing a letter to her children, was unaware of this unstoppable destructive invasion.</t6113> <t6114>Santa Sofia de la Piedad was struggling alone, trying to prevent the weeds from taking over the kitchen, cleaning the spider webs that would be woven again in a few hours, and digging out the nests of bedbugs.</t6114> <t6115>However, when she saw that Melquiades' room was also getting dusty, that she couldn't cope with the spider webs despite sweeping three times a day, and realized that the room had now taken on the same ruinous appearance that only Colonel Aureliano Buendia and the young officer had seen before, Santa Sofia de la Piedad understood that she had been defeated by nature.</t6115>
<t6116>Then he put on his bargaining clothes, Ursula's old shoes, the muslin socks given by Amaranta Ursula, and packed the remaining few layers of laundry.</t6116>
<t6117>Aureliano, "I have given up," he said.</t6117> <t6118>"This housework is not something my bones can handle."</t6118>
<t6119>Aureliano asked where she was going.</t6119> <t6120>The woman made an ambiguous gesture as if she didn't know where she was going.</t6120> <t6121>Still trying to offer an explanation, she said she would spend her final years with her niece in Riohacha.</t6121> <t6122>This was not a very plausible explanation, because after her parents died, Santa Sofia de la Piedad had neither interacted with anyone in town, received any letters, nor mentioned any relatives.</t6122> <t6123>When Santa Sofia de la Piedad attempted to set off with one peso and twenty-five cents in her pocket, Aureliano forcibly gave her seventeen gold fish.</t6123> <t6124>Then he watched from the room's window as the woman, with her laundry bundle on her shoulder, bent over with age, dragging her feet through the garden, opened the garden gate, and after passing through, bolted it again.</t6124>
<t6125>There has been no news from Santa Sofia de la Piedad since.</t6125>
<t6126>When Fernanda heard that Santa Sofia de la Piedad had run away, she spent the whole day searching the house to see if anything was stolen, emptying trunks and baskets, and rummaging through cupboards and drawers.</t6126>
<t6127>Then, when she tried to light the fire for the first time in her life, she burned her fingers and asked Aureliano to teach her how to make coffee.</t6127>
<t6128>Over time, Aureliano took over the kitchen duties.</t6128> <t6129>When Fernanda woke up as she used to, she found her breakfast ready and only left her room to get her meals.</t6129> <t6130>She would take the food that Aureliano had left on the embers to keep warm, spread the linen cloths, light the candlesticks, sit at the head of the table facing fifteen empty chairs, and eat her meal.</t6130>
<t6131>Even under these conditions, Aureliano and Fernanda did not share their loneliness.</t6131> <t6132>Each lived on their own, trying to clean their own rooms, and destroy the spider webs that fell like snow on the rose bushes, covered the ceiling beams, and plastered the walls.</t6132> <t6133>It was during this time that Fernanda got the feeling that the house was infested with spirits.</t6133> <t6134>The furniture in the house, especially the items used daily, had started to move on their own.</t6134>
<t6135>Fernanda was searching for the scissors she was certain she had left on the bed, and then found them on the kitchen shelf, a place she thought she hadn't visited in four days.</t6135> <t6136>She saw that there were no forks in the drawer where the silverware was kept, and found six of them on the altar and three in the bathroom.</t6136> <t6137>Especially when she sat down to write, the constant movement of objects became even more exasperating.</t6137> <t6138>The inkwell she had placed on the right side of the table had moved to the left, and Fernanda only found the missing blotting paper under her pillow two days later.</t6138>
<t6139>The pages of the letter he wrote to Jose Arcadio were getting mixed up with the letter he wrote to Amaranta Ursula.</t6139> <t6140>Fernanda always had the feeling that she put one person's letter in the other's envelope.</t6140>
<t6141>Indeed, it happened a few times.</t6141> <t6142>Once, she lost her fountain pen.</t6142> <t6143>Two weeks later, the postman, who found the fountain pen in his own bag, returned it.</t6143> <t6144>He had gone door to door looking for the owner of the pen.</t6144> <t6145>At first, Fernanda thought that these things were the work of invisible doctors, and that they had risen up like ghosts.</t6145> <t6146>She even started to write a letter to the doctors, begging them to leave her alone.</t6146> <t6147>But while writing the letter, she got up to do something, and when she returned to the room, she couldn't find the letter and had forgotten why she was writing it.</t6147> <t6148>For a while, she thought it was Aureliano who was doing these things.</t6148>
<t6149>He started to watch Aureliano.</t6149> <t6150>He left some things in the open to see if he would move them or not.</t6150> <t6151>But very soon, he definitely understood that Aureliano did not leave Melquiades' room except to go to the kitchen or the bathroom and that he was not someone who would play such games.</t6151> <t6152>In the end, he believed it was the work of spirits or fairies and decided to tie everything to its place of use.</t6152>
<t6153>She hung the scissors by a long string at the head of her bed.</t6153> <t6154>With the fountain pen, she tied the blotting paper to the leg of the table.</t6154> <t6155>She stuck the ink bottle to the right side of the table, where she always used it.</t6155> <t6156>However, the problems were not something that could be solved immediately.</t6156> <t6157>A few hours after Fernanda tied the scissors, she saw that the string was short and didn't reach.</t6157> <t6158>It was as if goblins had cut the string.</t6158> <t6159>The same thing happened to the string she tied to the fountain pen.</t6159> <t6160>It even seemed as if her own arm had shortened, because soon she couldn't reach the ink bottle.</t6160>

<t6163>It really was like that.</t6163> <t6164>Because he wasn't seeking a compromise in any matter, he wasn't making any concessions.</t6164> <t6165>It was as if life was dragging him back into the world of his own family, a realm where people didn't deal with daily problems but found solutions to these problems in their minds beforehand.</t6165>

<t6189>He was so convinced that he could get the permission he had been waiting for one of these days that one morning he cut his hair that fell on his shoulders, shaved his scraggly beard, put on a tight pair of pants whose origin he did not know, and a shirt with a detachable collar, and waited in the kitchen for Fernanda to come and get her breakfast.</t6189> <t6190>The woman who walked every day with her head held high and with firm steps did not appear that day.</t6190>

<t6257>Jose Arcadio fixed up Meme's room, having the silk canopy of the bed fit for kings cleaned and repaired, and the velvet curtains adjusted.</t6257> <t6258>He reopened the unused bathroom where the cement tub had become encrusted with lime.</t6258> <t6259>He got rid of his old exotic clothes, fake perfumes, and cheap jewelry, spending all his money solely on the repairs.</t6259> <t6260>The only thing that concerned him in the other parts of the house were the statues of saints lined up in the altar.</t6260> <t6261>He threw them into a fire he lit in the garden one day and burned them to ashes.</t6261> <t6262>He slept until eleven in the morning.</t6262> <t6263>Then, putting on an old robe embroidered with golden dragons and slippers with yellow pompoms, he would go to the bathroom and stay there long enough to remind one of the Beautiful Remedios, bathing with great care.</t6263>
<t6264>Before entering the bathroom, he would pour the scented powders he carried in three jars made of marble into the bathtub, which was the size of a pool.</t6264> <t6265>He didn't pour water with a gourd, but instead, he would dive into the scented waters of the pool-like bathtub, lie on his back for two hours, and enjoy the coolness while thinking about Amaranta.</t6265>
<t6266>A few days after her arrival, she took off the taffeta dress, her only garment, which was unsuitable for the town's heat.</t6266> <t6267>Instead, she started wearing a tight pair of pants very similar to those worn by Pietro Crespi during his dance lessons, and a silk shirt made from vibrant cocoon silk, with her initials embroidered over her heart.</t6267> <t6268>Twice a week, she would take off her clothes, press them into a basin and wash them, and since she had nothing else to wear, she would wait in her bathrobe until they dried.</t6268>
<t6269>He wasn't eating his meals at home.</t6269> <t6270>When the midday heat subsided, he would go out and return late at night.</t6270> <t6271>Then, like a cat, he would start prowling around the room, hissing and panting, and circling Amaranta.</t6271>
<t6272>The memory that remained from home was only the memory of Amaranta and the statues of saints with their terrifying appearance in the light of the night lamp.</t6272> <t6273>In the August heat of Rome that drove people to dreams, he had woken up countless times in the middle of his sleep, and in a glorification stemming from being far away, he had seemed to see Amaranta, with her lace petticoats and the bandage on her wrist, emerging from a marble-edged pool.</t6273> <t6274>Unlike Aureliano Jose, who tried to drown the image of Amaranta in the bloody mire of war, Jose Arcadio, while distracting his mother with tales of divine service, also tried to keep this image alive in the pit of lust.</t6274> <t6275>Neither he nor Fernanda knew that the letters they wrote to each other were filled with mutual lies.</t6275>
<t6276>As soon as he arrived in Rome, Jose Arcadio, who had abandoned the faculty of theology, continued the tale of theology and canon law to avoid jeopardizing the legendary inheritance that would rescue him from the poverty mentioned in his mother's letters, which he shared with two friends in an attic in Trastevere.</t6276> <t6277>Upon receiving Fernanda's last letter, written as she was dying, he packed whatever remained of his ostentatious luxury into a suitcase and crossed the ocean in the hold of a ship filled with immigrants crammed together like sheep in a slaughterhouse, eating cold pasta and worm-infested cheese.</t6277> <t6278>Even before reading Fernanda's will, which was nothing more than a pretentious account of her sufferings and misfortunes, Jose Arcadio realized, upon seeing the broken furniture and the weeds covering the veranda, how he had been swindled, how he had fallen into a trap from which he would never escape, and that he would never again see the time-forgetting air and the brilliant sunshine of a Roman spring.</t6278>
<t6279>During the sleepless nights caused by his asthma, he wandered around the dark house, filled with fears by Ursula's senile words, constantly pondering the nature of the misfortune that had befallen him.</t6279> <t6280>To prevent losing Jose Arcadio in the darkness, Ursula had designated a specific corner of the bedroom for him and had not allowed him to leave this corner, the only place where he could stay away from the dead that filled the house after sunset.</t6280>
<t6281>Ursula used to say, "If you do something bad, the saints will inform me."</t6281>
<t6282>Jose Arcadio's fear-filled nights were spent in that corner, where he would curl up on a stool, sweating and trembling with fear under the glassy gaze of the saints until bedtime.</t6282> <t6283>This was an unnecessary torture.</t6283>
<t6284>Because Jose Arcadio was already a child who was afraid of everything around him and ready to be frightened by everything he would encounter in life.</t6284>
<t6285>His head was filled with a heap of horrors.</t6285> <t6286>They had taught him to fear everything created by God's infinite goodness and twisted by the devil's malice: the women on the street who would suck his blood, the women at home who bore children with pig tails, the cockfights that brought death to men and left the survivors in lifelong agony, the weapons that could start twenty-year wars with a single touch, the unknown adventures that led to disappointment and madness.</t6286> <t6287>On the mornings after nights when the devil entered his nightmares, the daylight and Amaranta's caresses in the bath, the comfort of the powder sprinkled between his legs, would dispel the fear he had felt at night.</t6287>
<t6288>Even Ursula would become completely different under the bright sun in the garden.</t6288> <t6289>Because when she was in the garden, she wouldn't talk about terrible things; she would take Jose Arcadio in front of her and rub his teeth with coal dust so that when he grew up and smiled, he would have bright teeth worthy of the Pope. She would cut and file his nails so that pilgrims flocking to Rome from all corners of the world would admire the beauty of the Pope's hands that blessed them. She would comb his hair in a manner befitting the Pope and sprinkle cologne on him so that his body and clothes would smell like the Pope's.</t6289> <t6290>When Jose Arcadio was in Rome, he had gone to the garden of Castel Gandolfo, and the only thing that caught the Pope's attention, who was making the same speech in seven different languages from the balcony to the crowd of pilgrims gathered there, was indeed the whiteness of his hands that looked as if they had been soaked in ash water, the dazzling brightness of his summer clothes, and his breath that absolutely smelled of cologne.</t6290>
<t6291>After a year had passed since his return home, Jose Arcadio's only solace, who had sold the silver candlesticks and the famous chamber pot - which was discovered to be thinly gold-plated at the time of sale - and squandered the money, was to gather the children from the street and let them play in the garden of the house.</t6291> <t6292>When nap time came, he would appear with a bunch of children, make them jump rope in the garden, sing songs on the veranda, perform acrobatics on the furniture in the living room, and he himself would move from one group to another teaching the children manners and etiquette.</t6292> <t6293>He had given up tight pants and silk shirts; he wore an ordinary outfit bought from the Arabs' shop, but he maintained his distant dignity and papal aura.</t6293>

<t6352>On a warm dawn hour, they both jumped out of bed as the front door was pounded loudly.</t6352> <t6353>At the door stood a dark-skinned, elderly man with large green eyes that seemed to glow as if his face were phosphorescent, resembling a ghost.</t6353> <t6354>There was an ash cross mark on the man's forehead.</t6354> <t6355>His clothes were tattered.</t6355> <t6356>His shoes were torn.</t6356>

<t6381>With the first angels of December, swept along by the winds that open the way for sailors, Amaranta Ursula set out, dragging her husband by the silk cord tied around his neck, and arrived unexpectedly, wearing an ivory-colored dress, a pearl necklace that reached her knees, topaz and emerald rings, and her hair done up in a bun held by sparrow tail pins.</t6381> <t6382>The man she had married six months earlier was a middle-aged, thin, sailor-type Fleming.</t6382> <t6383>As soon as Amaranta Ursula opened the door to the living room, she realized that her absence had lasted longer than she had thought and that this period had been more exhausting than she had imagined.</t6383>
